UNDER THE GUMTREE
Newer French Poetry.
VII. —HENRI DE REGNIER
([?] 1864).
A FAVOURITE game with
the Parisian journalists
was to pick up a sym-
bolist booklet and to
exclaim at its slimness, “As-
suredly these- gentlemen will
never bury us under the weight
of their work ! ” carefully for-
getting that with Such a booklet
every year or two the weight
of work is soon there. . Here,
is Henri de Regnier at 34 with
14 years work behind him, three
compact volumes of collected
verse, two volumes of prose
tales, his first novel in the press,
and confessing to the influence
of Mallarme, so “sterilizing,”
after the legend. Add to this the gifts of
fortune, aristocratic birth and appearance, a
.beautiful aud talented wife, the daughter of
Heredia —and the rewards of honest work, in-
creasing recognition, access to all reviews, even
that of Two Worlds, and in near prospect,
to the Academy—and you will understand why
the youngest of the young are clamouring to de-
vour those who, after all, are themselves yet
too young to have committed the crime of being
their parents. But why should they call the
generation of 1886 “a generation of failures ”?
There is a picture by the otherwise indifferent
Hans Thom a. Twilight in the Beeches : on the edge
of a beechen wood, while shadows fall fast, a faun
flutes with face turned towards the secrets of the
forest; outside in the last'light a harnessed knight
rides by. Imagine him returning from futile
quests, having learned that all quest of glory is
vain, to give himself up to regretful musing
jn the home he forsook with hope, and
you will have a perfect symbol of de Reg-
nier’s poetry. It springs directly out of the
Renascence fusion of classic and seignorial
imagery which set Tritons in the waters of the
formal gardens of the 16th century : it is, perhaps,
above all an expression of aristocratic decay, and
not the least characteristic note in it is the re-
habilitation of Versailles : not the Versailles that
Hugo damned in 1828, but the Versailles that
lives on, a forlorn ghost of departed glory.
The first volume of his verse need not detain us.
Personal poetry, exercises in the symbolist habit
of embodying a mood in a landscape, exercises in
his own individual theme of heraldic and chivalric
imagery, all in rather rigid Parnassian verse
whatever is distinctive in these recurs, more skil-
fully handled, in the second volume, containing
the Poems of antique and romantic legend of 181 >O,
and that finest of all books written according to
the symbolist formula, th is Tel qu'en.songe (As in
dream) of 1892. These two are closely con-
nected, representing two aspects of a single mood,
a mood that dominates likewise all his first
volume of prose.
In the earlier of these two de Regnier first
breaks with the stiff Parnassian form, without
achieving real suppleness either in the vers libre
or in the alexandrine, which latter is here too
patently imitated from Mallarme. The hook is
informed with a mood of disappointment. Life
has failed to satisfy the desire of heroic grandeur.
As ashes in the wind
Doom of disaster and sterility
is shed over all the-fabulous land evoked before
us : from the arid shore where the Ariadnes mourn
for ever, to the castle where the Beauty shall never
be awakened, to the place of ruins that recalls only
regretful memories of a former life and some
Helen then loved, to the city where the despairing
pilgrims pass by the closed windows. Only the
enchanted forest is left where the foiled knight
finds the magic gem of illusion.
The worst of life is, not that it fails to fulfil th©
heroic dream, but that it tarnishes the early bright-
ness of the soul that was as the clear blue steel.
What remains? To disdain it, and in silence, and
secrecy, to seek again, by meditation, that ancient
candour. This is the mood of Tel qu ’ en songe—
the return into the self, with a lingering look of
regret on the life that was once so shining with
hope. The theme is treated mythically in four
long poems : 'The Bird, where a knight rides, his
casque burdened with an obscene fowl, into the
mysterious forest, there to perish in unknown
fight, and at his death there soars from his helmet,
into the sunset sky,
A blazing bird of azure, gems, and gold :
The Guardian of the Hearth, where the warrior
returns to her whom he had left, in the home of
yore ; and two entitled The, Home and The Thres-
hold, of more meditative form. Between these are
set four groups of lyrics wherein, over the twilit
waters of the garden, Someone dreams (so the sug-
gestive titles run) of dawn and shadow, of twilight
and of hope, of hours and years, oi shadow and
oblivion. Here is the third of the first series ; an
exquisite little piece in which a mood is delicately
hinted in a fragment of decor:
A rose smiles wan, expiritvj languidly
Above the blood-red rim of porphyry
And under shows more pale
In the dead water glass'd, only to fail,
Still smiling, to a pallid wistfulness
That dreams itself to death
Where the wave reddens from the porphyry
Around a Triton’s sun-gilt ■nakedness
Lau hiny, whose conch writhes to his urgent breath.
The evening steals from tree to tree with steps of
shade,
The crossing noises gleet and fail,
The,first haf fads, the last, bird Hies away ;
The rose is shed upon the wave,
Tin gulden Triton, shrinks to a rugged block of
bronze,
A th rra-lehi.no shade,
That silent grins into his concn,
The rose is shed upon the wave
Where only a petal-drift recalls its vanish’d face ;
The evening steals from tree to tree with steps of
shade. .
A rose was smiling on its wave-glass’d face.
With Tel qu’en songe de Regnier has beoome
master of his style : lie has created his own variety
of the vers Libre, and his own variation on the
alexandrine, fluid, dreamy, and suggestive. His
third volume shows greater skill, if the matter is
no longer so authentic ; and presents the finest
single poem written in the symbolist manner, The
Man and the Siren. This drama of the black-robed
thinker meeting the wayward daughter of the
waves, and, in the attempt to create in her a soul
after his own thought, finding only death, is true
symbolism, in that it is no rigid allegory but a
harmony of infinite suggestion : it may be inter-
preted of man and woman, of thought and feeling,
of any tragedy arising from a refusal to accept the
gift of life as life offers it. Around this drama are
grouped, in the series entitled Arethusa, delightful
motives of dream, Flutes of April and September,
wherein we find a tactful re-invention of antique
imagery with a strain of personal emotion flowing
in and out of the scenes ©yoked, sometimes more
direct in expression, sometimes evanescent, so that
a charming doubt is left whether the poet has
evoked these images for the expression of his own
feeling or for their own beauty’s sake.
Henri de Regnier is the greatest man of letters,
the finest craftsman of his generation. He has a
vast vocabulary, an inexhaustible stock of
images, an infinite science of rhythm at his
command, that permit him to say anything, to
distinguish clearly, for instance, almost indefinable
nuances of reverie. His power of mere writing
is amazing; none can equal him in the develop-
ment of vast verse-paragraphs, where the phrase
seems always just on the point of being lost in an
undergrowth of incidents, and is always surely
caught hack into the main track at the right mo-
ment. He has thus been able to set at nought the
conventions of the French sentence. In the first
stanza of the little piece translated from Tel qu’
en songe there are no less than six relatives de-
pendent one on the other (a complication which it
seems impossible to represent in English) ; and
note the art whereby this elaborate syntax is ap-
plied to the dream-fusion of the rose and its
shadow ; whereas, in the second stanza, a succes-
sion of simple phrases pictures the shedding of the
petals.
A doubt, however, remains. In de Regnier’s
most personal work, the period from 1890 to 1895,
covering the whole second volume of his verse and
the whole first volume of his prose, one often asks
whether the symbols are not too particular, too
dated, for the mood : is the poet really dealing
with fundamental human disappointment or only
with a special aristocratic languor ? Is there not
at times a note of elemental wailing that should
come over vaster waters than those of any garden?
And in the later poems, if the art is surer, is there
not a lack of personality in the rheme ? is the
attitude towards life certain? is it not influ-
enced by other poets? It is a danger,
too, that de Regnier’s prose is so perfect.
His last volume of tales, The White Trefoil,
is a masterpiece : will the forthcoming novel
mark him less poet than romancer, as Aphrodite
did Pierre Lnuys ? In a word, is his instinct that
of the poet or that of the ecrivain , the skilled man
of letters ? In any case, we may be sure that
Henri de Regnier’s contribution to French letters
will be of high quality, if not of the best: if not :
the true rose of poetry it will not be far from it,
like the work of that other great man of letters,
Gautier.
Foreign Letters.
[Many Australians have friends or relatives in foreign
countries, whose letters—or portions of them - would be
valuable and entertaining reading for Australians at
home. A man in the Transvaal just now, for example—
with an eye for the picturesque and some skill with the
pen—should make a capital correspondent. Tim Billk-
tin will at all times he glad to look at interesting letters
from Australians beyond Australia. It is of little import-
ance how they are written as long as the matter is
first-hand and intrinsically worth print.]
Arthur Streeton in Cairo.
9 Waghe-el-birghet, Cairo.
Dear ,
I’ve been so excited endeavouring to get some of
the Cairo brightness in my work that I let every-
thing else slide—my liver got its first touch here
too, so I never drink any more whisky. No, nice
Chianti or the filtered water from the old Nile,
with its edges bordered with lovely old boats and
date palms and minarets—and everything else
that used to constitute the playground of Cleo-
patra. I’ve stayed in three different hotels, and
at last got into this cosy apartment with the
Italian Consul. Just on the side of the window is
a court —with windows all round where is the
residence of about twenty ladies—they make it
pretty lively in their rooms at night. Just now
there is a sewing-machine kicking up a row ; a
canary sings; waiters shout the Italian orders in
the cafe, and the turn-turn-pom of the tambourine
slides through it all. Some are singing Italian
opera ; and the great hot wind from the Desert—
the lihamxeen— -blows through everything, and
deposits a fine film of dust on the Egyptian cat
that sleeps on the bricks through it all. Every-
body sleeps in the day-time here, from about 11.30
till 1; and all the cafes are buzzing till about 2
a. m., and often long after that.
’Tis a wonderful land, this Egypt ! I’ve been
time after time through the slipjier, brass and
bronze, jewellery, perfume, silks, ring, curios
bazaars —and yesterday with another artist I did
a quick sketch in tile spice bizaar; and the alley
was simply ehoqk-a-block all the time—hundreds
of Arabs standing round. The word passed round
that we were English, and they were fairly kind
and considerate, which would not have been so if
we were Greeks. The Greeks, 1 think, are the
largest foreign population here—and are appar-
ently disliked. They are the money-lenders, I
believe.
And now, would you care to drive to the
Ghezarieh Palace Hotel and have a little whisky
at a couple of bob per nip ?—to the sulphur baths
of Heli van ? to the tombs of the Mamelukes—or
a drive through the, Cairo slums? Well, I'll go,
too. Here, Ottoman Ali ! come aboard ! We
are seated, in a comfortable victoria with fares
printed (a.id bis number 1005) in English and
French, and, I think, Arabic. Off we go ! Right
up the main street, past the luxurious and expen-
sive Sliepheard’s Hotel—winding in and out of
the swell new quarters, past a most lovely modern
Arabesque house—one of the very best —the
French Ambassador’s house; past others with
beautifully carved projecting windows with wee
little openings from which the lovely ladies of the
harem may spy you at their ease. O, most com-
comfortable-looking places . . . Ha !ha !
(Now, look here, if you are reading this in some
bran-new, business-like, unsympathetic, electric-
lighted office, you’d best put this in your jiocket
and read at home, or in some comfortable soft-
lighted little bar, with the odour of Persian attar
of roses, or, at least, a nice cigar).
Well, we’re past that fascinating architecture;
and now—by .Jove ! we are rather early, and the
Nile bridge is not open (let me see! it’s about
1.45 p.m.) . . . and the bridge is swinging round,
as you see, to allow the high-waisted spectresque
Nile boats to swoop with their lovely sails and
rudder (made of a stable-door) down the-old river.
I think quite 700 or 800 are waiting with us for
the bridge to open. On the left scores of grey and
oehre-ooloured camels (and something very putrid)
wait with their varied loads —as they’ve waited
thousands of years before. Arabs, Egyptians,
Berbers, Copts, and all sorts wait quietly —their
long gowns of a dozen degrees of blue —women sit
on donkeys, or squat on their dark haunches,
dark-robed against the oranges they sell. On the
right-hand side of our vehicle is a long building
the British barracks—windows open, and the fair-
haired, fresh-com plexioned soldier-boys of the
Empire lean out and smoke and overlook the
whole party patiently watching the section of the
bridge as (worked by hand) it moves its heavy
length round to the chain.
We get into our vehicle and bowl across that
bridge at considerable pace—dozens of donkeys,
victorias, and a few bikes, and plenty of gaunt
camels and hundreds of dark pedestrians —all on
the rush—and simultaneously from the other end
of the bridge comes a mass, also in a hurry—just
like the meeting''of two strong winds. We only
see one collision —one chap bowled clean off his pins
—lie looks badly hurt, too —never mind ! can t see
him now. Over the bridge and past the great
lions perched at the entrance, where the road
divides. Left turn ; to the natty palace built by
Imail Pasha (and near the Great Museum of
Gizeh). The other leads under great old acacias ;
and on the right with us runs the old Nile, bor-
dered with giant date-palms. Between the palms
and us are great squares of land under cultivation
—cut up like a draught-board—all the water is
pumped up and flows over the land in shallow
ditches for vegetables—and all sorts of queer beasts
of burden knocking about. At last the Ghezarieh
Palace Hotel—truly a palace, with noble grounds
and its casino and lakes—truly a regal palace,
with esplanade overlooking the Nile. And now
we’ve had our 2s. nip of whisky—look at the
Casino, the gravel walks, and the floor around all
rose and red and purple and yellow with the (con-
fetti, do you call ’em ?) coloured paper cut small—
they’ve been shying it all over the show. To-day
is the Battle of Flowers. Sit' here and have more
whisky and see the crowd. Ye gods ! how swell
they iook, yet this is the day when the lords,
peers, countesses, &c., don’t come about—only the
common people, shopkeepers, &c.
Back to the hotel. Table d’hote—all stiff and
dignified too—five or six old maids ; one or two
old ladies and their daughters. A Frenchman,
wife, and pretty little girl who sits next me, and
little girl will persist in telling all the table how
she in her pyjamas watched the islands of the Red
Sea from a port-hole, and also that I in my
pyjamas looked-out of the same port with her—
she is only six years old—and the husband, who
joined the ship at Port Said, gives me rather a
look of interrogation. Then the fat Cingalese—
speaking perfect English and the rest.
And now a smoke—where to go to-night?—
damme ! We’ll drive though the fish-market—all
aboard—a victoria again—up the narrow alley
behind the hotel and under an archway. We are
there !—winding in and out of a perfect maze of
Eastern slum and (no smells) no damned little
shops selling all sorts of mucks to eat, people
hammering away making brass and copper wares,
tailors and embroiderers at work, little cafes where
Mahometans meet and play games like back-
gammon, and drink real coffee, and behind them
again the tum-tuin-pow of tambourining and the
girl dancing. No white people about here ; and
we feel rather strange —this our first trip. On we
go winding round, and all the time our driver
calls Arabic. It sounds like Au-arr-rigla, Ay-
arr-rigla, incessantly: and it means “Mind your
feet! ” Marvellous how he guides the vehicle
around the little corners (a quarter the width of
Little Bourke-street, and all along each side you
see low windows (three feet from the ground)—
bars over them, and three or four girls inside lie
down on their stomachs, and with their heads
Eressed against the bars, attract your attention
y a certain hiss —hiss—hiss, just like adders.
And yet one sees the red coat of Tommy Atkins
about—which may lie good for Egypt—for think
of the victory of the Abyssinians over the Italians
—due, they say, to the fact that Atkins had been
years ago larking about in Abyssinia—and mdae
the Abyssinians a conquering race. However,
you’ve heard that before.
One evening since I have driven through the fish
market, with a lady in the carriage next me; and
it excited her intensely to view these hundreds of
women with dark stuff rubbed round their eyes,
and spots of black about the brow and chin, re-
clining in the most dreadfully seductive attitudes
(or otherwise). It somewhat astonished the lady
to see some of them stand up and wriggle as we
passed (within hand reach). I think their being
behind the bars is a police regulation, otherwise
the feminine hordes would mob and reduce one to
a jelly.
One afternoon I tried a walk through these
slums alone, and it wasn’t pleasant —the guilty,
murderous-looking denizen with one eye all rotten
with ophthalmia, and the flies bucking into the
other as fast as they could—oh) he was a beast!
One little wench of 15 rushed from behind the
bars and held me with one arm. Eventually,
with the fiend and ophthalmia at my elbow 1 got
bushed, and still kept trying to look as though I’d
been through hundreds of times, not heeding the
persistent invitations of the fiend to come for a
few piastres and see a donkey-riding exhibition.
At last virtue (of course) triumphed, and I found
the thread which led out of the cave.
It isn’t difficult to be virtuous or goody-goody
here, I’m sorry" to say. On entering Cairo the
first time an aroma greeted me I never was con-
scious of before —from the donkeys, I think —and
it gave such a suggestion of an old, old, Oriental
city—and the long-continued bellowing of the
donkeys was just like the creaking and groaning
of a heavy waggon down hill—and then the smells
for the first time in the bazaars ! Oh, it’s inde-
scribable : foods, spices, and queer things one
never dreams of. On one side an Arab bare to the
waist pounds spice with a heavy pestle and a mor-
tar of solid stone four feet high and weighing half
a ton. Next him a shop full of beautitui lam s
for mosques ; and then a wee little bov sits, and
with a few lines of string and a bit of stick for a
loom works incessantly a lovely piece of tapestry
—a mass of design and varied color. Ihe piece is
many yards long ; he is about 12 years old ; and
two or three bits of string and a bit of wood for
tools. Then the perfume bazaar, and the wily
merchants who sit in their booths two yards
square and read, then hand the rarest attar of
roses from Persia, &c. Then the slipper bazaar—
thousands and thousands of red and bun and
lemon-colored slippers hanging like an exhibition
of scarlet herrings or capsicums. And the bronze
and brass and silver work bazaars ! and the
jewellery bazaar —each little shanty with its
great iron safe. Then the Persian carpets and
the silk wares, and the silk carpets !
All are orientals here—Arabs arid Copts, with
many a grand old face. Princely-looking chaps a
few of them (1 write this with a stick of mcense
burning under my nose, the mosquitoes are very
bad). These bazaars are the sight of Cairo, with
the grand large design of some of the 'mosques and
the fine arches and fastnesses of the city. Ihe
English hold the city by the citadel on the rise of
a neighboring hill, and with their guns could m a
few minutes shell the palaces, mosques in fact
the whole place. A mosque next the “ Sultan
Hassan ” one was left with the top unfinished, be-
cause the intended top would block out the Khe-
dive’s palace from the guns (so Mr. Atkins told
me). Ha, ha !
Little by little the French official markings
about the city are being taken down, and the
notices, directions, etc., printed in plain English
and Arabic. It must annoy the French very
much, to see the place held by a few English
troops—who, by the way, are all waiting ready—-
and police too—to make a dash, and nip a revolu-
tion in the bud, or a rising of the Moslems against
the much-disliked Greeks (I dislike Mahometans,
and the Greeks especially), who disport them-
selves with a gaudy display of revolvers in their
belts, and serve ’em right if the quiet-looking
Arabs do rise and give them a doing.
The entrance to the Khedive’s palace is a very
jerry-built looking concern —all pretentious, and
with cheap Italian decorations and oil-painted
columns of marble (haven’t been in his grounds
and don’t want to). The: eunuch is the most
hideous, wretched-looking devil on earth, they
still buy boys, and the majority die from the
effects. They arc, I understand, -kept standing in
sand to prevent ncemorage (can't spell hcemorrage
—no— d -d if I can). Yes, the Mahometan law
(the Koran) says you may marry four wives and
you can keep as many as you can afford ; and often
the beasts choose out of their 200 women an ugly
negro Nubian who is favourite, and is waited
on by the dear, fair-skinned and sad-eyed Cir-
cassian women—:and if the boss doesn’t like one
or a dozen, the bloody eunuch promptly strangles
her and drops her down the well (there are some
wells in the fish-market if you happen to be
robbed). . . . Yes, this silent cure and cold horror
goes on through Cairo as I write, and will till the
Sultan and Constantinople are squashed, and
police are free to enter these Moslem prisons. It
never rains in Egypt; there are no drains ; and
each house in the city has an underground chasm
to hold all -the refuse' that may go down —all the
hotels the same.
ARTHUR STREETON.
THE TRIOLET.
Fit for moonlit dells are you,
For you ’re the Muses’ violet;
Shy and sweet and known to few
Fit for moonlit dells are you—
Fair girl-eyes of fairest blue
And Austral stars !—Sweet Triolet!
Fit for moonlit dells are you,
For you ’re the Muses’ violet.
PHIL. F.
THE TRlOLET.— ANOTHER WAY.
Miserable Triolet,
I have tried you. All in vain !
You the Muses’ Violet —
Miserable Triolet !
Make the poor book-fellow sweat •
Grind his teeth at your refrain !

Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1893.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “ Eczema Capitis,” from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M‘Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. I will only
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, And remain yours
sincerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

SPECIAL NOTICE
THE AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOCIETY is prepared to LEND in any amounts,
at lowest current rates, upon-FREEHOLD and LEASEHOLD CITY and SUBURBAN PROPERTIES; on
FREEHOLD and CONDITIONALLY-PURCHASED PASTORAL and FARMING LANDS.
LOANS can be arranged either for a fixed term, or repayable by instalments of principal and interest
extending over long periods. '
Arrangements have been made, whereby valuations for country properties can be made for a very mode-
rate fee, to be paid as evidence of bona fides on the part of the applicant.
The legal expenses in connection with mortgages are also on a specially low scale.
- The Societ .V is also prepared to entertain proposals for purchasing Reversionary Interests in Estates, whether
in this colony or elsewhere, or to lend upon the security of the same on liberal terms.
Forms of proposal may be obtained on application at the Head Office, or any District Office of the Society.
Head Office, 87 Pitt-st., Sydney.
RICHARD TEECE, General Manager.
ROBERT B. CAMERON, Secretary.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A word to parsons who think of taking up
the anti-danoing “ cause ”: Rev. Gladstone,
the Nathalian apostle, told the Vic. Church of
England Synod that his screw had got down to
<6120 a year. This was by way of interjection
while a venerable Archdeacon was picturing a
poor proposed bishop (of Bendigo) starving on
=6550 a year.
“ Hermes ” on one aspect of the medical
struggle for a crust :
It is very easy to start a medical benefit and industrial
insurance society in N.S.W. Writer while holding a
position under the Crown became acquainted with a
medico. Medico was in the office the day writer got the
sudden sack, and immediately asked him to step down
to the surgery. His poor practice refused to increase,
and he had determined to start a medical benefit society.
He had a number of prospectuses printed which stated
that the Bolus and Coffin Medical Benefit Society had
decided to commence operations in Sydney on the lines
that had proved so successful in the large English pro-
vincial towns, and had engaged that eminent physician
and surgeon, Dr. Smug, F.R.0.5., L.R.C.P., &c. The
society was willing to enroll members, subject to ex-
amination, for Is. entrance-fee, and 6di per week after-
wards for a man and wife and two children. For Is. per
week members’ wives would be attended at accouchment.
(That point was to be strongly dwelt upon with young
couples when canvassing). If the family consisted of
more than four there was a slight increase. Medicine
was included for the money, but members would have to
bring their own bottles. Writer went out to canvass.
First door that opened revealed a forbidding-looking
harridan who sternly demanded what the devil he
wanted. 'Explained the aims and objects of the society
to her. She seemed interested. She was a dreadful
female, and writer had not much trouble in screwing up
his courage to mention the accouchment clause. She
grew affable and invited him inside : “ Say that again ?”
Clause re-read. “Are yous any relations to that man
Sheridan wot was hanged? ’Cos if yer aren t it's soon ye
will be—get out of ine house ye biaggard !To come talk-
ing about midwifery to a lone woman when her husband’s
out.” Most people thought the show had some connec-
tion with Sheridan, who had just been executed, and
sternly refused to have any dealings with it. Struggled
on for a fortnight, and was just beginning to get some
hope of doing business, when the medico informed me
one night that his wife had been out and got 16 members
in one afternoon. “ You didn’t go strong enough on the
accouchment clause. Mrs. found that that brought
’em every time.” Then he sacked me, and I went away.
When I came back to Sydney, about the first person I saw
was the medico, driving down George-street in a flash
turn-out, with an elegant groom. Whether his medical
benefit society flourished I kn w not; hut, if it didn’t, it
ought to have done so. Writer, and two other canvassers
he had, were instructed, when returning to report the
day’s success, to ask every man, woman, and Chinaman
the way to Dr. ’s, and mention the fa,c.t that it wa
said he was very clever—could do anything short o
resurrect the dead. One day, about 15 superim-lookingf
unemployed "’ere hived to wander round the blocks
adjacent to the surgery and make the same enquiries,
and tell of the wonderful cures they heard he had
effected.
Seven Honest Men.
Their faith in one another it was wonderfully
great,
But their claim it hadn’t paid a decent dividend
of late;
And so in solemn meeting they decided they
would sell
If the ‘ ‘ wash ” then in the puddles failed to pan-
out very well.
They knew their claim was envied, and rejoiced
that it was so ;
That fact it could be fairly worked to advertise
the show.
But never thought of “salting” struck the com -
pany just then,
For O ! they loved the shining truth, those seven
honest men.
Then it’s set the water moving,
And it’s soak the mullock well,
And while the yield’s improving
’Tis a foolish thing to sell.
And keep the old horse going
Till the slurry is no more,
And gold is freely showing
All around the puddling-floor.
Now, one man of the party thought that, as they
meant to sell,
A very simple plan of his would help to do it
well;
And in manner surreptitious then a “ rather tidy
weight ”
He dropped into the puddler when the night was
very late.
But O ! that inspiration didn’t come to him alone,
For each man of the seven struck the notion “ on
his own; ”
Though none the awful secret brought within the
others’ ken,
For O ! the truth was sacred to those seven honest
men.
Then it’s keep the water going
Till the “ ripples ” overflow,
And shining specks are glowing
As the pebbles rolling go.
Watch the boulders and the rough ones,
They may carry gold away ;
We can only wash the stuff once
Or the claim will never pay.
They had a splendid “cleaning-up” that day
upon the flat,
And never thought of selling for a moment after
that.
They mended up the windlass, and they “sol-
diered up ” with care,
And opened out upon the wash, and “ drove her ”
here and there.
And in a fortnight after had a pyramid in sight
Of wash-dirt for the puddler ; for they’d grafted
day and night.
But 0 ! what language picturesque succeeded
silence, when
They panned-off half-a-penny weight, those seven
honest men.
BEN SUN.

FOX BROTHERS & Go., Ltd.,
<v> Wellington, Somerset, ENGLAND.
Makers of Worsted Coatings, Vicunas, Serges, Tweeds, Tennis, and Costume Cloths.
Contractors to the English Admiralty, War Office, Mia Office, Crown Colonies, and Metropolitan police.
THEIR Mills are capable of an annual production of 2,000,000 yards of material, and find employment for 1,200 hands. Reliable
records show that the business at Wellington was founded early in the eighteenth century, and the present Company have in
their possession a balance sheet dated 1733.
FOXS’ PURE INDIGO.
As the result of many years of experience, FOX£ PURE INDIGO DYE is recognised as the best in the Market. The superiority of Pure Indigo over other
dyes may be briefly summed up as follows :
FASTNESS OF THE DYE.—lndigotine, the colouring principle of Indigo, is insoluble in water, alcohol, ether, and dilute acids, and alkalies either hot or
cold, hence its extreme fastness for all conditions of ordinary wear and tear. It is also quite fast to Sunlight, and for an all-round dye there is nothing superior to
it. There is no need to point out the advantage there is in a perfectly fast dye, as compared with one which may be fast to light, but will not stand perspiration, or
washing, or vice versa. .
STRENGTH AND HANDLE.—CIoth dyed Pure Indigo is stronger and tougher than the same cloth dyed in other ways, owing to the dye-bath being at
a comparatively low temperature, and the absence of any injurious chemicals or mordaunts. In almost all other dyes, the cloth is first boiled in the mordaunt and
then in the dye for some hours altogether. This always impoverishes the wool fibre, and destroys much of its natural lustre and softness, whereas the Indigo Vat
with its soft, mellow liquor, adds to the fulness and richness of the fabric. Indigo Dyeing being a mechanical process rather than a chemical one, actually feeds the cloth.
JHLII their standard cloths are stamped every two yards on the hack,

These Celebrated Serges can be obtained from the leading Tailors and Drapers throughout the Australian Colonies.
See foi? yourselves that the Serge is stamped—FOX PURE INDIGO,-as many dealers for the sake of an
extra profit try to sell inferior-dyed cloths, remarking “that they are just as good as Pure Indigo.”

ROOFING IS A DECIDED

Box 22, G.P.0., Sydney, 18181‘99.
Messrs. E. RI6H & CO.; Ltd., Sydney.
Dear Sirs, •
In the construction of roofs when timber is costly 1 have toith marked
success used toire netting as a foundation upon which to lay Ruberoid.
In the roof framing the' rafters need not be less than 4ft- apart, and
upon these should be placed broad side up battens or purlins, say, 4 by l\in.
sunk flush with the face of the rafters spaced 2ft. Bin. apart from centre to
centre, and over the whole should be tightly stretched from eaves to ridge wire-
netting, say, 16 gauge by 3or fin. mesh firmly secured at the ends. The
Width of the netting should be such that the edges can be nailed on to the centre
of each rafter.
Over the top of the wire-netting the sheets of Ruberoid should be placed,
commencing at the caves batten on to which it should be tacked down per-
manen'ly, the second sheet should then be placed so as to overlap the first by
4in. (after using the usual paint, of course), and the bottom of this and the
top of the first sheet securely nailed to the second batten, and so on until
completed.
The Ruberoid should also be nailed to the rafters for additional safety.
If ordinary care is used, any handy man can in this way construct a neat,
serviceable, light, substantial and inexpensive roof.
Yours truly, 0. M‘MASTER.
[copy] Albury, 10th December, 1898.
Messrs. E. RICH ds CO., Ltd., Merchants,
O'Connell Street, Sydney.

splendid horses for the use of the mounted men of
. the Victorian contingent. Before being handed
over, the horses will bo trained to romstin quiet under fire, to lie down at the word of com-
mand, and do many other things which would prove of service in campaigning."
Messrs. Fitzgerald might then take the Soudan Donkey in hand—teach him a
few amusing tricka, and the Transvaal Circus Co., Ltd., would be complete.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 21, 1899.
The First Federal Premier and His
Programme.
The Bulletin had a dream the other night ;
it dreamt that it was appointed Premier of
the first Federal Ministry in Australia.
And in view of that vision it has prepared
the following programme, which is the kind
of programme it would lay before Parlia-
ment if it were Federal Premier. Having
no expectation of that billet it is the one
which it proposes to urge upon the Premier,
whoever he may be :
TO THE MEMBERS OF THE TWO HOUSES OF THE
PARLIAMENT OF AUSTRALIA :
The first Commonwealth Ministry, having secured its
billet, and hung up its hat, and shaken down to some
extent into its new position, desires to lay before you Lhe
following propositions :
(1) The Legislature is occupying temporary quarters in
Melbourne, but as it is essential that it should have a
home of its own at the earliest possible date it is pro-
posed that a committee shall be appointed to visit pro-
pose I sites for a permanent capital and report on those
most suitable. A cooi, elevated and well-watered region
is essential, so the committee will deal with the N.S.W.
table-land only, and not waste its time in the lowlands.
(2) It. is specified in clause 125 of this Constitution that
the capital must be located on a.Federal territory of not
less than 100 square miles. A great effort will he made,
however, to secure a suitable Federal territory of some-
thing approaching 5000 square miles. All Crown lands
on this territory will be handed over to the Federal
Government free of cost. You will be invited toauthorise
the compulsory purchase of all private lainls on the
Federal territory ; to establish a board of arbitration to
fix the price in case of dispute ; and to vote the neces-
sary funds. A measure will also be submitted to you de-
claring that none of the land of the Federal territory in
question shall be sold under any circumstances whatever,
and that the freehold thereof and the income derived
therefrom shall be the inalienable property of the Com-
monwealth. , ...
(3) You will be invited to consider a scheme for leasing
the land of the Federal territory, on equitable terms, the
great object being to secure for the Commonwealth the
whole benefit of the increased. values created by the
establishment of a Federal city. It is also for this reason
that it is proposed, if possible, to build that city in the
centre of a territory of 5000 square miles, whereof the
Commonwealth is both sovereign and sole ground land-
lord. On a much smaller territory a great deal of the
increased values created by the construction of a Federal
city would go to enrich private landlords outside the
Federal border. .... .. ,
(4) It is proposed to lay out on this territory an ideal
capital with tree-lined streets 100 feet wide, and abundant
reserves, and governed by rigid building laws to prevent
the erection of unsightly structures. Even a hoot-factory
or a bone-mill will have to be a work devoid of archi-
tectural hideousness.
• • * •
(5) Partly with a view to raising the money for these-
works without interest, and partly as an essential feature
of a uniform Australian hanking law, which will be
brought forward at an early date, it is proposed to adopt
the principle so successfully applied in Canada whereby
the banks are required to hold at least 40 per cent, of
their cash reserves in Slate paper currency. This will
give the Federal Government command of (say)
£10,000,000 as a perpetual loan absolutely free of interest,
and more later on. Out of this fund it is proposed to
purchase all private lands inside the Federal territory,
to erect Parliament Houses and public offices, construct
roads, bridges, and water and sewerage works, and other
needful undertakings.
(6) The rentals derived from all lands inside the
Federal territory shall not be treated as ordinary revenue,
but shall be set aside as a public works fund, and used
for that purpose only.
(7) Two great works which it is proposed to make a first
charge upon the fund are a railway to connect the Wes-
tralian railway system with that of the eastern States,
and the completion of the transcontinental line between
Adelaide and Port Darwin.
(8) It is considered essential that the Northern Terri-
tory, with all its assets and liabilities, be surrendered by
South Australia and taken over by the Federal Govern-
ment. Until its population grows sufficiently large to
enable it to support a State Government of its own it will
be administered as Federal territory by a special depart-
ment. A separate account will be kept of its debt, re-
venue, and expenditure. It is anticipated that the Terri-
tory lands will become a very profitable asset when
railway connection is established between that hitherto
lost province and the South. Any surplus revenue
derived from them will be applied—one-half in reduction
of the Territory’s debt; the remainder to public works
within the Territory. Meanwhile land-jobbers and cheap-
labor syndicates are warned off.
(9) You will be invited to consider a uniform Protective
Federal tariff, calculated to raise about £7,000,000 or
£7,500,000 annually.
* * * *
(10) You will be invited to consider the advisability of
levying a Federal income-tax on the following basis:
Exemption up to £250 ; 4d. in the £ on the first £lOOO
after that,; 6d. in the £ on the second £lOOO ; Bd. on all
further income. This scale to apply only to incomes
derived from personal exertion. On incomes derived
from property the rate to be doubled. Also a Federal
land-tax, on the following scale : Exemption up to
£5OO ; after that the tax to be Id. in the £ on
all estates up to £7500 in value ; estates worth
over £7500 and under £15,000 to pay a penny
and one-eighth in the £ ; and a similar increase of one-
eighth of a penny in the £ to be made for each £7500 of
extra value until on estates worth £97,500 and over the
tax shall he 3d. in the £. Also, in the case of absentees
both land and income taxes shall be increased by 50 per
cent. At least three-fourths, and if possible the whole
of the proceeds of this tax shall be paid over to the
States, each State receiving the amount of its own con-
tribution, less Cost of collection. The purpose in making
the land and income taxes Federal in place of State im-
posts is to secure uniformity throughout the Common-
wealth, to discourage absenteeism and the accumulation
of large estates, and to foster a race of small independent
proprietors. The Federal Government takes it for
granted that the States will abolish their present land
and income raxes. If they don’t it is their own affair.
(11) A special feature in the proposed Federal land-tax
is that each owner shall value his own land for taxation
purposes. If the valuation is considered too small the
Government may appeal against it to the Supreme Court.
Also, the Federal Government shall always have the right
to resume the laud at 10 per cent, above the owner's valu-
ation, the value of improvements to be settled by arbitra-
tion. A right of resumption on this basis shall also be
vested in the State Government, in the municipal
authority, and in the tenant in possession, but the
Federal Government shall have prior claim.
(12) A scheme for the resumption of large estates for
closer settlement will also he submitted to you. This
work shall be done in each State under the supervision
of a Board appointed partly by the Federal and partly by
the State authority.
(13) It is proposed to establish Federal conciliation and
arbitration courts with extensive powers to deal with
strikes and trade disputes, and to compel the acceptance
of their awards.
* » • •
(14) It is proposed to combine the State Land Banks of
Victoria and 8.A., the N.S. VV. Advances to Settlers De-
partment and the Westralian Agricultural Batik into a
Federal Land Bank with branches in all the provinces.
In each province the bank will be supervised by a board
of dire tors appointed partly by the Federal Government
and part ly by the State.
(15) It is proposed to establish a Federal Life Insurance
Department. The Government shall be empowered to
take over one or more of the existing private insurance
institutions as the nucleus of the new department.
(16) It is considered desirable that the issue of bank-
notes should be a monopoly of the Federal Government.
These notes shall be received by Federal, State and
Municipal governments in payment of all dues and
taxes.
(17) One of the first duties of the Federal Government
is to take over the postal, telegraph, customs, excise,
defence and various other departments of the provinces,
and to ascertain the amount payable to each province
for buildings, &c., taken over. Most of these works have
been constructed out of loan funds, and if they were
paid for in cash there would probably be a tendency to
treat the money thus obtained as revenue instead of
using it in reduction of State liabilities. Therefore it is
proposed that the Federal Government shall make pay-
ment by assuming the necessary amount of each pro-
vince's 3 per cent. debt. Where a province’s 3 per cent,
debt isn’t large enough to equal the amount due to it
the balance will be paid by taking over 3i per cent, debt
at a sufficient discount to cover the extra interest.
(18) A Bill will be laid before you for the conversion of
the various State debts into a uniform 3 per cent. Federal
stock with a currency of 40 years, and after that redeem-
able at the option of the Federal Government. It is pro-
posed, in the first place, that the Federal Government
•shall take over the management of the existing State
debts, in order to reduce expenses, but without guaran-
teeing them in any way. Also, that it shall debit each
province every year with the exact amount it paid on
account of its public debt in 1899, and deduct said pay-
ment every year from that province’s share of its customs
and excise revenue. That it shall convert all loans as
they fall due into a Federal 3 per cent, stock, and that
it shall keep before the public creditor a standing offer
to give Federal 3 per cent, stock at par in exchange
for the existing higher-priced provincial securities.
The amount saved in interest on each province's
separate debt will be applied to the reduction of ihit
province’s liabilities until they are wholly extinguished.
The total debts to he taken over are about ' 190,000,000,
and it is calculated that, without any addition whatever
to the public burdens, and merely hy the saving in in-
terest, the whole amount can be paid off in a little over
60 y ears.
(19) A measure to establish uniform postage, telegraph
and money-order rates is in preparation. The vexed
question of newspaper postage has been carefully con-
sidered, and it has been decided to charge a rate of Jd.
up to ten ounces on all papers posted inside the Common-
wealth to places inside the Commonwealth. Designs for
Federal postage stamps are invited. No design'With a
Queen’s head on it will lie even considered.
(20) Australia urgently requires a uniform .railway
gauge in order to promote speedy transit and economy of
management, and to make I he country more capable of
defence. In fact until a uniform railway gauge is decided
upon It is useless for the Federal Government lo do an3’-
thing regarding the construction of the two transcontin-
ental railways ; to build 3000 miles of line only to relay it
when the all-Australiari railway gailgeisdefinitely decided,
w ould be an absuvd proceeding. Therefore the Federal
Government proposes opening up communication with the
State Governments with a view to arranging some terms on
which the entire existing railway system of t he Six States
can be taken over, and future railway construction be
made a Federal business. If this is done, the work of
converting the whole system to one uniform gauge will
be undertaken at an early date.
(21) A Bill dealing with aliens has been prepared. Its
leading features are that no Asiatic, negro, kanaka,
Papuan, or other variety of black, yellow, mottled, or
speckled person, shall be allowed to enter the Common-
wealth territory except on payment of £2OO. If also
deals with the exclusion of diseased persons, criminals
and lunatics.
(22) An old-age-pension scheme providing for every
indigent person, over 60, of reasonably good character
(absolute perfection will not be insisted on), and with a
record of 25 years residence in the territory of the Com-
monwealth, is another prominent feature of the Govern-
ment’s policy.
(23) The Federal Government proposes to make a fresh
start in Australian finance. It intends to he honest.
' No money will be borrowed at interest except for directly
reproductive purposes. No money will be borrowed ex-
cept in case of absolute necessity. No money will be
borrowed (loans for the conversion of old debts only ex-
cepted) ourside the Commonwealth. All new Federal
loans shall have a sinking fund attached.
(24) The measure providing fora uniform Federal fran-
chise which will be submitted to you will be short and
comprehensive. Its general tendency is to give one vote
and one only to every sane adult male and female, aliens
excepted, who has resided a year in any part of the
country, who can read and write, and who is out of gaol.
(25) The Government has many other important
matters in view, but it advances these as a few things to
go on with.
* » * *
The above are 25 of The Bulletin’s rea-
sons for suggesting Charles Cameron
Kingston, of South Australia, as first Pre-
mier of the Commonwealth. Among the
front-rank politicians of this country, he is
the only one who looks like being competent
to carry out the kind of programme outlined
above. The old Parliamentary hands, the old
Tories, the old, slow-going dwellers in a rut,
are not wanted on this kind of voyage. When
a new nation starts with a clean sheet, it
wants to entrust the creation of the new
order of things to men who know what to do
with a clean sheet, and those old politicians
who have left such a melancholy record of
blotches on the old dirty sheet, should all
stand down. The new nation begins with
practically no old precedents' to shackle it,
and it requires a live man who can make the
new precedents wisely. Also it wants a
courageous man who won’t be terrified when
he finds himself in the novel situation of
having no old precedents to guide him. Hav-
ing made a most democratic constitution, it
wants a proved democrat to work it. And
having to start everything anew, it needs a
man who has shown that he can make new
things—not an incurable repairer whose
career has always been one of putting patches
on old things, no matter how deftly the
patches were put on. And accordingly this
paper recommends Kingston for the job—
always with the right to withdraw that
recommendation if its candidate does any-
thing wildly and hilariously foolish between
now and the date when the new Government
starts.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Concerning' the Awful Contingent
Henry Lawson to The Bulletin on “The
Australian Contingent to the Transvaal ” :
What does it all amount to ? Only this : That, because
of the craving for the sensational horn of the world’s pre-
sent social system—the mad longing for change, inten-
sified in Australia by the hopeless flat monotony of the
country and its history—some of ns are willing—wilfully,
blindly eager, mad!—to cross the sea and shoot men whom
we never saw and whose quarrel we do not and cannot
understand. Our cry is “ For England !” or “ Blood is
thicker than water!” and so we seek to blind and
deceive ourselves as fools who are unanimous in their
eagerness to sacrifice right, justice, truth - everything, to
satisfy their selfish craving for what they consider a pic-
nic—to have “some fun” —to have a spree. England is
not going to fight for England—not for English interests,
but for the interests of syndicates, and because she is
forced to it by the power of private gold assisted by its
contemptible and ignorantly-willing cat’s-paw, Jingoism.
Oh! the cruel, blind, murderous Crimes of Crowds! —of
Popular Cries ! And we are going to help a big nation
crush a little one which is fighting for country and free-
dom only, and all because we want to have a spree—to
have a “ knock-round.”
I was never in South Africa, but the white man is
pretty much the same all over the world, and I saw the
Uitlander-versus-Boer play on a small scale in Westralia.
There it was T’othersider-wsus-Sandgroper. Because
tlie Westralian people and Government wouldn’t go out
of their houses and sacrifice everything to the camp-
ing swarm of adventurers whose only idea was to make
as much money out of the country as possible in the
shortest possible time, and then get out of if, they were
howled at as the “Forrest Gang,” the “ Sandgroper,”
&c., &e. ; and there, Australians once (in Coolgardie) ap-
pealed to the British syndicates for assistance to crush
the Westralian Government! Not only English against
English, but Australians against Australians ; and all and
only for gold—as is the Transvaal trouble.
I don’t know the Boers, but this I know. That in Wes-
traiia, as a workman, 1 would rather work for a settled
Sandgroping boss who is content to pay fair wages and
have his work done comfortably than for a boss from
my own “ side ” —a ’Tothersider who, in his sj fish hurry
to “make money” and get out of tiie country, would
sweat and drive his employes without mercy.
We have the Soudan affair to blot our history for ever,
and are not ashamed nor satisfied ; and 1 tell you that
the thing we are going to do about the Transvaal trouble
is ten times more shameful than the sending of the Con-
tingent to Soudan, a hundred times more shameful than
our “ birthstain,” with all its buried horrors dug-up,
would be. That, indeed, was only really shameful to
England. And I say we would do better, and it would
be more just, and just as reasonable if we took up arms
and fought to the death, one province against another,
here in Australia.
When England meets her match, or something like it,
the sending of contingents then will be another question
altogether.
A Very Small Jingo.
The frantic Jingo of Newcastle (N.S.W.)
Morning Herald :
The prospect held out to Britons, Germans, French,
Americans, that if they care to forswear their own
nationalities they will be made members of a one-horse
South African republic, and become cousins to the large
Kruger family, will not be regarded as a specially in-
viting one.
Query : Does this loud, wild buster, and other
busters like him, know what the row is sup-
posed to be all about? It professes to be solely
because the British in the Transvaal are not
allowed sufficient facilities for becoming Boer
citizens and having votes in that capacity. It
was demanded by Britain that a British sub-
ject in the Transvaal should be permitted, after
a brief residence there, to forswear Britain,
and throw up the glorious; flag, and leave off
his allegiance to the Queen, and abandon “ Rule
Britannia,” and the National Anthem, and
British protection, and the noble heritage of
"his fathers, and become a kind of manufactured
Boer, and a full-blown citizen and a voter of a
little one-horse African republic. It is because
theßritonis denied this blessed privilege that he
is fighting ! He wants to be a Dutchman and
with the Dutchmen stand. He wants to be a
subject of Kruger, so that when the Transvaal
and Britain get to war Kruger can order him
- out as a Boer soldier t,o shoot the British, and
hang him if he won't go. The whole trouble is
because the Kruger Government didn’t make
the terms easy enough on which the Briton
could forswear his country and become a
glorious Boer. Even up to a late date Alfred
Milner was heard stating that if the Briton
were allowed to throw his country overboard
and become a full and complete Boer after five
years’ residence, all might still be well. Logi-
cally, it is an amusing situation.
♦
The Toiling 1 (Jutlander and the Outlander
Capitalist.
J. A. Verhoef, one of the founders of the
Johannesburg Labor-party, interviewed by
Brisb. Worker, threw light on the real causes
of the Transvaal war ; which —in the multitude
of faked cables, syndicate-purchased news-
papers, and Jingo outcries—Australians know
little about. Said Mr. Verhoef:
We founded the Johannesburg Labor party not long
ago. It consists principally of the miners, but includes
also the Stonemasons’ and the Engine-drivers’ Associa-
tions. We are dead against Rhodes’s capitalist
clique, who are engineering the British aggression with
the object of seizing the country. We know from experi-
ence what to expect from them if they succeed. They
control the Kimberley district in which the diamond
mines are situated, and in which the working classes are
degraded by long hours, low wages, and political dis-
franchisement.
The Jameson raid was an attempt on the part of the
Rhodes clique to seize the Transvaal by force. Taunted
in Cape Colony afterwards with having made a mess of it,
and with having failed, Rhodes replied, “Well, we will
try the constitutional way now’.” A gigantic capitalist
plot was then formed. Knowing that the sympathies of
the working classes lay with the Boers, the mining mag-
nates set themselves to work to alienate that sympathy.
First Capitalism in the Transvaal began to organise itself.
It began to close down the mines in order to throw men
out of work. It displaced white labor wherever it could,
employing Kaffir labor instead. It laid idle many great
works that were going on. Barnato’s Theatre, which
was being built, was left unfinished, and has remained
so. In this way, by throwing out of employment
hundreds of working-men, a great ariificial depression
was brought about by the capitalists. Then the daily
press, which they own, started to shriek against the
Boers. The depression was attributed to the heavy taxes
which, it was maintained, the mine-owners had to pay on
dynamite, on the carriage of coal, to the duties on
machinery, and to every plausible pretext possible. The
working-classes were roused to agitation in support of the
supposed grievances of the millionaires. One by one the
Boer Government conceded the points demanded.
It reduced the railway rates for coal to below the
actual cost of carriage. It abolished machinery duties,
it reduced the duties on necessaries, hut increased the
duties on champagne, cigars, wines, and mineral waters
The Capitalists complained that the Kaffirs were unreli
able as miners owing to the Boer law permitting them to
purchase grog. On this point their demands were con-
ceded also. Having exhausted every plausible pretext,
the Capitalist organisations then attacked the franchise
and blamed it for the depression. Kruger conceded
point after point on this matter, until it became a ques
tion of constitutional suicide. When the crisis wa9
reached Kruger demanded arbitration ; Chamberlain
refused, hence the war It is all rubbish this pretence of
fighting for the political enfranchisement of the Out-
landers. I heard a millionaire boasting the other week in
Johannesburg that they did not care a fig about the
ranchise.
The Outlanders are divided into two classes. There is
the working- class Outlander and the capitalist Outlander.
The working men do not complain about the Transvaal
Government. The Boer law is as liberal as the English,
and makes no distinction between Boer and foreigner.
The worker doesn’t bother his head about the Govern-
ment or about the franchise. Most of the miners have
not brought their wives and families into the country,
and do not intend to remain in it longer than thiy can
help. The place has no attraction for them and very
little interest. The capitalist Outlanders include the
shop-keeping classes, the lawyers, the commission
agents, and the bankers. All little fleas on the backs of
the big fleas who own the mines. It is this following
that is howling so loudly against the Boer franchise on
behalf of their masters, the big capitalists.
Germany and France are not interfering in this matter,
because the English are not only fighting the battle of
the English capitalist, but the batrle of the French and
German capitalist as well. French and German capi-
talists are up to their necks in the Rand gold-mines, and
the Rothschilds, who can make or unmake war these
days, are up to their necks in it also. To show you what
they can do : When Kruger met the first demands of the
capitalists and greatly reduced taxation, the Boer
Government tried to float a loan on the European maiket
to make up the deficiency in revenue. To harass him.
the Rothschilds opposed the loan, which failed, the
European financiers refusing to lend the necessary
money.
By the way, do you know that Milner, the British
Commissioner in South Africa, was one of the founders
of the Fabian Society, a London Socialist organisation ?
To what base uses, &c. Also, do you recollect how
Rhodes gave £lO,OOO to the funds of the Irish Party when
Parnell led it in the fight for Koine Rule for Ireland ?
A Small Boom in the G-oat Market.
The attempt to pump up enthusiasm in Aus-
tralia over the Transvaal-contingent business
has been a poor affair at the best. There was
no popular demand for a military howl in South
Africa to begin with. The movement was not
started by the people, but by politicians—by
the men who are now Plain Bill and ver\
ordinary John, and who hope to become “Sir
William or “Sir” John by spending other
people’s money on ammunition, and sending
other people to stop the hurried cannon-ball, or
to arrive after someone else has stopped it.
Aud even among the politicians it has not been
all joy. In South Australia, the Upper House
decided to authorise that province’s little
shooting expedition only by the casting-vote <>!
the President, and the proposal only just
managed to crawl through a cold and unpleasant
Assembly. It brought about a political crisis
in Queensland, and in N.S.W. the new Premier
was compelled to promise that he would wait
for the meeting of Parliament instead of rush-
ing headlong into the breach. A good many
men are willing to go for excitement, for
change of scene, for the pleasure of a fight, for
the sake of getting any kind of a job at decern
wages, many of them in the hope that they
may find good billets in South Africa, and
thereby get away gratis from a distressed
country which they can’t leave on their own
account for want of the needful passage-money .
One or two Parliaments have sung “ Gorsave
in a way that showed more energy than tune,
and it is cabled that Premier Seddon, of Maori-
land, in an excited moment got through that
little poem on a platform all by himself; bui
the public has not risen and perpetrated the
Anthem on a large scale. If the war is a short
one the painful fact already begins to assert
itself that the Australian Contingent may only
be in time to help with the heavy baggage in
the rear, which is an ignominious suspicion. A
few newspapers have tried to lash the public
into fury with tales about the Boer’s neglect
to wash himself, but there are many' unwashed
people in the world—there are many of them
even in Australia; and, anyhow, the Austra-
lian isn’t a Turkish-bath attendant with a mis-
sion to soap-and-waler his fellow-men. The
whole business has been sad and apathetic,
except on the part of those leading


grovellers who gather the green an'l juicy
advt. out of it. N.S. W. was in the contingent
business once before; it gathered no glory
worth mentioning, but it paid £300,000 for a
goat. And N.S.W., with that fact in mind,
is less enthusiastic than of old. The idea of
speculating in the cold, perfunctory goat doesn t
appeal to it this time with the old force. Vic-
toria, which has its goat yet to buy, is warmer,
and it is the only province where there is any
sign of the war-fever having spread among the
mass of the people. Considering that Britain
is tackling an enemy so small that he can only
be seen through a microscope, it can’t be pre-
tended that she requires assistance, so the only
feasible excuse is that, when actual war comes
this way, it will be valuable to have a force
which has been seasoned by active service.
And to come in at the stale tail-end of a little
war against an irregular force of farmers, which
is the prospect just now, isn’t even seeing active
service on a very large scale. The goat-market
is weak.

Gordon’s Great Idea.
The wily idea of ex-Minister, ex-Democrat and
present Tory Gordon, that the S.A. Legisla-
tive Council should take a referendum of its
own constituents re the Council reform scheme
of the Kingston Government, is, after all, not
so wily as it looks. It has been hailed among
the Tory crowd as a master-stroke of policy,
but though it is rather a brilliant idea to
emanate from the Tory section of the House it
wouldu’t be especially brilliant coming from
anywhere else. In S.A. both Houses are elec-
tive, only they are elective in such a widely
different fashion that the Assembly represents
138,000 electors; the Council about 46,000.
The Household Suffrage Bill of the Kingston
Ministry aimed at widening the Council
Suffrage. When it had been passed by the
Assembly the Council scornfully carried it out
with the tongs and dropped it on the ash-heap,
giving as its chief reason that “ the people
had not seriously demanded any alteration
in the Council’s franchise,” and other good
old wheezes and groans to the same effect.
It was the argument that the Tory party has
always used, even when the air was thick with
the clamor of the people demanding reform.
But, in this case, it struck against the wrong
man. Kingston replied by taking a Referen-
dum on the Household Suffrage Bill at the
As embly elections, and the result showed that
the people demanded reform by 49,000 votes
against 33,000. By 16,000 majority the people
called the Council a liar in the matter of its
assertion that there was no popular demand for
reform. The Assembly passed the measure
again—and just here Gordon’s great inspira-
tion came in. He proposed that the Council
should take a referendum of its own propertied
constituents, and see if they were in favor of
dividing their authority with the mass of the
people ; and that the measure should be hung
up till the referendum w-as taken. The result
should be a very interesting gamble. If such a
referendum was taken, and the Council’s
handful of propertied constituents decided
that they were quite content to keep
the power in their own hands, and didn’t
see the necessity of letting anyone else share it,
the Council would be no further advanced. No-
body would seriously expect any other result.
If a vote is taken in any small privileged class
as to whether that class shall give up its special
privileges or not, the general result is that the
privileged class decides by a large majority
that it is quite satisfied with things as they are.
A vote of the British House of Lords as to
whether it should continue to be a hereditary
House of Lords, or a vote of the propertied
constituents of the Victorian House of 48 Rich
Landlords as to whether they should continue
to have 12 and 14 suffrages apiece, would
doubtless result in an expression of opinion that
nothing could be more admirable than the pre-
sent arrangement. Even if a poll were taken of
Kaiser William to decide whether the arrange-
ment whereby he is a very affluent German
emperor is quite satisfactory to himself,
that poll would probably close with one
unanimous vote to the" effect that Wil-
liam was quite satisfied to be an emperor.
So if the Council takes a referendum
of its own handful of propertied constituents,
and they say they are satisfied with things as
they are, it won’t strengthen the Council to
any appreciable extent, for the fact will still
remain that the mass of the people, as proved
by the Assembly’s n uch wider referendum, are
not at all satisfied. But if, as is not impossible in
democratic S.A., the Council’s own privileged
constituents gave a majority for Council reform
it will be an awful disaster to the House of
Fossils, and knock the ground completely front
beneath its feet. The trouble is that the
Council certainly doesn’t want to accept the
measure, yet if it rejects it again things will
have reached that stage at which the Governor
may dissolve both Houses and send them to the
country, and the obvious idea is to stave off
that woe a little longer. But it is quite
possible that at a general election, where all
the subjects of the day are mixed up together,
and there is no definite verdict on any one of
them, the Tory party would come off better
than if it obtained a vote on one clear-cut issue.
When the Tory party begins taking refer-
endums it is fooling with a dangerous weapon.

Interesting Discovery of a New Crime.
Vic. Crown Law authorities, assisted by that
province’s Penal Department, and backed by
the obsequious “ beak ” —of the common or
honorarium variety—has of late years invented
a fresh misdemeanor. ’Tis a pleasing offence,
and one calculated to give the utmost satisfac-
tion to the “authorities,” for it enables those
“ powers ” to deal summarily and persistently
with objectionable persons who fail to practise
Thrift and neglect to gain a Stake in the
Country. A recent offender was Mary
Watson, 42 years of age, brought before the
Coburg Bench on Sept. 22 charged with having
no dawful means of subsistence. Mary had
been originally ordered three months by the
City Bench for a similar offence, and before the
expiration of that term the Pentridge authori-
ties informed the police that she was Loo ill to
leave gaol. The prison doctor recommended a
further spell of three months in a cheerless
quod. Whereupon a servile and complaisant
Bench of J.’sP. kindly ordered her back IK)
gaol for six months ! It is hopeless to ex-
pect mercy or sympathy from the cold com-
inerciality of Melbourne, but surely the
common sense of that community must show
it that it costs less, financially and
morally, to keep a sick woman in a hospital
than in a gaol. The only plausible ex-
planation of Melbourne’s callous complaisance
at this weekly gaoling of its sick poor is that
it is a police-ridden community and simply
dares not object ; anil the only ostensible
reason for the Dept.’s acts is either that it must
fill its gaols somehow or other, or that it hopes
to atone for its neglect of the wealthy criminal
and the well-connected defaulter by its fre-
quent and persistent prosecution of the poor.
Even such an obtu e and venal affair as the
Vic. Crown Law Office cannot pretend that it
expects a prisoner to earn a means of subsistence
and acquire visible means of support while in
gaol. Moreover, it is only the poor who are
thus sent to gaol for the crime of ill-health ; the
influential person who can fee a big lawyer and
an expensive doctor to speak as to his want of
robustness while in confinement, is promptly
released on account of ill-health before his
term is completed. Sickness is a reason,
according to Victorian judical methods, why a
rich person should be let out of gaol lest his
health should suffer from confinement, and it is
also a reason why a poor woman should be sent
to gaol for six months more, lest she should
grow worse through want of confinement.

The People who haven’t Time to he Born.
In view of the scare which recently broke out
in Maoriland over the decline of the birth-rate,
some figures recently issued by N.S.W. Statis-
tician Coghlan are interesting. The Maori-
land birth-rate has declined so much in late
years that a spasm of alarm spread over the
country lest there should never really be any
Maoriland nation at all—lest the community
should start to die out almost before it had
fairly begun to exist, and perish through not
taking the trouble to be born. Yet, according
to Coghlan’s figures, Maoriland is better off in
this respect than some of its neighbors. These
are last year’s results
Births Deaths Increase Marriages
per per per - per
100,000 100,000 100,000 100,000
of of of of
Popula- Popula- Popula- Popula-
tion. tion. tion. tion.
Maoriland ... 2574 984 1590 691
Queensland ... 2834 1270 1564 605
N.S.W 2714 1248 1466 669
W.A. 3010 1646 1364 1014
Tasmania ... 2624 1352 1272 629
S. A 2455 1295 1160 608
Victoria 2566 1590 976 651
Maoriland comes out absolutely on top in the
most important respect. It has a higher birth-
rate than either Victoria or S.A., and it has
the highest marriage-rate of all, Westralia’s
only excepted. Also, it has by far the lowest
death-rate, therefore it is increasing faster in
population by the natural process of excess of
births over deaths than any other province ;
while Victoria, owing to its unaccountably
high death-rate, has the least increase
of any. Yet, on the whole, it would
seen that the Australian hasn’t time to be born.
On an average there are 2651 births annually
for every 100,000 people in Australia, as com-
pared with 3520 in Europe and 3150 in the
United States. And, on the other hand, he
hasn’t time to die, because he wants to go and
see the Melbourne Cup; in fact, he dies so
very seldom compared with the European that
the population of Australasia in general is in-
creasing every year by per cent, against
Europe’s average of a little over 1 per cent.
Under these circumstances it is possible that
the Australasian people may hang out for a
while longer, and have time to leave a No. 10
footprint in history before they go to join the
dead nations in the cemetery.
Byne and Leans.
Supposing the Lyne Government knows an
opportunity when it sees one, it will promptly
introduce a Bill declaring that only those
public works which yield a direct cash revenue
shall hereafter be chargeable to loans, and that
the whole mass of lion-direct-revenue-produc-
ing works (roads, bridges, gaols, asylums, river
improvements, harbor ditto, public offices,
schools, dredges, museums, &c., &c.) shall be
paid out of revenue. And it will make the
penalty for breach of this law anything short
of summary execution. If Lyne has discovered
what it was that struck him and Dibbs
in ’94, this will be one of his very first
public acts. The old Dibbs-Lyne Ministry
was a rather unusually honest one as N.S. W.
Ministries go. It paid out of revenue a
multitude of things which former Govern-
ments paid out of loans —therefore it had
to raise more revenue than its predecessors.
And Reid went up and down the country
raging like a small lion with an eyeglass, and
denouncing it as an extravagant Government
and an oppressor of the people, till he shunted
the Dibbs-Lyne Ministry out of office. Then
he won the great heart of the people by re-
ducing taxation and reducing expenditure;
and the. fact that he simply reduced expendi-
ture by charging more of the unproductive
works to loans quite escaped the notice of the
unthinking multitude. He was a mere political
fraud, but he was able to pose as a benefactor,
because the DjbbS-Lyne Government hadn’t
the sense to bind its successors by cast-iron Act
of Parliament to do as it did in the matter of
charging unproductive works to revenue.
Years before that the Dibbs-Jennings Govern-
ment made a similar mistake. It stopped the
old wholesale land - auctions, and thereby
stranded itself on a big deficit. But it hadn’t
the worldly wisdom, in its simple-minded
honesty, to stop laud-auctions permanently by
Act of Parliament, so Parkes came back to
power, and posed as the saviour of his country
by the easy process of selling his country’s
property wholesale to raise the wind. If the
last Dibbs-Lyne Ministry had imposed its com-
paratively-honest Public Works policy on its
successors by Act of Parliament then Reid
would not have held office for five years by
posing as the country’s great economist when
he was merely the country’s great loan-money
prodigal; he would either have had to publicly
announce his methods of economy by repealing
the law dealing with the application of loan
moneys, or he would have had to raise even
heavier taxation than his predecessors did.
The late Dibbs-Lyne Government lost a great
chance, and if Lyne loses his chance again he
deserves any kind of ill-luck that may befall
him.
