O Just Judg'e Gaunt !
A few days ago Judge Gaunt, of Melbourne,
was called upon, in the course of his ordinary
duties, to pass sentence on W. H. Buckley,
late chief clerk of Vic. Crown Law Department.
He decided that 1J years would meet the case.
Allowing for the absurdity of a system which
simply aims at breaking up its victims men-
tally wdiile putting them to no earthly profit,
the sentence of 18 months’ hard labor fora first
offender was perhaps sufficiently severe, but in
contrast with his Honor’s ferocious sentences in
other recent and contemporary cases, his alti-
tude was grotesque. Prisoner had been earn-
ing nearly £5OO a year, and his “ trouble ” was
brought on chiefly, if not wholly, by gambling.
There were three charges against him involving
a total of £lOOO, but the C.L. authorities with-
drew two on the first being proved. Before the
passing of sentence, the usual expensive lawyer
called sundry witnesses to character, who
all spoke highly of the now' weeping
prisoner, and proved that he was an in-
tensely honest man who had stolen a great
deal of money. Then the said expensive lawyer
put it to his Honor that there was no great
harm done, and the majesty of the law had
been vindicated, and that prisoner had suffered
keenly when he was put to the indignity of
being arrested (the indignity of being arrested
is good in a province w hich gaols its sick and
poor wholesale), and so forth, ad nauseam. At
this stage the court was one vast Ntobe, and
even the very walls sweated tears, and the iron
bars writhed in agony to such an extent that
the prisoner almost escaped from the dock.
Whereupon Gaunt remarked that “ the passing
of sentence in any case was always painful, but
it was a much more painful position for the
Judge when he had to pass sentence upon a
man whom he had known well.” He also pro-
ceeded to observe that “ though he pitied him
from the bottom of his heart he had a stern
duty to perform, and must administer even-
handed justice.” His Honor “ could not say
to a poor wretch in the dock, who had, per-
haps, taken money for his immediate needs,
‘ I shall pass a heavy sentence upon you,’ and
to another man like the prisoner, who was not
in immediate want, and took money merely
for the lust of gambling, say, * You will escape
lightly.’ It would be a monstrous doctrine if
that were follow'ed.” All which was very
pretty and quite the most ironical utterance
ever made from that Bench. For Buckley got
18 months for his large embezzlement, while
the case immediately preceding his was that of
a man who had clumsily stolen 100 files (value
under £10) from a city wharf. This man was
subject to epilepsy, had had a fit just previous
to liis appearance in the dock, and actually
appeared before this Judge with his head
swathed in bandages as a result thereof. And
did his Honor treat him as he treated Buck-
ley? Was he much pained at having to pass
sentence ? Scarcely ! In fact, not at all ! He
promptly ordered the epileptic who had stolen
less than £lO to be imprisoned for three years
and told him he was lucky to get off without
“ solitary ”on account of his epilepsy ! O just
Judge Gaunt!
“ Q.” to The Bulletin
The present slight stir in technical and other educa-
tional matters in Vic. reveals how little has been done
during Minister Peacock’s term. The younger boys have
been taught to sew, and the School Paper (srood in its
way, but hopelessly beyond the classes it is printed for)
has been started : what else has been done ? Certainly
the Dept, has been economised, but at what cost?
Buildings have got so out of repair that future mainten-
ance will be doubled ; child labor has multiplied, children
are taught by children little older than themselves;
sweating is rampant, there being a great prepon-
derance of pupil teachers over assistants (about 3
to 1), and the niGHEST-paid pupil teachers earn
the regal salary of—males 195., females 15s. a week.
Many of these p.t.’s have been teaching 12 and
15 years, and seeing no prospect of promotion, are
now agitating for a living wage. They have little hope of
getting it while Factories-Act Peacock remains at the
head of the Dept. “No money,” he says. The £50,000
proposed to be spent on the Transvaal picnic would give
all tirst-elass p.t.’s an additional £1 a week for five years.
If the wealthy loud-tongued Jingoes are in earnest about
that contingent, let them pay for it. If Peacock, the
sweateds’ professed champion, is really in earnest, let him
first suppress the sweating at home; let him get that
£50,C00, which has been admitted to beavailable (or some
other less amount), and sweep away the scandal of the
men and women who are guiding the intellectual life of
our children being paid half the wage of a coal-lumper or
gutter-scraper.
Premier Braddon was tricky to the last. On
the matter of sending a contingent of Tas.
warriors to the Transvaal it was held that in-
asmuch as the Acting-Governor had already,
at Braddon’s instigation, offered a Tas. con-
tingent to Chamberlain, Parliament could not
decently oppose the vote and go back on the
Governor. But the swindle was worked that
wav all round. In almost every province the
appeal to Parliament was : “ Some unauthor-
ised person has insolently usurped your
authority and promised this contingent and,
professed to commit the country to this ex-
pense, and we can’t go back on this promise
because it would make him feel bad.” And a
section of Parliament promptly responded :
“We are against the idea, but as the un-
authorised person has promised we must
assent.” And yet if the Tasmanian Acting-
Gov.,, instead of promising to spend good Tas.
money on a foolish contingent, had promised
to build the Great Western Railway as si
Government undertaking, which would have
been no worse a usurpation of authority, and
would have done some good to the country,
would Parliament have replied that it couldn’t
in honor go back on him, and his blatant
insolence in usurping Parliament’s prerogative
must be condoned and supported ? Not much !
It would more probably have thrown him out
of his billet and suggested that his state of
mind should be inquired into. The meanest
kind of groveller in the whole business was the
member who said that the contingent idea was
bad, and he wanted to vote'against it, but lie
would vote for it because its badness had been
made worse by the grossly illegal methods
taken to bring it about.

No less than four children, whose ages ranged
from six to nine years, were called in one
recent day as witnesses in Vic. law courts,
when the question of what amount of know-
ledge, or want of knowledge, is necessary
before a child is sworn got still further mixed.
One little fellow told Chief Justice Madden
that he knew Heaven to be a good place, and
Hell a bad one, and that if he told lies he
would go to the latter. When asked what
difference kissing the Bible would make to him
he was naturally floored—how many adult wit-
nesses would not have been floored by the
same query ? and admitted that he didn’t
know. Contrary to a previous decision of his
own, when he held that a child having some
knowledge of future rewards and punishments
was qualified to he sworn, Judge Madden this
time Said that he did not think the knowledge
sufficient. Another boy’s knowledge was
entirely limited to Hell ; he solemnly informed
the Court that good and bad people
alike went there. The Judge, who seemed
to recognise that he was taking part
in a howling farce, smilingly remarked that
the lad was very pessimistic and refused to
allow him to take the oath. Two other young-
sters happened along before Judge Casey, who
was most free-and-easy. The first was a boy
nine years of age. “Do you know what it is
to pray ?” asked Casey. “ No,” answered the
lad, “except at Sunday school.” “To whom
do you pray ?” said the judge. There was an
unpleasant pause, and then the boy replied,
“To Jesus.” The Judge allowed him to be
sworn on that knowledge. Then came a girl
seven years of age. She stated that she didn’t
know- what the Bible was, but most emphatic-
ally denied that it had anything to do with
God. “Do you pray ? ” queried J udge Casey.
“ I’m not allowed,” came the answer. It turned
out that she was the daughter of a Mohammedan
by an English mother. The mother was sitting
in court and when asked whether she objected
to the child being sworn on the Bible she
replied cheerfully, “Oh dear, no. She doesn’t
understand it; so there is no harm.” And yet
the Judge swore this child also. Truly the
whole business is a sham.

The N.S.W. Government is starting to collect
information about possible sites for the Federal
capital, so as to have it ready to lay before the
Commonwealth Parliament. Wanted, a region
on the N.S.W. table-land, with abundant pure
water to supply a future city of a million
people, over 100 miles from Sydney, healthy,
easily defensible, and with the very smallest
possible population so that the Federal Govern-
ment can buy up the freehold of all the private
land and become the sole ground landlord. A
cold place wanted where a hardy fighting race
can be produced ; if Australia is going to
make a new centre of population which will
one day muster 1,000,000 or more people, let
us have a million hardy, energetic snowballers
—not a million pallid, limp, sun-blasted
dwellers in the Turkish bath down by the
coast. If the Federal territory is down south
it should take in 5000 or 10,000 square miles of
country in the vicinity of Kosciusko; if it is
north it should take in a great stretch of
country somewhere about Ben Lomond.
Hobart Mercury grows ludicrous in defence
of Governors as they' Are, Federation or no
Federation :
The intense desire to reduce expenditure in connection
with the Governor’s establishment still continues to show
itself in the Assembly, a curious instance of selfish parsi-
mony, which says very little for the manner in which
those who are the prime movers in the affair appreciate
the value of the Imperial connection in past years. The
curious amount of feverish haste which is displayed in re-
gard to this one feature of possible federal change is the
more conspicuous because of its isolation, the abatement
of the vice-regal honors and dignity being apparently the
one fixed idea in certain minds, which allow all sorts of
variation in regard to other incidentals to the establish-
ment of the Commonwealth. Still, for all this persist-
ency in aiming at a lower salary for the Queen’s represen-
tative, there is no corresponding agreement as to the
way it is to be accomplished without disadvantage
to the colony, either in the character of the gentle-
man occupying the position, his ability to per-
form the responsible diplomatic functions of a
confidential character which devolve upon him, or in the
manner of his appointment, some members seeming to
imagine that an occupant of Government House will still
retain the kudos of the Imperial connection after he has
ceased to be an Imperial officer and become a colonial
one only. Some members are, of course, prepared to
face that. They seem quite reckless so long as they save
a thousand or two pounds. Others are more careful, awl-
are more desirous of keeping up the good old traditional
advantage of occasional fresh associations with the Old
World and its intellectual giants, which the periodical
changes at Government House ensure.
Just consider ponderous old Governor Gor-
manston in his aspect of intellectual
giant! “ Intellectual giants ” and “good old
traditional advantage of associations with the
Old World” are statements of which any
patriotic Tasmanian paper ought to be especially
proud. N.B.—A patriotic Tas. paper isn’t
necessarily a humorous paper.
A SMALL incident, which eventuated in Mel-
bourne the other day, shows the true value of
the sweating Tramway Co.’s oft-repeated asser-
tion that its employes are perfectly satisfied
with the present hours and wages, and their
treatment generally, and that it is only agita-
tors and sundry evil minded persons outside the
service'who desire the long-demanded 48 hours.
Recently, the co: placed in the men’s rooms,
at the suburban terminus of each line, a peti-
tion addressed to the several local councils,
urging said councils to giant the awful co. such
concessions as would enable it to give its em-
ployes, not the 48 hours to which they are
entitled —but “ shorter hours ” and “ improved
conditions,” whatever that may mean. These
petitions the men were invited to sign, if they
thought fit. Of course they thought fit !
Every man who knows his employer is watching-
him carefully, and expects him to do certain
things as the price of continued employment,
will think ht every time. But sundry coun-
cillors insinuated that the petitions weren’t
worth as much as a politician’s promise— which
is somewhat less than a curse—and, to
disprove this, the co. took a ballot on two lines
as to whether the men wotrld prefer the co.’s>

John Storm’s parishioner still hits him on
the skull at Her Majesty’s every night, and there
being no skilful surgeon at hand to amputate
John’s head before the wound mortifies, he dies
regularly. There is an uneasy sense in pious
Sydney circles of something immoral about
“The Christian,” and of late some person who is
disturbed in his sleep has written, complaining
that Parson John is alone with Glory very late in
her house, that night when he calls to murder her,
and when she overcomes his resolution by kissing
him and letting down her dazzling back hair. The
pietiest who advanced that theory calls for the
cordial scorn of everybody who can see through a
large hole. Of course the “ Christian ” is immoral,
in a sense, but it isn’t.in the ordinary woman sense.
The play is a yell of derision at the whole race of
preachers. It shows the fat, gentlemanly, worldly
pastor, who is of no use except to collect valuable
works of art and wave a large white jewelled hand
at a fashionable congregation. He is useless
enough in all conscience. Also, there is the pure-
minded father with a long black skirt and a bell-
rope round his waist, who moulders in the holy
calm of a cloister, and chants vespers, or whiskers,
or something equally irrelevant, and goes on his
slow, useless way to heaven, waving a fragrant
substance in a pot. He appears of very little
more value than the other kind. And the third
kind of preacher is John himself —the burning,
demented sort who sees visions and griffins, and
is regarded as a well-meaning kerbstone crank—
something a little lower than a prophet, and a
littie higher than a fruit-barrow. The last seen
of him lie is dying cm the pavement, and his con-
gregation, which threw the fatal brick at him, is
slinking away round the corner to bury its con-
fused feelings in beer. As for the rich worldly
parson, he continues to collect works of art and to
wave a white be-tliamonded hand at his fashion-
able congregation. And the other variety of
father is left mouldering in his cloister—presum-
ably he perishes in the end of dry-rot. And all
three fathers are a failure and a disappointment.
As a satire ou the father business this paper loves
“The Christian,” but when the uneasy pietist
coni plains that it teaches a doubtful lesson because
Glory lets down her back hair late at night, he
doesn’t touch even the outermost fringe of the
trouble. If Glory had no hair at all “ The
Christian ” would be just as bitter a satire on the
church as it is now.
** * *
Johnny Sheridan’s frivolous mixed joke in
four acts is doing respectable business at the
Lyceum. The heavy parts of it are certainly very
heavy, but to make up for that the light parts
woigh less than a small elephant, and there
are a good many of them. The plot, where there
is one, calls for no respect in particular. Inman
explains with much beer that he was once happy
and had a wife and child, but his faithless
friend deposited the proceeds of a robbery in bis
house, and ho was consigned to the cold harrowing
gaol for seven years. When he came out his wife
and child had vanished, so he took to drink and
joined Sheridan’s travelling comedy co. Two
stories flow on side by side as it were. The
harrowed father wades through depths of grief in
hunting for his lost wife and child, and clearing
his character from suspicious of murder, and
burrowing in cellars in quest of rum and clues;
and meanwhile John’s burlesque show struggles
cheerfully after a crust for itself and slavey
Semolina. The stories only get mixed when the
comedy co. disguises itself as an apple-woman, or
a small boy, or an intoxicated Dutchman, to rescue
somebody out of a cellar, or proves somebody’s
innocence by being an Italian with a guitar.
John’s cheery willingness to be any kind of disguised
foreigner if lie can thereby help a fellow creature
on his path, and Miss Gracie Whituford’s sunny
hair, and Leon Roche’s burglar, and slavey Semo-
lina all inspire respect, and the heavy parts of the
show, if they do nothing else, come in useful as
ballast.

The “Dreyfus Case,” declared to be by
Walter Bentley and George Rignold, is ex-
pected at Sydney Criterion on Saturday. The
pity is that the said case, which was very much
alive only a month ago, is now alxmt as dead as
anything so recently alive can be. Dreyfus is
hardly alluded to now in the cablegrams and
hardly one person in ten remembers that Estorliazy
ever was. Big Oom Paul came along with his
gun, and the Dreyfus ease crawled through the
first hole and dried up. If anyone could produce
a really good Oom Paul drama just now he might
fill a want, but Dreyfus looks a rather dubious
speculation.

There is a new basso at the Palace in the
person of Arthur Hahn, who booms “ Asleep in
the Deep,” and booms it very well to the accom-
paniment of much encore. Paul Pelham gives a
startling something described on the programme
as the “Transvaal War Song.” Paul doesn’t sing
much, but he makes up for that unfortunate
deficiency by expanding his chest, tilting his
chin heavenwards, and waving his hat
wildly. And he staggers about howling forth
paragraphs re the indignities that the Boers
have heaped on unarmed men, and infants in
arms, and defenceless women, and declares
that the Afrikanders would not be satisfied
with anything but sanguinary war, and, by
George, they have got it now. And his song is
full of the inartistic word “job” which would
wreck Milton’s “Hymn to the Nativity.” When
he comes to the chorus, “Then up with the good
old Union Jack, and down with Kruger and his
Boers,” he grips a flag from an invisible attendant
and flourishes it wildly. On Monday night the flag
slipped from his hands and fell over the footlights,
and the circle laughed derisively, and Paul nearly
fell down into the orchestra in his struggles to get
the Union Jack up again. He makes it revolve
rapidly till it is unfurled, and then the gallery
jingoes take a turn and yell for Berlud. And
Paul flourishes the meteor flag of England some
more, and the gods howl with him, but altogether
it is a jingo demonstration that splutters away
miserably. When Paul has gone off to kill Kruger
in the wings, the theatre starts to fill up till every
seat is occupied ; for the Marvel is coming. And
when he does come, Kruger and berlud are for-
gotten in contemplation of the nimble cigar, and
the rotative billiard ball that resteth never, and
the match that lights itself and the cigar and the
candle all on its own.

At the Bijou things are much the same as
last week, with the exception of three new “turns”
by Tom Costello, introducing some quaint leg-
humor, and a rather appalling make-up. Thomas’s
picture of a sexagenarian ballet-girl, with many
fissures and sinkages in her red enamelled bust—
the sort of wrecky Maudie who has to sweep the
bread-crumbs from her neck indentures after
dining—is an impressive nightmare effect that
haunts one all the way home.

This* being the appointed period for Mel-
bourne’s annual horse fever, Fitzgerald Brothers’
Circus is about to break out again at the old spot
in Flinders-street. Sundry new acrobats loom
large on the bill, also the lion perched upon the
elephant’s back will be an own brother to the
noble animal which destroyed the apocryphal
Unicorn and is just now enjoying an anxious time
among the Boers. The outlook is decidedly pro-
mising for the Fitz’s show. The brethren, it
seems, have improved the warlike hour by offering
12 horses to the Vic. Contingent. Not ordinary
clmckle-headed ’orses, but carefully educated
steeds. They will be taught in the circus to snort
with disdain at any mention of Oom Paul; they
will stand under fire, and lie down if necessary
without waiting to be shot, and if, perchance,
they should be required to fill lonely graves in
South Africa these Fitzgeraldian chargers will
feel glad to think they died lighting for the Hem-
pire in general, and particularly that section of the
Hempire which bears the name of dear old Fling-
land’s Queen. Rats!
The maddest melo-drama ever put upon the stage
Seems to thrive at Melbourne Royal, though it
isn’t “all the rage.”
The small boys hoot the villain as he wallows in
his guilt,
And perhaps it doesn’t matter how a narrative is
built,
If you give the gods slow music when berlud is
being spilt.
There’s bigamy and murder, likewise arson, in
the plot;
There’s a gang of silver coiners blown to pieces
on a yacht;
There’s a marvellous detective with no better end
in life
Than the “hunting down” of johnnies who were
married to his wife.
The hero, heaven help him! has the bloodhounds
on his track,
And the drama takes some time to prove the
hm-o-ccnce of Jack.
* * *
There’s an incidental life-boat which is launched
on wooden surf,
Whence it gallops ten times faster than a gee-
gee on the turf,
And when the leading lady is reported to be lost,
She pops up from the bottom of that life-boat
like a ghost ;
So the villain still pursues her with unscrupulous
desire,
He shuts her in a garret and he sets the house
afire
Because he loves her madly, and she favors not
his suit
(For the disappointed masher is an inconsistent
brute);
And all this time the matron weeps, and maidens
sigh “ Alack !
Will no one speak a word to prove the Inn-o-cence
of Jack i ”

The noble - hearted father of a girl who went
astray
Gives the hero up to justice in an idiotic way,
Which provides the leading lady with another
chance to weep,
Whilst a boozer on a door-step dreams that Susan
shares his sleep.
Then the villain and the ’tective having vengeances
in view,
Hope to get the hero slaughtered for the deed
he didn’t do.
The stray girl meets the villain, and she shudders,
as from cold,
For she knew him as “ the Vulture ” in her giddy
days of old,
Yet this weird repentant person, clad in melan-
holy black,
Regrets to find she cannot 'prove the Inn-o-cence
of Jack.
* * * *
Then the drama goes to prison where the curdled
traitor sneers
At the thought of all the evil he’s been working
at for years.
The ’tective, too, looks happy—his revenge is
drawing nigh,
But the hero shakes his shackles and is not afraid
to die.
Now, the villain is persuaded to assume a convict
dress,
And impersonate a Teddy, who has something to
confess;
And when the false confession has officially been
read.
Why, they send the silly villain to the guillotine—
for Ted.
His head’s cut off in error, but they cannot put it
hack,
And that is how Bill Adams proves the Inn-o-cence
oj Jack.

knew him 20 years ago as ttie husband of handsome Miss
Kaie Chard, the original Boccacio in this country.
Brand and his wife were devoted to one another, like
the Daores, and they had much the same luck as that ill-
fated pair whenever they ventured into management.

The Braddon Blot—Miles.
Some people have inferred from a Bul-
letin par. of Sept. 2nd, that the lady who beat
a well-known financier with her brolly outside
Elsternwiek (Melb.) railway-station was Mrs.
Joseph Flamufan. The inference is wrong. It
was another lady who gave the ill-fated citizen
“ beans.”
Stated that the Pope sent a telegram to Pre-
sident Kruger advising him to “ avert the horrors
of war,” by accepting the British Government’s
terms, and pointing out that the B.G. carries too
many guns for him. The Pope’s telegram, though,
had been preceded by a private message from the
Almighty, advising Kruger, so he said, to rush
into the horrors of war, because be was bound to
win. Which goes to explain the Yes-No ness of
the old gentleman’s policy what time those rein-
forcements were arriving from India.
Attorney-General Rutledge, ofQ., is the
boss jingo of Australia. During the debate on
Dickson’s offer of troops Rutledge referred to the
Premier of Cape Colony as a “ traitor,” and bel-
lowed for Boer blood with the zest of a thirsty
cannibal. The Attorney-General was once the
occupant of a Wesleyan pulpit, which, perhaps
accounts for his belief in gore. Anyhow, Rut-
ledge is a big, strong man, quite superfluous in
Mongrelia, and be will lie taken for a large, blus-
tering braggart unless lie himself Lears a hand in
the actual fighting. The jingoistic fighter is a
fanatic ; the fire side jingo a coward.
Norman Cameron, M.P. for Deloraiue in
Tas. Assembly, stood alone for weeks demanding
an inquiry into Btrahan Marine Board affairs,
and eventually won the fight by sheer persistency.
Very naturally Cameron wanted to move the vote
of want-of eonfidence against the Braddon Minis-
try, and actually put the motion on the notice
piper. This meant a victory for the Govt., as
not more than half-a-dozen Oppositionists would
have been found following the comparatively un-
known and vitriolic member in a divis'on. Only
at the last moment could Cameron be induced to
stand aside and allow the regula.rOpposition leader,
Bird, to do the trick. But Cameron will yet be
heard of in big politics.
Ex-Premier Reid complained savagely at
the recent Town-hall demonstration about Premier
Lyne’s lack of velocity in his growl, bacau.se he
waited to ask the authority of Parliament before
spending the public money on getting ready an
African contingent. Reid himself has just been
thrown out for spending public money without
Parliamentary authority, and his cry that the
province has lost its supremacy in Australia
because his successor didn't do the same, and get
thrown out also, is a very shallow, not to
say idiotic, piece of scheming. The weakly wily
one says that “ any tnan who could read within an
inch of his nose ought to know that the whole
Parliament would hold him harmless if lie had
maintained the leadership of Australia in his own
hands ”—in other words, if lie was the fiist hog to
rush down a steep place into the sea. Of course
the emphatic statement of the Labor-party has
already proved that the whole Parliament
wouldn’t hold Lyne harmless. Reid’s shriek of
rage because his antagonist had sense enough to
keep out of so visible a trap is pathetic.
Already one cabled “ Boer atrocity ” —the
wrecking of a train carrying many women and
children —has proved to be a lie. The story
about limv the Dutchmen spat on female refugees,
and ill-used their children, and how the trains
loaded with women and children were detained
for many hours through pure malice at way-side
stations, till people in them actually died from
exposure and weariness, will probably prove to be
mostly lies also. No doubt the trains were
delayed amid the general rush and chaos
and break down, lmt the rest of the yarn is too
improbable. General Colley, afterwards killed at
Majuba, wrote that, in all his knowledge of war,
there had never been such a display of humanity
and respect for the people of an invaded territory
shown by an irregular force as was displayed by
the Boers who repulsed him at Laing’s N k, and
afterwards scoured the country around Newcastle.
They molested no non-combatants, and paid for
everything they took. The Boers had their faults
two of them very baa ones. They were never
able to recognise the hospital train, and seeirn d
to regard the Red Cross flag— 'which they had pro-
bably never seen before—as a British flag, and
always fired at it, and they were guilty of using
white flags for decoy purposes; but they always
confined those unpleasant practices to the fighting
force, and their conduct towards women and chil-
dren and unarmed men was of the gentlest.
Certainly few—very few—civilised nations would
have been so gentle in their treatment of the
Jamieson raiders, every one of whom by the law
of nat ons was liable to the death penalty. But in
war time the lying horror-monger is always abroad.
It is just lot years since the last fight between
England and Scotland, and though these two
countries were close enough to let the people really
know each other, the charges brought by the
English against the Scots invaders included
cannibalism, drinking the blood of their enemies,
feeding their dogs fin the flesh of the English
prisoners, and such-like trifles. And the special
correspondent’s gentle art has expanded a great
deal since then.

Gov. Jerry Smith (W. A.) must now be rather
ashamed of his connection with Darlington Simpson and
the Peak Hill co. In W.A. Leg. Council some strong
comments were made about the undignified attitude oc-
cupied by the Queen’s representative in this matter.
The action of H.E. in writing a fulsome letter to Simpson
“ with the object of obliging a lot of adventurers” was
condemned in severe terms by Stone, brother of the judge
of that name. One tiling escaped the notice of members.
Only a couple of years since a motion of censure was
almost carried against Minister of Mines YVittenoom for
having allowed one of his mining inspectors to report on,
the Peak Hill property for the sake of assisting Darlington
Simpson to float the co. in London. Frank Reed, the
inspector in question, promptly resigned—and was ap-
pointed manager for the co. at £2OOO a year, besides being
presented with shares. To get the Governor of the
province to undertake a long journey to Peak Hill, and
(lien crack up the property, was no doubt clever on.
Simpson’s part, but the attempt to permanently boost lip'
the shares has been a failure. The last crushing,
it is openly known, was the smallest since the co. was
formed, despite the extra 30 head of stamps. Looks like
another fizzle for the Murchison.
* * * •
When “Toppy” Longden, who (much to the,
chagrin of the Darlington Simpson Co.) secured Tom the
Rager’s interest in Peak Hill property, visited his pur-
chase, he found the shaft had been filled with mullock.
He got from the Warden, after some demur, an order to
descend the main shaft and inspect his share of the show,-
hut the co.’s manager refused to recognise it, and politely
told “ Toppy ” that he could go to the Transvaal. There
was but one constable in the place, so recourse to com-
pulsion was out of the question, and “Toppy,” who had
taken up a London financial magnate and some mining
experts co report, was forced to return. Peak Hill is
about 80(1 miles from Perth, and even at the best not a
pleasant journey. However, “Toppy” says if Darling-,
ton Simpson wants his share, of the property—in which,
by the way, all the richest stuff is said to exist—he will
have to pay through the nose. Tom the ltager got
£15,000 —Longden’s price is now £150,000. Simpson was
in the same steamer that Longden sailed in.

Every day, and every week, for nearly two
months the fall in Westralian stocks has been attributed
to the Transvaal i rouble. A reason and an excuse had to
he found somewhere; a big, all-round slump had
occurred ; a war was threatened in South Africa, so it
had to bear the brunt of the fall. Five weeks ago the
fall in Westralians in London, taking it in round num-
bers, was quite equal to the fall that took place in the
stocks of the leading Rand mines. Now, suppose the im-
pending war had been at Hannan’s instead of Johannes-
burg, is it reasonable to imagine that the stocks of the
latter p'ace would have equally suffered on account of it?
It also happened that on the day war was declared in S.
Africa a rise in Westralian stocks occurred. A reason for
this had to he found, and the same newspapers said it
was all on account of the worst being known—by which
it is reasoned that stocks fall with a threatened war and
rise with an actual one. The true reason has never been
given. It is that shares bought before the boom were
sold when it was raging, for the sake of the profits.
What occurred last month will occur again and again,
war or no war. It happened at the beginning of the year
and will happen again before the end of it. Last October
there was a much bigger slump in We-tralians, and as
there was no war threatening it was all on account of
“ bears.”

There are now at least 1000 men knocking about
Greenbushes (W.A.) tinfields. Tin has been got over
10 sq. miles ; but not much has been exposed—the over-
burden is heavy and the forest dense. Old hands, like
Capt. Hancock, of S.A., will not talk much till lodes have
been found. Certainly, formations have been opened up
a little, hut the result to date is not too cheerful. Water
plentiful; hut it needs conserving.

The Tasmania Co., Beaconsfield, has had
another good crushing; 2170 tons yielded 27120 z. gold
valued at £10,499. This oo.’s reduction plant is most
complete. The Luhrig system of concentration (vanners,
etc.) was introduced nearly five years ago, and as an
adjunct to the 65 head of st-mps continues to save large
quantities of valuable pyrites. Full particulars of Luhrig
machinery may be obtained from the General Agents, W.
and J. Lempriere, Melbourne and Broken Hill. An ex-
perimental plant is erected near Melbourne for treating
small parcels from two to five tons.*

TRANSVAAL CRISTS.
Six weeks ago, in the interests of nervous South African
shareholders in this country, 1 published a notification in
the press to the effect that there would not be “ war.” A
few days subsequently the Right Hon. Cecil Rhodes con-

again notify that the dispute has been practically
arranged, and the draft terms of settlement are in the
hands of Her Majesty s official representative in Pretoria.
WM. F. REGAN.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Both in the Transvaal and in Wrstralia (the
two trreat gold boom countries of the world) the revenue
reached about tbe top in 1896. In that yearWestralia
extracted from its people at the rate of ±19 lis. fid. per
inhabitant (aborigines included), or something: between
£‘20 and 1 21 per white inhabitant. The Transvaal Govt,
raised about £5 10s. per inhabitant, or ±'19 12s. per white
inhabitant. But the Transvaal goldfields produced that
year nearly eight times as much gold as those of W.A.,
and if taxation had been as heavy in the Transvaal as in
Westralia, the revenue would have been a good deal
larger than it was. apropos, in the same year the white
population of tbe British colony of Natal was about
50,000, and the revenue was ±1,457,338 —over ±29 per
white inhabitant. The “ oppressive taxation ” of the
Transvaal is doubtless heavy enough, but the Briton can
show quite as bad records himself.
* • * *
Concerning the awfulness of Boer taxation of
mines, owned wholly hy aliens. On tin, raised almost
wholly by aliens, the Rritish Govt, of the Straits collected
an export tax of 1,108,978 dollars during the first six
months of this year.
* • • •*
Sydney municipality having piled up an over-
draft not much short of ±150,010, which costs 5 per cent,
interest, it is proposed to float a permanent loan of
£150,000 at 31 per cent, in order to pay it off, and thus
save about £2000 a year interest. And so the good old
circle goes round. In the first place, the overdraft
accumulates because the aldermen haven't the courage to
levy a rate that will meet necessary outgoings, hut con-
solation is found in the fact that it isn’t a permanent
debt. Then when it reaches a great size, it is converted
into permanent debt, and consolation is found in the fact
that it costs less for interest that way. This will make
the net debt, after deduction of all sinking funds, about
H,24(1,000 —equal to nearly seven years' revenue. Mel-
bourne owes about £1,170,000, equal to 6£ years’ revenue ;
Adelaide, £76,900, or about l| years’ revenue ; Brisbane,
1305,000, or five years’ revenue ; Wellington, £667,000, or
eight years’ revenue, hut this includes expenditure on the
city water-works, which are corporation property. Still,
barring Adelaide, there isn’t one of the five whose sol-
vency is anything to boast about. The worst feature
about Sydney is that it is falling into abject disre-
pair, and the debt and the average size of the holes in
George-street keep increasing at about the same ratio.
* * * «
The Premier Permanent Building Society (Mel-
bourne), after nine years’ liquidation, has cleared off all
the multitude of mortgages, liens, and overdrafts which
encumbered its assets, and is paving depositors a first
dividend of lid. in the £. When it suspended, there was
hardly even an ink-bottle that hadn't a lien on it, and all
these had to lie got rid of. Then a multitude of its assets
consisted of unfinished houses—whole streets of them—
and it was necessary to find the money to put roofs on
these to make them remunerative properties. After the
smash, the smartest Jews in Melbourne were paying 14s.
to 16s. in the £ for Premier Permanent deposits. They
are worth about 2s. 9d. now.
* » • *
The Royal Bank (Melbourne) shows a profit of
£4624 for the half-year ended 30th Sept., 1899. This is
the record for over six years past, previous profits having
been as follows :—
September, 1892 .. £8,261 September, 1896 .. £2,044
March, 1893 .. 2,510 March, 1897 .. 3,398
September, 1893 .. 1,901 September, 1897 .. 3,625
March, 1894 .. 3,258 March, 1898 .. 3,864
September, 1894 .. 2,875 September, 1898 .. 4,088
March, 1895 .. 2,136 March, 1899 .. 4,314
September, 1895 .. 2,038 September, 1899 .. 4,624
March, 1896 .. 2,099
The profits are equal to £5 19s. 4d. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds (capital, £150,000; reserve
fund, £5000), so the Royal is doing remarkably well, and
the improvement which set in at the beginning of 1897 is
being well maintained. A dividend at the rate of 3 per
cent, ppr annum (£2250) is declared, and the remain-
ing £2374, with the £3011 brought forward from
previous half-year, leaves ±6415 at credit of profit and
loss. The business shows a further improvement all
round. During the half-year, note-circulation has in-
creased from ±5223 to £6882, anti deposits fro n £321,801
to £368,217. Cash on hand is £114,167—equal to over 27
percent, on all liabilities to the public, which is a ve y
solid reserve. Altogether, the institution is doing a ra id
and healthy revival.
The Royal Bank of Melbourne started business
in 1888, so it was only five years old when the smash fell
upon it. It is now in its eleventh year. The shareholders
had 3| years’ dividends at 6 per cent., dividend
at 5 per cent,, 3 years’ dividends at 3 ~per cent., on a
capital which had been written-down one-half, or L)
per cent, on the amount originally paid ; and four years
with no dividends at all. So, on an average, they have
received 12 11s. per cent, per annum on their original
capital. And as they have lost £150,000, or just half their
capital, they have really got no dividends at all, and
have, in addition, lost about £55,000 of their own coin.
In view of these things, and considering that, the next
crisis will come along in due course—the next crisis is as
reliable as the next sunrise—the Roval directors should
resolve firmly to adhere to their present cautious svstem
and be better prepared for the coming smash than they
were for the last one. When some of the old hanks had
to write-off I heir capital and reserves, the shareholders
had the barren consolation of knowing that they were
only giving hack what they had previously received in
dividends. The new banks, like the Royal and the North
Queensland, hadn’t even that satisfaction. They arrived
on the scene just in time to pay up without having the
joy of joining in the game.
* * * *
A Westralian jury has given a verdict of £5762
and costs against Governor Jerry Smith and others. The
Bank of New South Wales was the plaintiff, and the whole
affair is a hideously bad advt, for the tactless Smith.
That potentate became a large shareholder in the Aus-
tralia Hotel, Ooolgardie, which was a rash proceeding to
begin with. His A.D.C. and secretary were also in it.
When the hotel fell into difficulties, which it very shortly
did, a fine large overdraft was contracted, Gov. Smith
and others being guarantors ; and when it broke up, and
the hank sold off its securities for very much less than
their nominal value, and requested the guarantors to pay
up the difference. Smith and co. refused. By so doing
they dragged their lucklessdrink-emporinm into the glare
of unpleasant publicity. Their plea was that the hank had,
by carelessness or incapacity, sold their securities far
below their value, while the hank’s plea was that property
in Ooolgardie is now worth only a quarter of its value of
two years ago, and that this was tile sole cause of the
deficit which it required the vice-regal Smith and his
friends to make good. Other people at who
have property to sell are so ]>l eased at tliis exposure of the
town’s decadence that they would gladly eat Smith raw
So lhe ill-directed Smith has to pay in full, plus the law-
costs and the publicity. The worst feature of the latter
was a letter in which Smith spoke in a tone of glad sur-
prise about the excellence of his own hotel and the mag-
nificent profits it was making. The said letter, it is
alleged, was used in an endeavour to pursuade other
people to take shares in the lamentable emporium, and
when these people learned that it was in part his own
hotel that Smith was so pleased and surprised about, they
said—well, it wasn’t so much what they said as the nasty
way they said it.

Broken Hill had its biggest-on-record gathering
of mine-directors to meet Gov. Beauchamp ; and, as
usual, there was much after-dinner talk of Govt, neglect.
And then Cann, one of the local Labor Ms.P., had his
chance. “It is true, gentlemen,” he said, “that the State
has done little for this district. But when 1 and my col-
leagues sought to induce the Govt, to acquire the Silver-
ton railway line, did you help us? It is true that the
State has neglected the town. But when I and my col-
leagues have striven to induce the State to take over the
waterworks or provide others, have you helped us?”
And so on. It was said so plea ant ly. too, that the vic-
tims had to smile even while they squirmed.
* * * «
So terrific have several recent duststorms bpen
at Broken Hill, that not only have all outdoor surface
works been suspended on the mines, hut the concentra-
ting mills have been thrown idle. Moving belts became
displaced, and the maintenance of the necessary steady,
unruffled flow of water was out of the question.
• * * »
Tim dust-storms, or grafting in tbe open-cuts,
or something, knocks most of the poetry out of the
Broken Hill man. So, while men in other places con-
sidered which of their acquaintances they could best-
spa re to “ fight for the flag,” Broken Hill was moved
most bv the announcement that Rand stocks had fa'len
£70.0110,000 ; and a resolution was unanimously adopted,
at a meeting called to consider the crisis, that a syndicate
be formed to lay in so re of those knocked-out shares.

Lord Beauchamp has been to White Cliffs, the
first N.S.W. Gov. to penetrate to the opal fields. But it
isn’t an impressive place. Though an average of about
700 men are employed, mostly “on their own” and
trihuting, no real mining has yet been done—mere pig-
rooting down to 30ft. or 40ft. The gem is found anyhow,
and at any depth ; I here are none of those “indications”
which the mining “ expert ” so loves to talk about in his
reports. When you’ve got it you’ve got it, and that’s ail
there is to say about the matter so far. Lately there has
been a slump in price of the gem-stone ; but the market
has been steadied again by the announcement that the
co. —an English concern which has got hold of many of
the best leases —is now a buyer of stone.

“ Akaroa ” : Your correspondent rc Waihi
crushing is a little “ off ” ; as I have recently returned
from the mine, perhaps I can put him right. At present
all the crushing is dry both at the mine—where are 90
heads at work—and at Waikeno, live miles away, where
the ore is run down from mine by light railway. At
Waikeno 100 bead of stamps are driven by water-power,
another 100 heads are in course of erection. The ore is
kiln-dried, dry-crushed, and transmitted from battery to
cyanide vats by means of spiral travellers. The vats, big
enough for small circuses, hold 150 tons each. All the
gold is now won by cyanidution, but it has been decided,
when the new hundred heads are completed, to revert to
the wet process, as, although percentage of extraction
will not be so high, the fact of putting through nearly
double the quantity of ore will amply compensate.

* • • •
The N.Q. copper-boom will probably prove
disastrous to the wages miner in that locality. Up to
now, the recognised miner’s wage in Etheridge, Croydon,
and Cloncurry districts has been 43 10s. for a week of 44
hours; now the Hampden Co., in Cloncurry district, and
the Einasleigh syndicate near Georgetown offer Bs. per
day for surface men and 9s. below, working 48 hours per
week. Though, at latest, the Hampden men were out on
strike, both co.’s will probably carry their point, and that
will mean the beginning of a big- slump in miners’ wages
all over the North. Considering the cost of living up
there the old rate of wages was none too much, and what
with the disadvantages of climate, &c., the Southern
miners who are now rushing the Land of the Crocodile
will not find it all beer and skittles.
Melb. Aruup special says tliat general manager
Stewart sets down the ore available on the Chilla-
goe Railway and Mining Co.’s properties (without
counling anything below 100 ft.) as 500,000 tons,
worih 10 per cent, copper on an average. “ Without
attempting to state the quantity of ore in sight, I fully
believe that the carbonate ores alone showing in the sur-
face workings of the former co., and the deposits dis-
covered but yet unworked, must sum up a very respect-
able total, sufficient to justify the erection of up-to-date
reduction works. As to the productive capacity of these
deposits at deeper levels, only a thorough system of ex-
ploration and the future can tell. The Q. geologists have
pointed out that as- the ore deposits about Chillagoe
mostly occur at or near the junction of the limestones
and granite country, the geological inference is that, they
are not true fissure lodes, but only ‘deposits.’ But, so
far, data is not available on which to base a positive
opinion as to whether the deposits go down or die out
within shallow limits.”
* * » *
Editor Bulletin. —Your par. re Hampden
copper-mine from “ A Man on the Spot” is somewhat in-
correct. In the first place, there are only five shafts sunk
on the lease of 160 acres, but there are several open-cuts
which show large bodies of ore. Secondly, 1 am
sure that “A Man on the Spot” has never been
down any of the shafts, but is simply going
on hearsay. Any man with ordinary knowledge
of mining can see that the ore-body on the
surface is disturbed and broken, and must be cut
through at a shallow depth, hut there are any number of
leaders going down from the break, which shows that the
ore-body will make again at a depth, hut not anything
like the size it is on the surface, lie. the sharps, syndi-
cate-agents, &ci The men who stick four pegs in the
ground, apply for a lease and six months’ exemption at
the same time, and then sit down and wait forsomeone to
come along and give them cash and a big paid-up interest
in something that has never had a pick put in it, struck
me as the (would-be) sharps, and I think their hitter ex-
periences with syndicate agents amount to the fact that
they have overreached representatives on one or two oc-
casions.—-A Late Visitor to Cloxcurrry.

Vic. Govt, seems determined to resuscitate the
dead-and-gone Mt. JJeddick alleged mines. A surveyor is
marking out a line, and a heap of public money will be
spent in making a road to the Deddick mountain top. So
far as can be seen, it will serve no other purpose than to
advertise the “mines” and enable some influential
boomers to float new companies and unload their old
shares, on the strength of a few casual erratic streaks of
galena that have been adversely reported on by Govt,
geologists, and unreservedly condemned by Govt. Metal-
lurgist Jenkins.

South German (Maldon) shares look very much
as though the beastly Bulletin’ alarmist, whose story
started them on the down-grade, wasn't making a mis-
take. Melb. Argus, which published a reassuring report
when the stock was first beginning to drop, spoke last
week of “ the uneasiness felt” among S.G. holders. The
Age of Oct. 13, carefully ignoring the fact that it took its
cue from this paper, announced that—“ln order to get
at the truth and, if possible, allay the panic, we applied
nearly a month ago” (Tub Bulletin alarm was issued
Sept.l4) “ to the chairman of directors, Mr. W. B. Gray,
for authority to allow our mining reporter to inspect the
South German mine.” As the application was not joy-
fully granted, the irate organ (on Del. 13) “determined
to expose the secret methods” of the S.G. management.

div. declared, absorbing £100,600. Many shareholders
would like to know how much has been absorbed in other
directions. It is suspected that much Associated money
has gone into places from which it is not easily recover-
able—a good deal of it into schemes of share-rigger
Bottomiev. It is now proposed to separate the Iron
Duke, Iron Monarch, and the two Menindie leases, and
set them up in business for themselves as “the Asso-
ciatecl Northern Blocks," with a capital of £350,000 —
working capital, £lOO,OOO. Shareholders to receive one
for every two held. The present co. has long waited an
opportunity to do this, which came t’other day with a dis-
covery of good ore in the Iron Duke.

Now that the N.S.W. Public School-
teachers’garden-party is over, it will be interest-
ing to note what other branches of the public ser-
vice receive wholesale Guvmentouse invitations.
Drawing the line will be a ticklish point. Many
of the higher-grade teachers, by the way, accepted
the invitation as a matter of etiquette, and re-
mained at home as a matter of choice —and
economy; and some made a point of replying, not,
as requested, to the A.D.C., but through the
Minister from whose department their invitations
came.

behind that of N.S.W., so, as neither H.E. nor

young man found the dusty city in a general state
of unpreparedness.

At one place in the Never Never where

“ They’re all out, mum, ’cept the blue billy, and
’e’s safe under the bunk.”

corporation. That was certainly a long time ago
—l6BB. The remarkable part of the latter case is
that on every Lord Mayor’s Day for nearly 150
years the town crier bawled out Creagh's name,
and demanded that he should come forth and
the Lord Mayor’s chain.

A N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge not long
ago complained bitterly of the noise during the
night in two country hotels at which he put up
while on circuit. He believe-; at one house the
people didn’t go to bed at all. Oaths and the
clinking of glasses and beery songs went on
till morning. Alfred Stephen, when Chief-
Justice, wholly lost temper at 2 a.m., jumped
out of bed, ran into the pub. bar, and called
on his trooper bodyguard to clear out the
boisterous revellers. The boss pubman
turned on the C. J. and told him to go back
to bed ; he was licensee of that shop, and
no one had legal power to clear the place
without his permission. The C. J., angry
as he was, realised that the pubman was
right on the legal point involved, and
quietly returned to bed. Stephen was
fond of telling the story after.

Queensland boasts some dozens of
factories wherein white women and girls
are employed by Asiatic aliens. What
style of men do they raise in some parts
of Bananaland ? Just fancy a father send-
ing out his girl to slave for a few shillings
in a Chow shop or a Syrian factory!
Though, after all, the heathen are probably
little worse than numbers of European em-
ployers who engage girls to work 12 months
for nothing, 12 months at ss. a week, and then—
the sack.

• » * *
S. M. Herald discourses on the Transvaal
State’s artillery:—
“To suppose,” says a recent writer on the subject,
“that the Boer artillery, with its inexperienced officers
and its nondescript detachments, could maintain a duel
with the batteries of a regular army, is tantamount to
declaring that any rabble provided with arms of precision
is a match for disciplined soldiers.” The boasted artil-
lery of the Boers is more than likely to be the chief cause
of their downfall.
Then it whoops wildly for sending to the Trans-
vaal N.S. Wales’ solitary field battery “ with its
inexperienced officers and its nondescript detach-
ments ” who have had so little practical train-
ing that it was authoritatively stated in the
Assembly that, to give their horses exercise
enough to keep them in health, it was necessary
to lend them to people who were kind enough to
work them.

RESOURCE.
NO. 1: “Rob brought a pair of rabbits for me to
cook yesterday, t tried to do them like cook
did the roast hare at home. But in trying to
singe the fur off I spoilt them.”
NO. 2: “ You silly girl! That isn't the way."
NO. 1: “ What is the way, then? ”
NO. 2: “ You ought to pluck them !”
Indeed, to judge by the reports in the daily
papers they were simply trampling over each
other to get their names upon the list. As soon
as Parliament decided to send a contingent a
selection parade was called ; then came a revela-
tion. Out.of the 510 volunteers only 215 attended.
Next morning the daily papers which have been
simply yelling for Blood, came out with the
explanation. Many of the absent ones, it seems,
had missed their trains, etc., etc. ; thus was the
national honor vindicated. One of the men who
did attend was selected and sent in to be medically
examined as the final test. He was passed, but
the strain was too much, and on leaving the
medical inspection room he fell in a faint in the
barrack yard.
Adipose was a Brisbaneite, some time ago
—stout, popular, and amiable. Was also secre-
tary to the committee of a certain benevo-
lent institution, said committee being
composed almost entirely of ladies.
It happenedfthat Adipose fell sud-
denly ill, and was unable to
attend the usual monthlymeet-
ing. Ladies of committee, on
hearing the cause of the secre-
tary’s absence, were exceed-
ingly sympathetic, and sug-
gested thatoneof their number
should forward Adipose (as a
mark of kindly attention) a
ticket o the General Hos-
pital. A day or two afterwards,
the invalid was startled by
the arrival at his diggings of'
a ticket for the Lying-in Hos-
pital. And the unfortunate
man’s friends call on him, now
and again, and ask him, with
unholy grins, if he is doing as
well as could be expected.

A London beerman, lately in Australia,
cabled ’Ome and included in his message an in-
quiry after his wife, who is expected to add to the
population. His London partner had great diffi-
culty in finding code-words to suit the situation,
but at last discovered two which he thought would
fit. They were “ Rangueibos,” i. e., “Goods in
very fair condition,” and “ Vertroilas ”—“No-
thing as yet delivered.”
In a certain Australian town two
friends met a Scotch acquaintance with
frenzy in his eye and a revolver in his fist.
In explanation he said—“ A mon has des-
troyt raa happiness, an’ ahm gaun to
shoot some lead intil him.” Three hours
later they met him again, and he con-
fessed to having spared the destroyer’s
life. “It’s joost this way,” he said, “a
hae transfairred ane a’ ina hooses tae ma
weef, an’ a thocht that if a shote the
scoondrel a mightna get ma hoose back
again! ”
* * * %.
The Medical Congress at Brisbane
was very severe on the growing practice of
over-working growing boys and girls in
schools. One doctor wanted to know what
could be thought of parents who, while
emanding an eight-hour day for them-
selves, exacted ten hours study a day from
their children. . . . The wicked and
vicious practice of robbing children of a
portion of their dinner-time and compel-
ling them to study beyond specified school
hours is on the increase in Australia.
There are hundreds of school children in
big Australian towns who, driven by the
mad ambition of parents and teachers,
habitually study from eight to ten hours a
day ! And the officials in the Education
Departments know it and wink at it.

The gallantry of the police—or what '?
Males barely outnumber females in N.S.W. ; yet
in the gaols at the end of last year (the official
report is but just issued) of 2081 prisoners only
187 were females.

Captain and Mrs. Craigan and some of
their friends paid a visit to the warehouse of
Messrs. Brandt Bros., 236 Pitt-street, Sydney, on
Saturday last to witness an exhibition in ice-
making with their small hand ice machine. They
expressed themselves as delighted and promised
to take two at 50s. each before leaving Sydney,
and one of their new ice-cream freezers, which
require no turning of the handle. These appear
to be much superior to the old style.*
In a letter received some time ago from
missionary Gillies (since, it is feared, murdered
in Tanna), he said that some natives who had
unsuccessfully tried to kill him, sent him word
that they’d make a better job of it next time, and
that they were “keeping the oven hot for him.”
Is grim humor of that sort native to the cannibal
nig. as well as to the comic papers ?

The fight for the Melbourne mayoralty
t’other day was the end of a three-volume story.
Ship-owner M'Eacharn and Lawyer Sim Gillott
had simultaneous hankerings after the billet in
1897, but the lawyer’s supporters finding them-
selves outnumbered, retired without giving battle.
Mayor M’Eacharn’s first year of office was so
very gorgeous that his re-election in 1898 followed
as a matter of course. When he came forward
for the third time in succession the Gillott-
ites made out a grievance for their champion.
They said that old friend Sam stood aside last
year to oblige the unretiring Mayor. On the
other hand the M‘Eacharnites contended that
their man had been sure of re-election at second
time of asking, and was not under any real
obligation to anybody in the matter, but they
admitted that Gillott would be entitled to the
chair this time—if he could get it. Twentv-five
municipal fatmen took part in the recent struggle,
and the same old M ivor won by a pip—l 3 to 12.
Whereupon Gillott effusively congratulated the
winner and hoped he would get all the knighthood
and other “distinctions” that seem likely to
happen to him in Federation Year. If there was
any satire intended triumphant Mac took the
sting out of it when he candidly confessed that, as
there was an important figure to be cut by the
Mayor of Melbourne within the next few months,
he had been anxious to get the opportunity to
cut it.
» • * *
“ Yarra ” gossips concerning the municipal
contest aforesaid :
I dropped in at the City Council for the Mayoral elec-
tion, the most important to date. The full-length
framed oil-paintings on the walls of the departed great—
Mayors J. T. Smith, Moubray, Nicholson, Greeves, A. K.
Smith, Hodgson, etcetera—and some of the mighty
living, such as Butters, Stewart, and Carter, gazed down,
tremulous as it were, on the scene. The question was
whether moneyed M'Eaoharn, or moneyed Gillott, the
only two men with cash enough, was to be Mayor for 1900,
with the Parliament of Australia to banquet and a cock-
sure knighthood dangling. M’Eacharn, presiding in his
furry robes and Mayoral chain, was in an electric state of
repressed excitement. Gillott lounged apparently as cool
as a cucumber, but it must have been the South Polar
Mount Erebus, with ice swathing the volcanic fires.
The more I see of Mayor M'Eacharn, the better I like
him. My first idea of him was as of a mere conceited,
empty—in an intellectual sense—money-bag. Indeed, he
is not of a strong calibre, and will find a somewhat
mediocre level in the Australian Leg. Assembly, which
he is pretty sure to get into, most probably as the mem-
ber for Melbourne city. Yet he is a fine fellow, trans-
parently candid, and very sensible. There was nothing
hollow or merely conventional in the (customary) praises
showered on him at the Mayoral luncheon after beating
Gillott. who behaved splendidly, taking his gruel like a
man. The see-saw of the votes had been so nice for a
week before, apparently even, that it was bound to be a
crushing disappointment to whoever got beaten, though
M’Eacharn would naturally have felt it more than Gillott.

In connection with the recent island
excursion-trip of the s.s. Waikare (patronised by
tin- top-layer of the Australian social cream) there
is being related a yarn the true inwardness whereof
will appeal forcibly to those who know how far
male New Hebridean costume is an advance, in
suggestiveness, upon mere stark-nakedness. When
a boatload of ladies, chaperoned by a matron of
ferocious virtue, was approaching the landing-
place at one island a line of “ full dressed ”
warriors drew up on the beach to receive the
visitors. The horrified officer in charge longed to
crawl under the boat and inspect the keel—the
ladies looked carefully out to sea, and the she-
dragon of the party muttered something about
“ reporting such indecency.” Then the officer
rushed off to consult with his comrade in another
boat upon the enormousness of the situation, but
upon his return he was paralysed to find the fair
creatures—thus relieved from (white) male obser-
vation—calmly engaged in “snap-shotting” the
lusty brown animals as they stood. And, later
on, I’ttle furtively-sniggering groups of women,
standing about the deck with their hpads together,
testified that the camera had “nothing ex-
tenuated.”
There i.s a lady in a Sydney suburb whose
olive-branches sprouted respectively on Anniver-
sary Day, St. Patrick’s Day, Guy Fawkes’ Day,
and Prince of Wales’ Birthday. Great was the
domestic disappointment when a she-Benjamin
made her appearance towards the middle of June,
just missing the “ Glorious Fourth ” and ruining
the family record for anniversatility,

On the new frank-stamp envelope issued

This runs your champion mean man pretty
close. An old lady in Australia had property, to
the value of some thousands, in Chancery for
years, but never had sufficient money to engage a
solicitor to take up the ease for her. So there it
remained while the old lady struggled on with .£5O
a year, till a rising young lawyer of her acquaint-
ance offered to take the matter in hand and see
what could be done. After much time and litiga-
tion the property was redeemed, and the old lady
came into her fortune, but there was no mention
of remuneration for the solicitor. Then the good
dame took a trip to England to settle her affairs,
and on her return brought her lawyer the hand-
some present of—a box of note-paper !
* • » •
A Thursday Island correspondent to The
Bulletin :
A sergeant in the local garrison company of soldiery
died recently at the local hospital, it is alleged whilst
or after undergoing an operation for the removal of some
growth in the nose. The body was coffined in the same
state as when death occurred, in ensanguined uniform.
The state of the corpse was seen by members of the gar-
rison previous to coffin being screwed down, and duly
made the subject of report by an indignant bombardier ;
but nothing inore was heard of the complaint. A field-
gun and limber could easily have been turned out for the
funeral. The company has harness, and horses would
readily have been lent hy civilian owners. Instead of this,
a rough contrivance of wood was hastily knocked to-
gether in the wheeler’s shop, and some black paint slapped
over it. This elaborate affair was carted to the hospital,
and from thence, with coffin mounted, carried to the
cemetery by eight men, remainder of detachment accom-
panying. A letter of complaint from a civilian friend of
deceased was published in the local paper. The doctor
stated that death was due to epilepsy. Local opinion
thinks a post-mortem should have been held. The failure
to lay-out the body, which could have been proved, was
not gone into.
* * * *
“ Groper,” to The Bulletin :
Re the outraged shriek over the Dreyfus court-martial.
The indignation meeting held in Perth (W. A.), last month
was convened by a windy person named Weiss, who has
a grievance of his own against the Government, and, in
consaquencs thereof, regards himself as a W.A. Dreyfus.
Mayor Forrest overflowed the chair, and the speakers
included Thomas Walker, ex-N.S.W. M.L.A., and a rev.
somebody, whom the crowd referred to as “ Smiler.”
Resolutions carried with enthusiasm, but, being requested
to subscribe to cost of cabling, the indignant populace
reached for its hat and sought the open air. Subs, having
totalled 2s. 9d., the Govt, was asked to forward the
message at country’s expense. Those resolutions have
not yet started for “ Yurrup.”

to a European school, and he backs horses
with European bookmakers, and belongs
to a Masonic lodge. But he will go to
his grave in the firm belief that the earth
is flat, and that the thunder emanates
from the throat ot the dragon, whose
flashing eyes cause the lightning ; tfyab
luck lives in the chalice of a water-lily,
and that the “Ingleseman up on the
hill, who make the weather—him only
pull leg always the whole time long.”

“ G. 8.” : In Melb. Hospital, many
curious things are done. They bathe
patients, for instance, shortly after they
have partaken of dinner. Which breaks
up the popular idea of the danger of such
a course. Also, there is the hospital
sponge—in each v/ard. The hospital is
large, and sponge is highly absorbent, and
the last person who comes under its ap-
plication finds this out. For there are
odors of many patients, with many
diseases, in the recesses of the hospital
sponge, which make it a hateful and
loathsome thing to the last patient wiped
down. These are strange things, which
stand out like ugly knobs on an other-
wise smooth surface of excellence and
efficient management.
The inevitable after-effect of Melbourne’s
land-boom, and subsequent disposition to accept
roguery as a matter of course, is seen lately in the
continual disclosure of embezzlement by civil ser-
vice clerks and others. Men get into prison now-
adays through following in the footsteps of the
criminals who went unpunished a few years ago.

Another sweet instance of the Law’s affec-
tion for the legal brotherhood happened in Mel-
bourne t’other day. William Buckley, late chief
clerk and accountant to the Vic. Crown Law
department, came before Judge Gaunt at General
Sessions to receive sentence for “larceny” of
£350. What was called larceny in the Law dept,
clerk would have been dealt with as embezzlement
in an unprivileged sort of clerk, no doubt ; but
the point is hardly worth mentioning, for larcenist
Buckley got 18 months hard labor, and embezzler
Buckley, in the Criminal Court, might not have
come worse off. Prisoner was defended by bar-
rister O’Hara Wood, secretary to the Law Insti-
tute, who found it “ doubly painful to speak of a
client found guilty, when he was a personal
friend.” Also Wood referred to the awful “in-
dignity ” that a Crown Law department clerk
suffers in being arrested. Charles O’Halloran,
the sporting amalgam, followed by prothonotary
O’Halloran, and Jenkins, clerk of Parliaments,
testified to the exceptional uprightness of the
prisoner, and then Judge Gaunt had to nerve him-
self for the ordeal of sentencing “one whom he
had known well.’’ The Court nearly broke down,
Buckley’s case was so peculiarly sad. “At
Moonee Valley races he appeared to have staked
on one horse the amount of half his income for
the year,” and unfortunately the gee-gee did not
win. His Honor pitied the busted punter from
the bottom of his heart, nevertheless he had a
stern duty to perform, so, after stating that a

The average legal practitioner has a hard
row to hoe in Westralia. In the late important
appeal by the Lake View Consols v. Australian
Gold Recovery Co. (MacArthur Forrest Patent),
the members of the Full Court, after two months’
consideration, delivered their judgments. Four
days later counsel on both sides moved the Full
Court for leave to appeal to the Privy Council;
but as the Court then only consisted of two
Judges, counsel asked for an adjournment until
the Chief Justice would be present, as they wished
the Court to explain its decision to them, in
order that they might know which side should
appeal, or whether both sides should do so. But
perhaps it is the Judges who have a row to hoe.

The Bulletin hears that on a big estate
at Camden, N.S. W., 40 acres are under asparagus
this season, and that it is being sold in Sydney at
about a quarter the price of the preserved article.
But, curiously enough, Australian-grown aspara-
gus can’t compare in flavor with the preserved
article imported from America and France.
“ R.E.,” to The Bulletin :
“No British soldier has had to face cannon since the
Indian Mutiny, 42 years ago.” Wrong. In ’63, the
Ghazis had several light guns ; so had the Bhoteas, in ’64.
In 79, the Amir Slier Ali brought a fine train of artillery
into action at Ali Musjid ; and, the following year, Ayub
Khan made skilful use of his artillery when he attacked
Burrows at Maiwand. In the subsequent battle with
Roberts, at Candahar, he added to his own artillery guns
he bad taken from Burrow's. In Egypt, there were some
Egyptian batteries in action at Kassassin Lock. The lines,
too, at Tel-el-Kebir, were, according to official reports,
heavily armed, and the fire, according to Hamley (who
led tbe assault, and, having been through the Crimean
war, was a good judge), was tremendous. In the Soudan,
in ’B5, the Dervishes made free use of the Egyptian
ordnance they had taken. So much for what is past.
Neither you nor I know what is to come, but it's even
betting there will be no fighting in the Transvaal worth
talking about.
