A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October IG, 1899.
My Dear Myee.—
The Fleet Ball was quite the ball of the year.
Brass buttons were responsible for 90 per cent,
of the glamour, but the ball was also remarkably
well stocked with all kinds of men—civilians, naval,
and military. Rumors of three wall-flowers to
each man came to nothing. About a week before
the ball, the streams of letters from the girls
wanting invitations sent the list of women ahead,
but with great courage the navy rushed out and
gathered in as many extra men as it could find.
Result —the ball of the season. People with
imagination and feeling said it was just like “ The
Battle of Waterloo ball,” because war had been
declared that day in the Transvaal about half-a-
seore thousand miles away.
Still, there may have been a similarity. French
versus English, don’t you know. Some French
residents stayed away from the ball, by way of
hostilities with His Ex. re Dreyfus.
By the way, we must surely be coming to the
end of the big men who have taken “the huff.”
Never ljefore. in the history of Sydney, have
people stood so hard, and long, and solemnly, and
with such marked unoomfortableness, on their
Dignity. The Admiral, Consul Biard d’Aunet,
Colonel Smith, our Mister James—but I might go
on like this for ever. His anomalous Ex. makes
no attempt whatsover to lift any of them. He
reasons that the man who can climb up can
crawl down. That is the worst of a Governor with
ideas.
To return to the ball, —Garden Island spent
three days over the preparations. Bridges were
built, awnings hoisted, and torpedoes polished up.
It was a gusty night, and every time the wind
rose the impromptu bridge leading from the store-
room to the ball-room where the ball went on,
trembled and squeaked. A thousand guests
passed over it. I was told, by way of reassurance,
that three hundred blue-jackets jumped on it
beforehand to prove its stability, but we were not
all blue-jackets. Boats carried the guests from
Man-o’-war steps to the Island. Who was the
officer who managed to say to every woman as if
he meant it, when helping her off the launch,
“You must take care of that step ! ”
“ To meet His Excellency the Governor ! ” said
the cards. But not the thin, pale, poetic youth
with the high white brow, and long white fingers.
His Ex. arrived in plain dress, with a deep band
of red sunburn all over his face and hands, and a
new firm light in his eye as though he had met and
routed for the first time the Australian mosquito.
It was strange to see those three young men
arrive alone—His Ex. and the two aides—after the
large accompaniment of V.R. ladies we have
grown used to. They were assisted through the
ordeal by Kenneth Mackay, the “Stirrup
Jingles ” bard, also in the forces, who has the pull
over all other bards in this bardful land. He
travelled in the West with His Ex. for a fort-
night and could have fired his poems by day and
by night into the vice-regal ear if he hadn’t been a
“soldier anil a man,” and also —which is more to
the |xiint —a metaphysician and humanitarian.
Being the “end-of-the-season ball,” nothing
extra special cotdd be expected in the way of
frocks. There were a good many beautiful dresses.
Also, there was a larger number of tired and tat-
tered and world-worn garments than I have ever
seen at a ball before. This must, in charity, be
attributed to the frightfully perishable nature of
the present-day ball-dress. Tulle is a thing that
you can’t sew too much ; when tulle begins to go,
it just goes. Likewise, mousseline de soie, and
gauze, and embroidered net. The dress that
doesn't go to rags round the feet after the first
lancers is quite entirely out of it, and a new frock
for every ball is the only way to keep a creation
in anything like its freshness. And now I would
like to point out the awful danger —the biggest,
awfullest danger of going to a Squad, ball in a
frock that has dirty little stalactites of white
chiffon draggling trotn under the hem, transparent
sleeves with a tendency to burst, and a Jot of
crushed illusion all over everything. The men at
the Squad, ball were heard remarking, “ What a
lot of plain women!” So you see, a mere man
can't distinguish between a pretty woman who
looks plainisli in a very dirty frock, and the plain
woman who looks pretty in a clean one.
Mrs. Lyne, the new Premier’s wife, was elegantly
clad in grey brocade, cooled down with white lace.
By the way, people still look round for a little red
man with an eyeglass at the word “ Premier,” and
feel surprised at the large, dark man, with the
now fast-greying beard, who has changed all that.
Mrs. Admiral was also in grey. Mrs. Hayward,
who returns with her husband to the English
Admiralty next month, wore a black net
and sequined frock behind a huge bouquet
of pink roses. Another naval lady, who
can always be relied on to look decorative,
was Mrs. Dicken in white brocade and heaps of
lovely diamonds, and the usual enormous posy of
pink flowers and pink streamers. Mrs. Phillip
Morton was the Ijest-dressed figure in the room,
perhaps, in black richly embroidered with black
pailettes, a faint outlining of white tulle round
the bodice, and the hair dressed in a highly-
becoming Beardsley style. The three pretty
daughters of Mrs. Alex. Oliver were all in white,
the debutante, Miss Ruth, distinguished by
white flowers in her hair. Miss Mabel Laidley’s
pale-grey silk veiled with fine black lace and
lightened with a flower posy was not half as old
as it sounds. Girls seem to go in for greys and
blacks largely nowadays. I noticed one of the
prettiest girls in Sydney—not out of her teens,
either—dressed in the severest black frock im-
aginable—one that her worldly-wise mamma
would certainly not have Worn herself.
Of course, the craze to look old only endures
while one is very young. Apropos of which, Maud
MacCarthy must be a phenomenon. She really
must. I saw her in the Gardens with her father
and mother the other day, and observed, with
wonder, that—hideous to relate !—she wore white
stoclcinys-J With a short frock too. The ordinary
infant in her ’teens wouldn’t wear white stockings
for a royal mandate. “They’re so dreadfully
behind the times,” any little girl will tell you.
Nellie Cooke’s paying concert illustrated the
fixed desire of Sydney to go free. Her invitation
concert Saw thousands of ardent music-lovers
rushing the Town Hall in opera-cloaks, and
revelling consciously in the thought of going to a
function. Her 35., 25., and Is. warble saw a scanty
collection of admirers scattering themselves over
that great space, and looking a mere handful in
comparison with those who had gushed into the
young singer’s free show.
And she deserved such a good house to. A
really promising soprano, who puts everything she
knows and feels into her singing, and takes one’s
breath away sometimes by her daring flights
through the most florid of florid music. But no
organ will stand the frightful strain Miss Cooke
put on it last Friday, forcing it up where it was
unable to go, and treating it as if a voice that bent
so flexibly could never break. Her platform frock
was a white silk polonaise over soft white flounces.
I am glad to say it was long enough to touch the
floor all round. It hurts to sit in the front row
and gaze at a singer’s ankles, especially if she is
singing “I am coming with flying feet,” while you
are discovering that she wears seven shoes. I
don’t mean that Miss Cooke’s shoes were more
than two’s, for she is a pretty, dainty-looking little
woman.
Lemmone assisted. Lately, there has scarcely
been a concert that Lemmone has not fluted
for. Also, he is breaking out again as a manager,
undeterred by his bad luck with thefiery-tempered
Alva and her meddling husband. He managed
Fanny Bauer’s concert on Tuesday week ; Maud
MaoCarthy’s last Wednesday ; and is now in nego-
tiation with Paderewski. That will be a treat for
us. We don’t often get a great artist out here in
the flower of life—neither a phenomenon, nor the
ruin of a past magnificence.
Speaking of age, our Sydney Beau Brummel,
who is a dear old man and as kindly as you make
’em, came to 70 years lately, and was very nervous,
not at the 70, but at a certain paper which has a
well-meaning little way of announcing: “Many
happy returns of the day to Mr. John Smith, aged
102 to-day.”
For my part, if I were a man I should take that
column as the greatest compliment to my sex.
Who would wish a leading woman happy returns
of her fiftieth year?
Evidently, Mr. Lyne is very deep. A Suffrage
deputation waited on him the other day to press
him to urge on the Cause. He replied that he
had the Cause so deeply at heart that it was not
necessary for him to receive the deputation, as he
was working for them with ail his will, and
wanted woman suffrage with all his heart and
soul, more, unutterably more, than he could say.
That was the message he sent out to the deputation-
ists who were twitching their veils nervously in the
lobby. Rather an elegant way of getting rid of
them, wasn’t it?
Suffrage reminds me—l don’t know why—-that
the Ladies’ club, alias the Victoria Salon, is in
danger of losing its life. Things haven't prospered
altogether with the Salon. Subscriptions, for
instance. The “at homes ” got snuffed out some-
how this season, although they seemed to draw
well last year. Still, the plain truth is that the
Salon never has been, and never will be, while it is
what it was, a salon. As a ladies’ club it has never
caught on at all. Too much suburb drowned the
requisite amount of intellect for a salon ; a rather
out-of-the-way situation spoiled it as a club, and
people tired of it. lam told the shutters will go
up soon, if a big change does not set in.
Of course you know that Rose Musgrove played
“ Glory ” all last week in “ The Christian.” From
Monday till Monday Miss Watson was too ill to
act. Rose’s Glory was admirable, unimpeachable
as an imitation of Henrietta’s. Every trick and
inflection was reproduced, down to that weird
clutching and clawing of John’s waistcoat in the
kiss-me-John scene. “So like, so wonderfully
like.” But clever as Rose Musgrove undoubtedly
is—she arrived at second lead in six months—she
isn’t heavy enough for Glory, and her voice gets
thinly shrieky when emotions press on it. The
chief difference between Glory Musgrove and
Glory Watson was the pretty green frock sported
by the latter, which was worn by Miss Musgrove
in her original part of variety actress. On Monday
Miss Watson was well enough to cry “Why not ? ”
again. I hear that she and her bridegroom, Mr.
Ratcliffe, leave for England pretty soon.
Mr. Radcliffe, by the way, has a double in
Sydney—another young Englishman, Lord Some-
body Something, in blue and white stripes and
pinee-nez. He has been knocking round Sydney
for several months, and distinguished himst If at
the last Govt. House garden party by his flirtation
with the prettiest girl in Melbourne. They are
both rather young. All the same, people remarked
on the way they found two chairs, dragged them
along the gravel to the side of the entrance, and
coolly sat alone there all the afternoon within full
view of the incoming guests.
Mayoress Harris in mauve chiffon opened the
Manly Flower Show last Tuesday, assisted by
Miss Harris and a clergyman. r i ■ e Show lias
seen changes; this year it merges into a weird
combination of Congregarional-cum-Presbytei :an
interests, with a dash of Wesleyan. As a paying
concern, this year’s show was a failure. But
aesthetically, it was far ahead of last year. The
flowers were fixed up in a large tent with
green grass underfoot, and refreshments were
arranged in another large tent. People lost their
way from the boat to the Show. The ad. that
bade one find “Fern Gully” was misleading
enough in all conscience, considering that the
Show was held in Church grounds in the heart of
Manly. I know people—of opposite genders —who
walked from one end of the beach to the other in
the moonlight looking for anything like a gully,
and finally gave up the Flower Show in despair.
For a long time the glory of the old days when
gjirls used to get their frocks for the Manly Flower
Show as they do now for the Cup, has been slowly
waning. For one thing, the wild flowers are pretty
well played out—pulled out, I should say.
And when it comes to the present cut-throat
competition between florists in houses anil florists
in the gutter, a Flower Show doesn’t seem to have
much of a chance. The street flower-sellers have
a new grievance. Bush fires mysteriously broke
out round Manly lately. The flower-sellers ex-
plained that the florists had set fire to the bush to
burn the flowers, so that the street flower-sellers
would have to go further for their wares, and
subsequently charge more for them, and sub-
sequently to that, not find anyone to buy at
raised prices. Then everyone would flock to the
florists in shops, who only keep garden flowers
that mysterious bush fires can’t affect, and all
would be well—for the florists.
Two Sydney ladies, Miss Hassall and Miss
Macnllum, are going as Missionaries to Palestine.
Sydney setting out across the globe to teach the
Holy Land all about the Saviour and the Apostles !
What next?
So far, I have only heard of one local nurse who
is accepted for the Transvaal. This is Miss Stella
Montague, sister of little Neville, solicitor, of this
city.
New South Wales presented George Reid with
a beautiful silver service last Monday—a service
that was solid beyond all doubt. The ex-
Premier’s admirers had intended to run into
something more actively useful, but, unfortunately,
subscriptions stopped at the tea-service. Also,
there was a lovely little half-moon set in diamonds
for Mrs. Reid, who was too ill to appear at the
presentation meeting. Georgie said, in returning
thanks, that it was Julian Salomons who had
induced him to go to the bar at the age of 34.
Which adds yet another to the many Sydney
barristers ami solicitors who will tell you they
went into law because Salomons, with marvellous
discrimination, saw unparalleled legal acumen
going to wasto within them.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Lady Brassey and Lord Dick Neville visited
the Assembly to hear the Glory and Gory debate
on sending a contingent to the Transvaal. Uncle
Tom’s wife was evidently much amused with
Warrnamhool Murray’s slashing speech in oppo-
sition. Lady B. is now very grey. She was in-
clined to greyness when she first came to Victoria,
but her spouse’s numerous falls-olf. ami the worries
of Government House, have made her more so.
She is 41—and looks it—quite. Witty John
Hancock’s absence—he was lying delirious at
death’s door—was greatly felt by the anti-J ingoes
on this occasion.
“ ‘ Big ’ Clarke’s widow will return to Victoria
in a few weeks. She leaves London almost im-
mediately, after having a high old time. The
duchesses and countesses have taken to the smart
little widow, who, unlike nearly all the
rest of the titled Victorian women, can talk
cleverly on most things without putting her foot
in it.
“Another returning Victorian is Pastor Fit-
chott, who comes back as ‘ Doctor ’ Fitchett— by
grace of some little Canadian university which
gave him an honorary degree. Mr. Fitchett is a
B.A. of Melbourne, which university wisely docs
not confer honorary degrees.
“Re MayorMcEacharn’s re-election for another
year, it was an open secret in the City Council that
if he had not been re-elected he would have retired
from municipal politics and gone to live in London.
He is very anxious after the title which ought to
have come along last Queen’s birthday, hut didn’t.
McEacharn is most ambitious for distinction.
Some time ago he talked of going ’Orae and enter-
ing the Commons as a Conservative—which any-
one can do, if ready to subscribe hugely to the
party funds. Now he aims at a place in the
Federal Parliament. He spends Ins wealth in
princely fashion, but he spends it in such a fashion
as to help his ambitions all the time.
“A fashionable and wealthy bride-elect has
engaged a lady-companion- the daughter of a
Melb. medico—for the trip ’Ome. A companion
for a honeymoon couple ! Romance is nearly dead.
“ Our heiress is still free, and hopes are flourish-
ing in various, directions. A suitor from the
North is inclined to fancy his chance strongly,
and he neglects the little girl who follows him so
bravely in the hunting-field. The sweeps on the
affair are in somewhat bad taste maybe, but the
holder of the alliterative initials is very sure of
winning, and after all, though theswain is a very
old hand at thegaine of lovo-making, he is probably
in earnest this time.
“ I heard two smart little girls discussing several
others who had volunteered as nurses for the
Transvaal. ‘Of course it’s very plucky of them,
but I can’t say T envy them. My own idea is to
go out and nurse the Boers—l fancy I’d be of
most service to my country that way.’
“Dr. Fraser, wiio has determined to survive
his wound, is one of those white-skinned, red-
headed men of whom one expects cantankerous-
ness and temper. He is not beloved by the
nurses, who express keen sympathy with Mrs.
Fraser.
“Another doctor has developed mania, fancied
his wife wished to poison him, and has been
placed in the Kew Asylum. Some people
declare he is sane, but they are a not favored
minority. To marry a smart Society doctor now-
a-days is to take a big risk.
“The District Nurses’ball, due in Melbourne
last week, was indefinitely postponed on account
of the influenza epidemic. Australia will have to
sit down and consider the influ. question very
seriously indeed one of these days.
“Some wildly-exaggerated Melbourne yarns
touching the Jew-Gentile marriage question have
gone the rounds of the Australian press. One
paragraph stated—“l4 proselytes were recently
admitted to the Jewish Church in one week.
The grievance is that the converts are not be-
lieved to be genuine in their professions of change
of faith. They are all unmarried women, and the
disturbance of their religious convictions has been
occasioned by the circumstance of their being en-
gaged to Jewish men. This is a sore point with
the daughters of Judah, of whom there are thirty
to every male of their fa.th in Victoria.” The
fable was born, seemingly, of the fact that three
Gentile damsels, with wedding-rings in view, en-
rolled themselves among the Chosen within a fort-
night, and the synagogic conscience felt some-
what shocked thereat. Girls of the Christian
persuasion, and especially Roman Catholics, are,
as a matter of course, looked on with suspicion.
The chances are that they don’t get converted
from the main principle of their young belief, no
matter how little belief they may have absorbed.
The religious objection to these easy proselytes is
strong among serious .Tews, but as poachers on
Rebecca’s matrimonial preserves, the ‘converts’
have not been sufficiently numerous to cause a
scare, therefore Rebecca runs only a fair average
risk of not getting married, as far as one can see.
Needless to say there are not 30, nor even
three Shebrews to every Hebrew in Victoria.
What have Jewish mothers ever done that they
should be accused of such a oue-sided arrange-
ment?
“ In the ‘ Merciless World,’ Dora De Winton is
a lorn young thing, who, owing to the mysterious
stage-disappearance of John Saunders her Jo,
John, can’t be quite sure whether her widowhood
is permanent or not. Anyhow, she gives her
wardrobo the benefit of the doubt, unlike the song
heroine, whose ‘ widow’s sombre cap concealed her
once luxuriant hair.’ Miss De Win ton’s sack-
cli't.h-and-ashes, so to speak, take the form of
white muslin over pink lace flounced at the foot,
and a fichu folded round her shoulders, so as to
bare her round white neck to pitying breezes and
covetous male glances.
“ One gets out of patience with these martyred
maidens of melodrama, persecuted by the unwel-
come attentions of ruffianly lovers. There’s noth-
ing easier, worse luck, than to choke off an
admirer. Don’t curl your hair for a few nights,
leave your stocking-heels to the ravages of Time
and low-cut shoes, advertise their condition by
holding up the tail of your modishly-trailing skirt,
and the trick’s done. He’s gone, and all the
king’s horses won’t bring him back again ! It’s
hen-foolishness to loosen your curls, manicure .your
hands, and don your prettiest things with a view
to shooing a woman-hunter. John Saunders,
small blame to him, sees nothing to be ashamed of
in his neat hands and slim wrists. His sleeves
have a cunning knack of coming unbuttoned—the
signal for their wearer to clasp his milk-white
arms behind his head in picturesque reverie.
Y ours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.
Woman’s sphere broadens, and the cable
relates that a duke’s daughter has gone to the
Transvaal as a war correspondent. But the true
mission of every woman—amateur nurse or other-
wise—is to delight the world with her beauty.
And this every woman can accomplish by wear-
ing P. D. corsets. They improve the grace of
form and by thus giving satisfaction to the wearer
arid beauty to her countenance. You can see the
P.D. expression playing round her mouth.*
“The Light of Asia,” which, as is well known,
was on • of the principal books of its year, was an un-
doubted success, and without doubt so is the new light
of Australasia, the “ Albitross Lamp Wick.” “It requirts
no trimming” emits no odour, uses less oil, prevents
sil'.oky chimneys, and is far safer than the ordinary wick.*
“Ten men armed to the teeth fighting a
man in his shirt ’’—remarked John Morley, of the
Transvaal war.

N.S.W.
Sydney music-lovers will be sorry to hear
of the rather serious illness of Mr. J. A. Delany.
Kalgoorlie Town Council has just received
as a gift the miner’s right with which Pat Hannan
was armed when he pegged out the first of the
local mines. It is dated August, 1893.

self the immorality of attending the performance
of a stage play, would be horrified to find Ins
double in Vernon’s Chancellor in “lolanthe.

have never represented the same constituency
twioe, but the real truth is that I never repre-

N.S.W. Justice Minister Wood, The Bdl-
lETIN is told, is not a Cornstalk ; he is a Victorian
—bom at Wood’s Point, Vic., in ’69, consequently
he is 30—not 28. He worked in his father’s store,
at Delegate, N.S.W., until elected to Parliament
in '94, in the Labor interest.
A Sydney paper, taking cue from a Sun-
day one, cabled Kruger for a current opinion on
war matters. The prepaid reply was : “No
opinion ; watch and pray.” The editor was so
mad at being asked to pray that he declined to
publish Kruger abridged or unabridged.
Vic. Chief-Secretary Peacock, when he
addresses or refers to the Right Honorable Sir
George Turner, P.C., K.C.M.G., M.L.A., J.P.,
calls him “ Boss.” “ I must refer it to the Boss,”
he says as to a doubtful point of policy, and lie
hails Turner as “ Boss ” in any company in which
they may be.
When the real pinch ci«.ne, the most pro-
minent of the Johannesburg “ reformers ” moved
to the rear with great celerity. The leading
TJitlander editor, Mr. Moneypenny (London
Times correspondent, by the way) left disguised
as “a motherly-looking lady of uncertain years,
in a velvet gown.”
China mineral fields have lately taken
several distinguished mining men, besides Queens-
land ex-geologist Jack. Among them is Dr.
Vogelsgang, who was lately in Australia with
Herr Schmeisser, on behalf of the German Govt,
and others. Another ex-Australian visitor, Dr.
Simon, is prospecting in Siberia.
The rumors of Judge Williams being about
to retire from Vic. Bench and make his home in
England have resolved themselves into the official
statement that he will start an eleven months’
holiday at the end of the judicial year, returning
to work in Nov., 1900. Meanwhile the other
Judges will have to take turns at keeping brother
W.’s seat warm.

S.A. Labor-leader Batchelor, who is
against the Transvaal contingent idea, raised the
laughter of the grovelsotne and the ungodly by
admitting that he volunteered for a similar service
in ’B5. Coneybeer went one worse by admitting
that he stowed away on the vessel that carried the
Soudan contingent, but, being found, he was
thrown ashore with ignominy, a rejected warrior.
C. M. Bagot, a popular South Australian
who died the other day, was one of the organised
party of half-a-dozen who, when the province was
behind-time with the construction of the trans-
continental telegraph-line, carried the messages
over the unwired distance on horseback. To his
grandfather is credited the discovery of Kapunda
copper-mine, and that led to the opening-up of
the Burra and Moonta, in which Bagor, sen., also
took a leading part.
One of the funniest things in the recent
Neild-Waine military inquiry at Sydney was
Waine’s indignation at a press paragraph, to the
effect that several witnesses confirmed the state-
ment that W. had been “ glorious ” at a certain
festivity. The word, he maintains, should have
been “corrobcrated,” not “confirmed,” and as
soon as his onerous military duties will permit
he will set about the compilation of a reporters’
handbook.
Rumored in Westralia that Jerry Smith,
the awful ’Groper Governor, who wns sued for the
price of sundry drinks and other things, and who
also got into great trouble over a hotel transac-
tion, will be granted 12 months’ leave of absence
from next month, and will leave for England
immediately. The meeting between Smith and
Chamberlain, and the cold, steely glare of Cham-
berlain’s withering eye-glass as lie fixes it on
Smith, should be worth photographing. Smith’s
stormy career in Westralia has been one long put-
ting of his foot in it.
Stafford Bird, the Wellington of Napo-
leon Braddon’s Waterloo, grows astute in his old
age. Instead of seizing the Tas. Premiership, he
puts Neil Lewis into the billet and takes the
Treasurership for himself. In the battle with
Braddon, which is almost certain to ensue before
the Federal posts of honor and profit are all filled
up, Lewis will thus be in the forefront. He will
probably be slain ; but Bird—the unambitious,
solf-abnegatory Bird —will not even be wounded.
It is an open secret that he would have taken the
vacant Lands portfolio under Braddon the other
day had it been offered him.
T. F. De Courcy Browne, dead at 65 in
Callan Park hospital, leaves behind him alive
there two other ex-members of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment. Browne and A. G. Taylor were M’sL. A.,
R- H. D. White was M.L.C. The three were
Mudgee men, and were all prominent in their day.
Browne was an Irishman, born in Dublin, but
arrived in Australia over 40 years ago, and has
worked pretty well through all of it. He was
policeman in Queensland, mining-registrar in
N.S.W., mines-law compiler in W.A., and two or
three things in other provinces. He started a
newspaper in Gulgongand several private mining-
agencies.
Major Reay, editor of Melb. ’Erald, is
going to the Transvaal as “special ” for that rag.
He wanted to go with the contingent, but com-
mandant Holled-Smith told him plainly that if he
did he would have to go as a soldier pure and
simple, and not scribble while on service. Where-
upon Reay was bitterly narked, and said cross
things about the General. Since Reay went ’Orae
with the Jubilee picnickers, the readers of the
evening sheet he runs have had to endure many
splashes of ultra-jingo slush, such as is talked by
the lower-class English Tory prints. Indeed,
Reay’s Imperialism has been as nauseous as the
Herald’s grammar is atrocious.
Dear Bulletin. —Notable that the slowest
persons to move against the Boers have been men
whom the Queen delighted to honor. General
Butler, who refused to send Her Gracious the
Outlanders’ petition, is a special favorite whose
marriage with Miss “ Roll-Call ’’-Thompson the
Queen personally promoted. Lord Wolseley, who,
after four months’ notice, is not yet ready to
despatch an army corps to the Cape,' is the only
man who has ever been permitted to propose a
toast at the Queen’s table in her own presence,
said toast being the health of that overwhelming
military genius the Duke of Connaught.—E.
[Maybe ; but Wolseley is notoriously disliked by
Her Gracious, for all that.]
An idyllic place is Tam worth (N.S.W.).
The other day Father Ryan, the local R C. priest,
departed this life, and on the following Sunday,
at evening service, the Anglican pastor, Ven.
Archdeacon Lewis said :
1 desire to preface my sermon to-night by a reference
to the loss which our Roman Catholio fellow-citizens have
suffered in the death of their much-loved parish priest
The Very Rev. Patrick D. Ryan, whose death on last Fri-
day—the Feast of St. Michael and All Angels-over-
shadowed the life of this town, was a man who was not
only endeared to his own people, but also commanded
the esteem of all who knew him. As a brother in the
holy ministry of the Church of Christ, I pay my tribute
to the memory of a fellow-worker in the kingdom of our
Lord, who was also a generous-hearted man, and a genial,
sunny influence in the community. Our sympathy goes
with his sorrowing friends, and we—l am sure 1 may say
it—add our best hopes to theirs for the eternal peace and
refreshment of the departed.
If the Tamworth clergy are all like the priest who
is dead and the Archdeacon who uttered the
above-printed In Mernoriam, and the people take
after their shepherds, Tamworth must be a plea-
sant and really Christian place, and well worth
living in.

Memo, for Australia’s youngest Governor:
Gov. Hampden, in his last month of office, said :
“ I think a Governor of N.S.W. is expected to
speak too much. Trying to meet this expectation
he often speaks amiss. It would be better for
public and Governor if this expectation did not
exist. And, if it continue to exist, it would be
better for a Governor to resist than to comply on
all occasions. What can a Governor know about
half the things he speaks on ? ”
President Lackey, of N.S.W. Leg. Coun-
cil, now entering his 70th year, is the third lay-
man to hold that position. Murray and Hay
preceded him. The four remaining Presidents,
going back to the time of Responsible Govt.—
Wentworth, Burton, Plunkett and Stephen—
were lawyers. Lackey has been a J.P. for 48
years. He had large Leg. Assembly experience
before going to the Upper House, having been
elected for Parramatta nearly 40 years ago. He
was twice Chairman of Committees, and twice
Works Minister. The appointment to the Presi-
dency of N.S.W. Council is in the hands of the
Ministry—a sore point with Councillors, and one
which has stirred them to prepare Bills time and
again giving them the right to elect a man from
their own body ; but nothing has resulted.
Curiously parallel with the Dreyfus case is
that of the British naval officer who after-
H from the English alphabet?” roared a
State school-teacher. No one has ever since heard
’U ngry publicly drop this letter : he now always
pauses before a word commencing with H, and
takes care to get it out at all risks.
Holy James Balfour, M.L.C. (Vic.), who
was lately pulled through a severe illness by the
prayers of many Presbyterians, landed in Aus-
tralia in ’52 as agent for Edinburgh ale and
various other strong Scotch drinks. He became a
partner in James Henty and Co. (Melb.), and
eventually retired from that respectable old wine
and spirit firm with a fortune. Jimmy’s exasper-
ating piety grew much worse after he abandoned
the barrel business and took to financial pursuits.
In his present character of ex-busted-companies
director and high cockalorum of the Council of
Churches he most ungratefully heaps damnation
on the liquor-traffic which enriched him.

quainted with every man in his wide-spread con-
stituency—the Hume. He is, therefore, expected
to shake hands with and address a few words to
eaoh, otherwise, “ Bill ” Lyne will be accused of
“big head.”' Before addressing a meeting, how-
ever large, in his electorate, he makes it a point
to clutch the outstretched hands of everybody
present. It happens always, however, that a few
electors arrive late, and hurry in while the
Premier’s eloquence is in full blast. He is not
blind to their advent, however, and stops short
with: “Just a moment, ladies and gentlemen,
while I shake hands with iny sturdy supporter
Mr. Dingbat, of Erog-hollow. He has come a
wards became the famous Lord Dundonald,
and who, early in the present century, was
imprisoned, fined, and disgraced through the
machinations of Admiral Lord St. Vincent;
his offence being that he had drawn incon-
venient attention to the fact that St. Vin-
cent had been rewarded for an action in
which he took no part. For 40 years Coch-
rane suffered under the stigma of a false
charge ; but when at last he succeeded in
getting his case revived, he was exonerated
and rehauilitated. And remembering that
the officials concerned in his scandalous

affair, we shouldn't be so ready to shriek

Melbourne University once possessed a
cat much addicted to sleeping about in chairs,
and once a Professor of great tonnage, having
occasion during lecture to sit down, was startled
by a muffled yell. Springing to his feet he de-
manded the name of the student who had made
such an unseeirdy sound. Then he heard a gasp
behind him, and, turning round, was horrified to
behold a badly-squashed cat breathing its last.
Tom Mandeno Jackson, the Maoriland
tenor now in the front rank of English vocalists,
is the son of “ Sam ” Jackson, head of one of the
oldest firms of Auckland solicitors. “Sam ” was
sent for in a hurry one morning by a ragged old
person named Costley who was at the point of
death. “What have you got to leave?” asked
“ Sam ” perfunctorily. “More than a hundred
thousand,” gasped Costley, and the astonished
lawyer there and then drew the will which gave
over .£1:30,000 to various Auckland charities.
Sam Jackson’s brother, Major Jackson, was, after
Von Tempsky, the most noted of the plucky
“colonials ” who officered the Forest Rangers, a
corps which covered itself with gore and glory in
the Maori war of the early sixties. Major Jackson,
who once sat in M.L. Parliament, was mysteriously
lost overboard a few years ago during a voyage
between Auckland and Wellington.
Premier Turner relates that when he was
made a member of the Munro Govt., and attended
his first Cabinet meeting, the late Sergeant Mills
(then Premier’s orderly) refused to let him in.
The sergeant had never seen him before, and
wouldn’t believe that he had business with the
Cabinet. Turner (of course) hadn’t got a card, so
he had to wait in the draughty corridor till Mills
could see Munro. Sergeant M. reported: “There’s
a shabby man in a bowler hat outside who wants
to see you, sir.” “What’s his name?” said the
Premier. “ Says his name is Turner, sir.”
“ That’s the new Minister ; show him in at once.”
Turner had only been a few months in Parliament
then, but he had distinguished himself over the
Local Govt. Bill. He knew more about the sub-
ject than anyone else in the House, and though
but a private member at the time, he drafted
many of the most important clauses in the mea-
sure.
This story of the late J. B. Patterson has
never been published before. While he was Vic.
Premier, a member, still in the Assembly, attacked
him bitterly. Another member, who relates the
story, sympathised with Patterson, and said,
“ So-and-so has not behaved like a gentleman in
this matter.” “Gentleman!” roared the ex-
butcher, “Why, sir, did you ever see him eat?”
Anyone who had ever seen the said honorable
member eat —or rather, stoke his face —will admit
that Patterson hit him off in a phrase.
THOMAS SUFFIELD.
(Died, at Summer Hill, N.S.W., 4th October, 1899.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pause, Stranger, here and kneel—
The place of Suffleld’s sleep 1
Frenchmen for him may feel
Whilst we for Dreyfus weep.
Why ask for justice here ?
Take mercy, pardon free
Else thy full sentence bear—
Justice is not for thee 1
For Dreyfus, far away,
The world demanded all.
We hearkened, day by day,
Nor heard our brother’s call.
The time for help has passed,
And after years of toil,
Thy due reward thou hast—
Six feet of alien soil !

Aboriginalities.
A medico discourses to The Bulletin re
bushmen’s town-aoquired colds :
The experience of Dr, Martin, of Adelaide, anent bush-
men who develope influenza on arriving- in town (2/9/’99)
is shared by most city practitioners. In Melb. the Cup is
always associated with a rush of wheezv sons of the soil
who hoarsely curse their luck in the intervals of sneezing’
me explanation seems to lie in the fact that continued
residence in a locality confers a sort of immunity, more
or less complete, against the germs inhabiting that par-
ticular locality, and a change of station brings the subject
into contact with a new set of organisms who are not so
r ~ < i a oc . curs analogy with mosquitoes.
Another explanation (possibly more practical) is that in
he purer country air there is less opportunity for the
development of infective organisms than in rhe vitiated
atmosphere of cities and towns. From certain blessed
portions of the earth (writer has a cold now) influenza
and its attendant curses are absent. The island of St.
h fV 8 - a case, for there the yearly provision-
Doat brings, in addition to its usual cargo, a consignment
or catarrhs that sets the whole population running at the
nose. Writer knows of a case where the visit of a man-
or-war to one of the Orkney islands thus laid up
almost the whole population, although there was
no case on board. In the Arctio regions there are no
resident germs of the sort —nor of any injurious kind pro-
bably. Nansen and his companion did not suffer until
they reached the company of men, and then the pair
were ‘bunged up” for weeks. Several other alleged
explanations recur—damp sheets, for instance, but this
oannot account for every case. Some bushmen will not
use sheets at all in town for this reason. A possible ex-
planation strongly insisted on by a certain Australian
surgeon is that the voyager takes his annual wash and
change before setting out, and the draughts of the jour-
ney acting on the unprotected skin lower his vitality.
There is probably something in this, but the rarity of
habitually-washed oountry visitors makes investigation
difficult. At any rate, it is a useful tip for the bush
denizen to remember.

“ Sym.” : At Castlemaine (Vic.) recently
many pigs died of some obscure disease. The
local hog-raisers used to dress them and cut their
throats, so as to make them appear freshly killed.
Saw one trying to sell to a Chow a pig which had
been thus treated. “Me just kill ’im, John, you
see im throat cut?” “Me savee,” says John,
im die, then you killum.”

The other day at Mt. Lyell Co.’s aerial
tram, a couple of buckets got caught and stopped
the whole concern. No other way being practic-
able, an walked out on the wire a
distance of 200 yards, hanging by the top wire,
and after emptying half-a-ton of ore out of each
bucket he replaced them in proper position. As
he walked out he had to bind the two steel cables
together at intervals, and on returning had to
undo the fastenings again. He was out on the
wires for over two hours, and a slip would mean a

One of the machines sent to the recent
Melb. Agricultural Show was forwarded by a
demi-semi crank from an up-country town. It
was an utter impossibility that claimed utility as
a plough and several domestio utensils. It was a
good joke only to the experts, but the inventor was
confident of success. Soon after their deoision
had been conveyed to him his corpse was found at
the bottom of a neighboring waterhole.

Th o ro is at Meningoort Station (West.
Vio.) a pet emu whioh has run for years in the
horse-paddock. The emu has chummed up
thoroughly with the horses, but quickly resents
the intrusion of newcomers. The other day a new
pony was put in the paddock, and the emu spent
some hours in chasing and kicking at it. Pony
had eventually to be removed, or it would have
been worried to death.
X.: Re snakes (B. 22/9/’99). Two anec-
dotes for McCooey : Some years ago I was
out quailshooting when my dog, a pointer
jumped over a tiger-snake on a track. As
he passed across I noticed the snake make a
swift, striking motion towards the dog,
whose action in leaping did not occupy more
than one second of time. I thought no more
of the incident until, missing my pointer
about an hour afterwards, I walked back
and found him almost dead. He died half-
an-hour after I found him. The late Dr.
Valentine, of Ross, Tas., had a live snake
brought to him,. which he imprisoned in a
box. While trying to examine the reptile
m its captive state, it bit the doctor on his
hand through a crack in the box. Was
this biting or striking ? Writer remembers a
case of snake-bite (or strike) when a tiger
fastened on to a boy’s coat sleeve. The boy
tried for some time to shake him off, and <
ultimately was freed by a man who pulled
the reptile away by his tail. But the reason
of the snake’s fixture was not because he was
hanging on by both jaws—biting in fact—but
that after striking his fangs became entan-
gled in the woollen fibres of the coat, which
was proved afterwards by examination. If
you menace a Tasmanian snake with a stick
he will strike it with a forward and down-
ward motion, but never grip it. As I have
slaughtered, by rough computation, about
3000 snakes during my sojourn in the bush,
and held post-mortems on half of them, also
met with and watohed the habits of as many
more, I may be excused for disagreeing with
McCooey, and oalling Boyd, F.R.G.S.,
brother. By the way, a small stick inserted
into a dead snake’s mouth behind the fangs
will, on being pulled horizontally outwards,
appear to ereot them; but I am not posi-
tive that the snake does so at will. I leave
that to McCooey.

“Yarrum” : Tongarra, in Illawarra dis-
trict, means “bread-plain.” Darkies used to
father seeds there from whioh they made bread.
>on’t know where the “ plain ” comes in, though,
as there is not one within 50 miles. Possible
that the hills there are comparatively recent up-
heavals. Saddleback—seven miles from Tongarra
—was once an active volcano, and the Kiama
blue-metal deposits were ejected therefrom by
eruptions, with long-time intervals between, as, in
places, stratified rocks have been formed over the
igneous deposit. The Shellharbour deposit was a
branch diverted from the main stream of lava by
infringement upon Wallaby Hill, between Saddle-
back and the ocean. The crater of Saddleback is
discernible enough. Must have been queer geo-
logical “goings on” in the Ulawarra district a
few thousand years ago, because in some places
the carboniferous strata are right on top. Whab
sorb of a shove from below must they have had ?

No wonder Wilcannia (away back N.S.W.)
wails unceasingly for the extension of the railway
from Cobar, with a view to its ultimate con-
tinuation as far as Broken Hill. The other week
four or five steamers with their barges crept up
the Darling on a “fresh.” Chaff (the whole
country is dismally bare, and the working horses
on many stations had to be fed) dropped in a few
nlays from <£l6 10s. to ,£6 a ton, and flour from
.£lB to .£lO. And prices had been higher than
that during a few |weeks previously, when the
track from Broken Hill was closed to all but
camel teams.

“Shaker”: Re astonishing statement
(8.j_22/7/’99) that “no Australian snake is really
deadly, in the same sense that the rattlesnake
and cobra are, to a healthy adult.” Have had no
experience with rattlesnake or cobra, and have
only witnessed the effects of Australian snake-bite
on three healthy adults—who all promptly died,
despite, in one case, immediate professional treat-
ment. However, we have in the Gulf country a
brown snake which will kill a healthy horse inside
80min. every time, and if he is not really deadly
in the same sense, etc., then that other snake is
an undesirable neighbor. And that’s a fact !

“Huia ” : All nonsense about the physical
inferiority of N.S.W. pedagogues being due to in-
ferior food when young (“ Dominie ” B. 30/9/’99).
Barney and Tim, with similar accommodation
and tucker, are not weaklings, and as for the age,
by 20 the frame is usually already set. The real
causes are neglect of physical training and want
of pure air.

“ A.G.” : Had just ridden into town, very
wet and cold, and pulled up for a whisky at
the first “mill” I came to. In the bar was a
draggled female. I can’t think why I asked
her to drink, but she breasted the bar with me.
Then a rough-looking fellow entered and used
some swear-words. Asked him to be more parlia-
mentary in the presence of a lady, but he only
cursed more and asked what the ■— I was
doing with his wife, anyhow. Then she said
something nasty, and he struck her. Gave him
one on the jaw and was just picking him up when
I was struck on the back of the head with a thick-
bottomed tumbler. Came to my senses 24 hours
later in hospital, with shorn and bandaged head !
But what killed me was that the creature I had
defended called to ask “how the young gintle-
man was gettin’ on.”

At a N.S.YV. coast town recently an
American black turned up at the pub., and called
for a whisky. Said Bung : “ Sorry, ole man, but
can’t serve aboriginals.” The indignant “Mr.
Johnsing ” departed and returned with a police-
man who said : “Here, give this man what he
requires; can’t you see that he’s not an
aboriginal?” “No, I can’t,” said Bung.
“Aboriginal mean’s ‘ black,’ an’ this here feller’s
as black as the ace o’ spades ! ”

“Silly B.” : Re ’roo-fights (“Alia,” 8.,
9/9/’99), I once saw a set-to between two “old-
men ” kangaroos on the side of a small hill. They
sparred and ‘ ‘ stopped ” almost like two men, and
it was interesting to note the way each would
keep edging round to get the uphill advantage.
When, some years ago, Illustrated Sydney
News offered a prize for the best picture of an
Australian subject, it was, I think, won by Mr.
Blagdon Chambers, with “Peace” and “War,”
“ War ” being represented by two kangaroos hav-
ing a stand-up fight; and there oan be little
doubt that the artist had seen such an encounter
himself.

Re the statement frequently made that
before the introduction of the white man’n tobacco
there was no such thing as smoking among the
Australian blacks, and that no aboriginal pipes
have ever been found, a correspondent of a
northern paper writes :
The Messrs. Jardine, In their account of N.Q., remark
that before the introduction of tobacco the native biaeks
were extremely partial to smoking the probably narcotic
leaves of a lartfe spreading Eugenia—allied to the Lilly-
pilly of N.S.W. and Victoria. The pipe was fashioned
upon a aigan tic scale from a hollow bamboo, about 24 ft.
in length and, “as thick as a quart bottle.” The leaf
was placed in a bowl of funnel shape, and a party con-
gregated to smoke in common. By blowing through a
hole at one end of the tube, the pipe, supplied from the
bowl, was filled and then passed round to the assembled
smokers in turn, each inhaling and swallowing as great a
quantity as possible. Frequently they smoked them-
selves insensible, and the travellers relate having seen a
man lying helpless from the effects of a single dose.
MacGillivray, in the “ Voyage of H.M.S. Rattlesnake,”
describes the Cape York method of tobacco-smoking in-
troduced from the islands of Torres Straits, the Papuans
and native Australians alike being smokers. The pipe
was similar to that above described. Assembled in a
ring, the company handed it round, each inhaling deeply,
and then slowly exhaling the fumes. Tobacco was
“choka,” and the writer mentions that several times he
has seen a blackfellow semi-comatose after a single in-
halation, with perspiration pouring from him ; and that
he himself—an accustomed smoker —felt faint and sick
after once trying this manner of consuming the fragrant
weed. At Dalrymple Island, in Torres Straits, a similar
custom obtained (see “Voyage of H.M.S. Fly").
The enormous pipe had a large opening at the mouth-
piece, and a small hole near the opposite end in which
was fixed a conical wooden bowl, made air-tight bj T wrap-
ping a leaf around it. The tobacco being placed in the
bowl, the native sucked until the bamboo stem was filled
with smoke. Then, removing the bowl, he placed his
hand loosely upon the aperture, swallowed a draught of
smoke himself and handed the pipe along to his neighbor.

“Fancy F.” : Re efficacy of cursing bul-
locks (“Nemo,” 8., 26/8/’99). I see much of bul-
lock-teams hauling totara logs—the heaviest
timber of the M.L. bush—but I never hear any-
thing stronger than “ damn ” from the bullockies.
Each animal knows its own name, and, when
specially addressed, will make a special effort.
Beyond that there is only whip-cracking. I have
never yet seen the lash laid on. In mud-holes or
bad crossings it is interesting to watch the
animals pause to breathe, and then, on their
“own,” make a united effort to “ rush ” the nasty
place.
• * * •
“R.E.F.”: Recently saw a curlew pro-
tecting its nest against the advances of young
cattle, which were ringing it round. Every time
the steers came too close the bird fluttered up
among the horns of the leaders with an angry
squawk ; result—a rush round and gambol by the
curious cattle, followed by a fresh advance. Sat
on the fence for an hour watching this. Takes a
good deal to weary a bush-naturalist of roosting
on a fence, if he is paid by the week and his boss
is on the spree in town.

Gilbert Probyn Smith states one side of
the criminal libel argument:—
Reoently John Norton caused me to be arrested for
criminally libelling him. The case lasted for eight days ;
cost to the Crown, £lOOO. I had no coin. Norton had,
and the Crown found him more. After eight days’ trial,
jury disagreed, ten being in favor of an acquittal, and
two for a conviction. Since the disagreement the Crown
has filed a nolle prosequi. This is my point. If a man
has a damaged reputation, why should the Crown, at the
expense of the tax-payer, defend it. [Or put it this way:
if a man of good reputation is criminally libelled by a
scoundrel, why should the Crown not prosecute the man
who steals his reputation, just as it proseoutes the man
who steals his purse ?—That’s the other side, without
reference to any particular case.—Ed. B.]. In Victoria
there is no such thing as criminal libel. The complainant
has to go to a grand jury, and if a true bill is found, he
has to pay all expenses. In any case the Crown expended
a large sum of money in trying to prove Norton’s inno-
oency. I ask now, why should not the Govt, pay my
expenses too ?
The portrait herewith represents Miss
Alice Simmons, a pupil of the late Signor Cecchi’s,
and one of the many singers from Bendigo, where
all the good Australian singers are supposed to
come from. But Miss Simmons came from
Bendigo at a time when a girl who came from
Bendigo had to travel on her merits, like a girl
who came from any other place. That was before
the Castles Boom had created an impression that
living in Bendigo was in itself evidence of having
a voice, and that it wasn’t necessary for a golden
city girl to prove that she had a voice to be a great
singer—she simply was a great singer, till the
public proved that she hadn’t any voice at all.
Miss Simmons proved that she could sing
in the common vulgar way—by doing it. In
one of Williamson’s Melbourne pantomimes,
a year or two ago, The Bulletin had
great pleasure in pointing out that she was
the only lady in the crowd who could
warble to any extent worth mentioning. Her
principal ditty was thereupon promptly relegated
from the first act to the remote tail-end of the
second, whereby it was presumed that the im-
ported star was feeling cross. The panto, in ques-
tion, if The Bulletin remembers aright, was
“ Beauty and the Beast.” It does not remember
who was Beauty, but the Beast was the person
who shifted Miss Simmons to the far end of the
programme. Since then Miss S. has been a credit
and an ornament to Rickards’ show and various
other shows, and she is now going to London with
a lengthy engagement at the Palace. Probably
the vessel she sails in will break its shaft, as is
the regular process nowadays, and she will live for
a few months on tinned fish out of the cargo, as
she drifts on the bosom of the deep. But the
young lady from Bendigo reokons she will chance
it.

Paul Kruger.
Deep mournful eyes that seek the ground
The devious path to trace ;
The giant form of Lincoln crowned
By Cromwell's grosser face ;
Coarse, rustic garb, of uncouth out,
That masks each mightj’ limb ;
Its shapeless folds the ready butt
Of Europe’s jesters trim.
So much the crowd can see ; the rest
Asks critios clearer-eyed :
So rough a scabbard leaves unguessed
How keen the blade inside ;
The trenohant will, the subtle brain
So strangely domed to wage
With Destiny’s still climbing main
The hopeless war of Age.
His kindred are a rugged brood
That nurse a dying fire :
The sons of Calvin’s bitter mood,
And sterner than their sire.
By faith through trackless deserts steered,
Lost miles of lonely sand,
Far from the intruding world they feared,
They found their Promised Land.
By suoh stern guardians tutored well
His Spartan childhood grew.
The wind-trail of the fleet gazelle,
The lion’s path he knew ;
The camp surprised at dawn, the rush
Of feet, the crackling smoke.
When on the sleeping laager’s hush
The sudden Kaffir broke.
Nay, once, ’tis said, when Vaal in flood
Had barred the hunters’ way,
And ’mid its swollen current stood
A wounded buck at baj';
While some before the brute drew back,
And some before the wave ;
Striding that torrent’s foaming track
The mercy-stroke he gave.
A stream more rapid and more wide
His strength has stemmed since then ;
Called from the plodding team to guide
The starker wills of men ;
Chance-’prenticed to so new a trade,
Unlettered and unschooled,
The clod-bred clownish peasant made,
No less, a realm, and ruled.
Yet though that realm he still sustains
Against an Empire’s might;
And with untiring skill maintains
The so unequal fight;
He buys his victories all too dear
Whose foes have Time for friend ;
Each fatal triumph brings more near
The inevitable end.
Hapiy the hoarse-voiced guns must close
The long debate at last,
Ere the young Future can compose
Its quarrel with the Past:
Natheless, our England unashamed
May greet a foeman true
Of her own stubborn metal framed :
B'or She is iron too.
Edward Sydney Tyler,
“The Spectator."
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.
A.B. : The Queensland majority of 7500 for the
Bill, out of a total of 69,000 recorded votes, is equivalent
to more than 20,000 had the gross total of votes recorded
been the same as in N.S.W., viz., 190,000. As a majority
of 20,000 would have effectually silenced all opposition
hi N.S.W. Assembly, it was a foregone conclusion that a
7500 majority would suffice to carry the Enabling Bill
through the Q. Legislature Frank : You say we “dish
up trivialities for imbeciles to grin at.” All right—grin
away 1 Colo : All wecan say is that the N.S.W. public
will be glad to see carried-out Attorney-General Wise’s
avowed intention of rectifying the scandalous wrong by
which a number of civil servants were swindled of their
pensions by being retired just before attainment of the
qualifying age of 60 years (with 16 years of service). .

Mr. Henry Peck : “ Well, Mariar, I’ve
enlisted for the Transvaal to fight
for the dear old flag. We must be
separated for a time.

Three months ago English papers were
constructing Cabinets at Johannesburg. And all
the time Kruger was being assured that Boer
independence wasn’t threatened !
N.S.W. ex-Treasurer M’Millan will be a
candidate for the Federal Parliament, and being
now a man of leisure, means to devote his life to
Federal politics—in the Freetrade interest.
The two Sydney morning papers are
e vidently more or less ashamed of the Transvaal
business ; the D.T. in its Yes-No, a-bit-both-vvays,
O utis-column way, takes no pains to conceal the
fact. But the evening papers—they fairly howl
for Blood, like midnight dingoes outside a bush
killing-yard.
The Sydney telephone tunnels have been
» stand-by for more than one N.S.W. ex-Labor
M.L.A. Bavister has just lost his refuge through
his own seeking. Williams, Labor-member for
Hunter River in 1891-’94, has succeeded Tolley,
who has received Bavister’s appointment. W. J.
Toomey,; ex-sec. to the A.W. Union in Young,
was also on the telephone tunnels, but has now
been transferred to the Public Works Dept, and
suitably provided for.
“ Sandgroper ” writes :
You have no idea of the hatred borne to the Forrest
crowd on these (W.A.) fields. Even ten-foot-drop
Wittennoom is not more disliked than the boodling
Forrest crowd. Lefroy is pitied and despised, but it
would be practically unsafe for Big John to take a trip
to the Boulder. It would need little to cause a serious
riot here over the alluvial business. Forrest has prac-
tically disfranchised the goldfields again, and though
every man, woman and child on the goldfields favors
Federation, we can see no hope of carrying it except
through separation, making Esperanee our capital, or
joining South Australia.
The advantages of being an aboriginal in
Westralia. About three years ago Lilimarra, a
nigger under sentence of death, bit his way
through the walls of Her Majesty’s valuable stone
gaol, and escaped. A few weeks back the same
nigger, or one very like him (there is a consider-
able sameness about the numerous Westralian
nigger) was captured in the north-west, and on
tbe assurance of the local police that he is the
same old nigger orders have been sent up by the
Forrest Government to resume the hanging where
it left off three years ago.
S.A. Leg. Council only approved of the
proposed Boer-murdering contingent by the cast-
ing vote of the President, and the Assembly would
have rejected the motion had Premier Kingston
not practically pledged Parliament beforehand.
Laborites solidly opposed in both chambers. King-
ston was twitted, and apparently not without
reason, with being at heart opposed to the scheme.
A strange feature of the affair is the fact that only
by energetic canvassing has S.A. secured the
necessary 125 men. In the first week less than 50
men volunteered. Good.
One of Brisbane Telegraph's chief objec-
tions to the influence of the North in deciding the
recent referendum was that there were so many
aliens in the North that its vote ought to be dis-
regarded. One would imagine from this that the
aliens actually voted, and the Telegraph is quite
capable of maintaining that they did. It may
comfort that discontented organ, however, to
recollect that aforesaid aliens are mainly there
through the corruption and ratting of Southern
politicians, often aided and abetted *>y that
valorous anti-Federal organ itself.
Downes Carter, M.P., is bitterly opposed
to the Vic. Immigration Restriction Bill, because
it threatens to shut out a class of men, Hindoos
and others, who would “ teach our people early
rising, industry and thrift.” Which means that
“our people ” should work as long as the coolie,
slave as hard, and live on as little, for the advan-
tage of gentlemen like Downes Carter, who doesrpt
rise any too early himself. The capitalistic indi-
vidual who starts preaching “thrift” to Mel-
bournites at this date, cannot have intelligence
enough to grasp the amazing magnitude of his
own gall.
Brisbane Telegraph stated, with due
deliberation (in its leading article of 11/10/’99)
that the passing of the Federal Address through
Queensland Leg. Council by 16 votes to 9 was “ a
sorry spectacle” :
But let Us look at the situation. The Chamber has 41
members ; 25 were present to vote ; 15 were absent; the
President was in the chair. It is known well enough
that the President is entirely opposed to the proposed
union • he has said that he would not sit if elected as a
senator. What then did the vote of last night amount
to? Eliminate Mr. Gray, and the number of “ contents ”
is 15 To the “non-contents” add the President, the
Chairman of Committees, who walked out, and Mr.
Turner, absent through illness, who would have voted
against Federation, and we get 12 to 15. Add Mr. Gray
to ihe I*>, and he should have voted with them, and we
get IB to 15 in a House of 41, voting on a measure in-
volving the fate of Queensland. We sa.v without a par-
ticle of feeling, in cold blood, it was a sorry spectacle.
This off-hand way of adding to the “ noes ” three
men who didn’t vote at all, and taking Mr. Gray
from the “yeses” and adding him also to the
«s n oes,” and thereby making it 15 to 13 instead of
16 to 9, is interesting. “Eliminate Mr. Gray ’’
sounds well, and if you only eliminate enough
people anything may happen. But there is only
a chastened joy, after all, in showing, when the
fiodit is over, that if the thing had been that
wasn’t, and if the thing hadn’t been that was,
then the thing chat is wouldn’t be.
Great sticklers as Tas. politicians profess
to be for constitutional practice and things like
that, their desire to assist Britain in her Hour of
Greed jumps even that fence. The Braddon
Ministry fell, and Premier Braddon wearily
acknowledged defeat at nine o’clock on Friday
night. Under ordinary circumstances Stafford
Bird would have been sent for on Saturday morn-
ing, but an arrangement was come to between the
leaders of the Assembly parties that Braddon
should not formally abdicate until the following
Wednesday, thus allowing Parliament one even-
ing (Tuesday) to decide without loss of precious
time to dispatch a Tas. contingent of Boer-
slaughterers. It was Braddon’s last official act —■
and his worst. Then he went to sleep with his
predecessors.
A. B. Piddington on the Transvaal situa-
tion :
As to the duty ot supporting the Imperial Cabinet,
right or wrong, and volunteering Australians needlessly
as a symbol of Imperial unity, that is just the same
doctrine in essence as was preached when Burke and
Chatham opposed the popular war of Britain against her
own American colonies, and when Bright opposed the
still more popular Crimean war, in which England main-
tained the infamous Ottoman rule, which two years ago
gave us the Armenian massacres. It is in essence the
same doctrine as that which sacrificed Dreyfus to “ the
honor of the army,” and it bodes ill for Australia, if for
such a doctrine, she pushes her way into sharing a war
which involves the violation of treaty, the spoliation of a
white race, and the imposition of British rule upon a
Christian people who do not desire it.
Leaving out the “ Christian ” aspect (it is doubt-
ful if there is much Christianity on either side)
that just expresses the case. A section of the
French people held that Dreyfus should be
sacrificed, however unjustly, to prevent disunion
in France. And the people who preach that Aus-
tralia should wade in Boer blood, be the quarrel
just or unjust, to show the “solidarity” (good old
word) of the empire, and to promote that good old
“ solidarity,” and to prevent the growth of dis-
union between Britain and Australia, are in
exactly the same situation—except that they want
to kill a lot of men ; the French solidarity crowd
only wanted to imprison one man.
Victorian Speaker Mason made an exhi-
bition of himself in his Jingoistic desire to get the
motion for sending troops to South Africa passed
without adverse criticism. He at once caught up
the impatient, slaughtering spirit of the House,
and put every obstacle in the wav of those who
had the courage to oppose the scheme. His efforts
to stifle discussion, however, only resulted in his
placing himself in a most humiliating position.
Little Maloney was speaking against the motion
in his usual, roundabout, jerky fashion, when
Mason pulled him up and said he was flippant and
irrelevant. Maloney answered that he was
not; whereupon the pompous Speaker had to
subside. But not for long. Maloney began
a quotation from the extracts of Joubert’s
address to the Queen published in the Times, and
Mason again pulled him up, saying that his quota-
tions were too long. This got Maloney’s back up,
and he retorted that if the Speaker knew his duty
he must be aware that there was no limit, to the
number or the length of a member’s quotations so
long as they were relevant. By this time the
House had begun to see bow unfairly Mason was
acting, and cheered Maloney’s retort ; while the
pompous one drew his furs up oyer his knees and
retired in anger to the far corner of his chair. It
is some time in Victoria since there was a Speaker
who barefacedly sided with the majority.
ON A LONESOME STREET .
What might occur any day, if the Sydney authori-
ties don't hurry up with that electric tram.!
So far as it has told, the Lyne Govt, will
drop the proposal to continue the locking of the
Darling River, and will, instead, when it gets the
coin, continue the railway from Cobar to Wil-
cannia, and perhaps to Broken Hill. Works
O’Sullivan has already broken into poetry with
regard to the Barrier trade and the Beautiful
Harbor, and the iron link that is to be between
them ; but the sooner the idea of competing with
the 300-mile line from B.H. to the S.A. seaports
is dropped the better. Wilcannia s position is
different ; yet so far as even that town is con-
cerned, rail carriage at remunerative rates would
be simply impossible so long as the Darling and
Murray were open —which is, on the average, say,
nine months in the year, though the last five or
six years would not show so good a record.
Westralian Premier Forrest : “ Why
should we adopt the one-man-one-vote system?
Hasn’t plural voting been in vogue in England
for generations ? Then what is good enough for
them is good enough for us.”
ALL THINGS ARE
BRITTLE.
THE PARK “HARD-UP" (loq.): “Bank broken;
heart broken; boot broken; and now blessed if
my damned pipe ain’t broken !”
The eagerly-proffered, unasked-for aid of
Australia to Britain in this Boer-butchering busi-
ness is just about the most cowardly, snobbish
piece of work on record. The Australian popu-
lace, left to itself, would emphatically pronounce
against interfering between the game 121 b. bull-
terrier which in defence of its own little bone has
boldly gripped the forefoot of the ever-hungry
4001 b. lion—at least, it wouldn’t interfere on behalf
of the lion ; Australia has simply been dragged
into this disgraceful affair by a lot of politicians
anxious to stand well with the lords and dukes
over the water. To help the Johannesburg
boodlers kill the gallant little Boer Republic is
just about as valorous an act as helping a 14st.
man to kick-in the ribs of his prostrate 7st. wife.
The narrow majorities with which, despite the
tricks of the British-official and British-greaser
brigade, the Boer-murder project has so far got
through some of the Australian legislatures, shows
clearly what would happen to the Ministries pro-
moting it had the electors time to reflect and
organise.
The Westralian constitution contains a
proviso to the effect that the Queen may, at her
own pleasure, and without any reference to the
local Parliament, cut Westralia up into two or
more provinces. No one seems to be certain
whether the Colonial Boundaries Act overrules
this proviso or not, but it is believed on
high authority that it doesn’t. At all
events the gold districts fully in-
tend, if the Forrest gang blocks
Federation much longer, to storm
the Imperial Parliament with a
monster petition for separation
into a new province with its
sea-outlet at Esperance. And if
their prayer is granted Perth and Bun-
bury will be left high and dry, and the
glory and revenue of Westralia will
be deader than the Caliphs of Bagdad.
“ Teddy ” Mulcahy, a glib Hobart
clothier, who vociferously declares that
the twelve-and-sixpenny tweed trousers
he sells are not made by sweated labor,
is to have the Lands and Works port-
folio in the new Tas. Ministry. For
years he has posed as faithful fol-
lower and champion apologist of the
Braddon regime, but stood from under
when the disastrous Miles was taken into
the Cabinet.
Speaking in the Commons in ’96, Cham-
berlain said that “a war in South Africa would
be one of the most serious wars that could possibly
be waged. It would be a long war, a bitter war,
and a costly war, and as I have pointed out
already, I believe generations would hardly be
able to blot out the memory of it. To go to war
with President Kruger, to enforce upon him
reforms in the internal affairs of State in which
Secretaries of State, standing in their place, have
repudiated all right of interference—that would
be a course of action whioh would be immoral! ”
And now !
Dear Bulletin. —You ask (B. 7/10/’99)
“ what any of us want to go shooting Boers ” for?
Well, the majority of us are not overflowing with
Imperial ardor, but, my God ! we want to get
away—anywhere—-out of this accursed country
where they advertise ( vide Sydney Daily Tele-
graph, 11/10/'99) like this :
Wanted, 2 young fellows for woolshed, 9s.
Man, garden and useful, Bs. ; and orchard-hand, 10s.
Young fellow, groom and milk, Bs.
Also, tell the man who has never been out of work
that I, too, have read those beautiful (?) lines,
“ Breathes there a man,” &c. —Volunteer.
The passing of the Federal address through
Queensland Leg. Council by 16 votes against 9,
and that, too, after only an hour or two’s debate,
was a hard blow to the Brisbane parochialists.
To the last, it was hoped that the Council would
either reject the address or accept it by an almost
invisible majority after long struggling, and its
acceptance by a vote of more than two-thirds of
the members present was a Great Shock.
Protection and Foreign-trade : Victoria, at
the end of 1898, had 286!) factories; N.S.W.,
2384. Victoria had 54,778 factory employes,
N.S.W. 52,518. Increase for the year : Victoria,
i 960 males and 117 females, total 2077 ;
N.S.W. increase, 340 males, 739 females, total
1079. Victoria has still far more female
workers than N.S.W. (14,147 against
7845), but the gap has diminished this
year.
Hackett, of the West Austra-
lian, the man who owns Bigjohn
Forrest and makes that enslaved mag-
nate run in the anti-Federal interest,
has, no doubt, got the usual griev-
ance of the newspaper-owner against
Federation. It will necessitate an
office and a Parliamentary reporting
staff in the Federal capital, and the
expenditure of tons of money annually
in salaries and telegraphic intelli-
gence. The Federal newspaper-owner is
probably sincere every time, for he is
voting against his apparent cash in-
terests. The anti-Federal one is pro-
bably a fraud every time—the man whose
convictions and whose pocket are of the
same opinion is mostly a fraud.

N.S.W. Premier Lyne has at least one
claim to the backing of every Democrat, lie has
been a consistent supporter of the early-closing
movement all his political life—probably its most
consistent supporter. Lyne is the Early-Closing
Premier, in fact. Reid in particular hopes that
he will close very early indeed.
The political programme of the Victorian
Labor-party has been revised and brought up to
date. It now stands thus :
1. One adult one vote.
2. The referendum to be applied to the settlement of
vexed public questions.
Progressive tax on land values, town and country,
without exceptions, exclusive of improvements.
4. The establishment of a Slate Hank.
5. The establishment of a Department of Labor wnn a
responsible Minister.
6. The establishment by law of courts of conciliation
and compulsory arbitration for t lie settlement of dispute*
between employers and employes.
7. The maintenance and perfection of the policy of I ro-
teotion, tlie adoption of the eight-hours system, a trades-
union wage in alt protected industries, and the abolition
of all revenue duties.
8. The establishment of a pension fund for aged per-
il. The sustaining of village settlements and the crea-
tion of a body of small landholders in contradistinction
to large ones.
10. Provision for enfranchisement of seamen and other*
following migratory occupations..
11. A cumulative tax on all incomes over £2OO per
J That with a view of preventing undesirable alien*
being brought into the colony by sea and land, a poll tax
of £lOO be imposed on Chinese and other Asiatics.
13. No further alienation of land.
14. Reform of the Upper House on the electoral basis
of the Legislative Assembly, with single electorates.
Fourteen planks, all sound ones, are quite enough
to go on with. The Queensland Labor-party lias
a platform of 40 planks, and the burden of carry-
ing so much timber all at once retards progress.
Any Labor-party that has 14 planks under its
arm and 26 more stored in the yard has plenty of
material for immediate needs.

IFIROSIE’IECTTTS
OF THE
Spring- Creek Gold Dredging Co-
NO LIABILITY.
Capital: £20,000, in 40,000 Shares of 10s. each.
Provisional Directors : Hon. DAVID HAM, M.L.0.; THOMAS HARVEY, Esq.
Consulting Engineer: J. W. H. PIPER, Esq., 0.E., &c., &c.
Bankers: ENGLISH, SCOTTISH and AUSTRALIAN BANK LTD., MELBOURNE.
Solicitors: CLEVERDON and FAY, 125 Queen-street, MELBOURNE.
Brokers- (MELBOURNE) JOHN S. REID, Queen-street; E. L. and 0. BAILLIEU, Collins-street-.
(SYDNEY) SOUTAR and HOARE, Pitt-street.
Manager (pro tem.) : PERCY P. COOK, 375 Collins-street, MELBOURNE.
Objects of the Company.
This Company is being formed to acquire, dredge, and work a good area situated on Spring Creek, and
known as Novvland’s Backwater, New England District, New South Wales, consisting of 100 acres or thereabouts,
of gold-bearing alluvial flats.
Report by JOHN W. H. PIPER, of the firm of Cut-ten Brothers, which speaks for itself :
“ I have visited and examined your dredging property on Nowland’s Backwater, New England, New South
Wales, and beg to report as follows :
“The property is situated about 25 miles from Guyra Railway Station, near the main road between that
station and Kookrabookra. It embraces 100 acres or thereabouts of th# creek and flat. The wash to be worked
is fine, being a granite-sand deposition. In a few places a little overburden exists, but there should be no dif-
ficulty’in dealing with it. , . . , ~ . j .* *u
“The bottom consists of a decomposed granite, and can be easily cleaned, up. At the time of my visit there
was sufficient water running for all purposes connected with dredging operations.
“I had no opportunity of testing the depth of the ground, but, judging from appearances and from informa-
tion gathered from local residents, I do not apprehend that the depth of the auriferous wash will exceed 16ft.
below the water-level. Many attempts, lam told, have been made to bottom on the fiats, but the water was
ever present. A portion of the banks have been worked by ordinary methods, with, I understand, good results,
but none of the workings have been conducted on a very extensive scale, mainly on account of the difficulty of
getting water at a sufficient elevation for slucing, the country having little natural fall. A party of Chinamen,
however, have worked, and are still working, in a very primitive way, an area on the left bank of the creek.
One of the party informed me that he made, on an average, about £2 per week, he having been on the ground
for about 11 years. , „ , „ , - ~ , ~, ,
“of the value of the auriferous contents there is no doubt. I saw three dishes of stuff washed, which yielded
about 9 grains—an average of 3 grains to the dish (about 15dwts. to the ton), but it must be understood that this
wash was taken from the bottom, where the same is exposed on the bank; however, it goes to prove the general
auriferous character of the ground. I also saw- many other prospects taken from different parts of the property, all
of which gave highly payable results. The gold is of a shotty character, and ‘good weighing.’ There is also a per-
centage of tin and gemstones, the latter being of no great commercial value. The tin will be recovered on the
tables'in the ordinary way. In conclusion, lam satisfied that the claim has been prospected sufficiently, and with
the evidence before me, I feel perfectly justified in asserting that your property will yield good returns, if properly
and intelligently worked.
“ There are no snags, growing timber, or large boulders on the whole area of your lease, and I understand the
creek is not subject to heavy floods, consequently dredging operations can be carried on without interruption.
“The wash being fine, and of no great dept-li, a constant quantity can be dealt with without the usual shocks
imparted to the gear by heavy and rough ground. The road is in fair condition, and I believe cartage is cheap.
There is abundance of firewood at hand. Suitable sawn timber, I understand, can be obtained locally at a cheap
rate. I would recommend a dredge of maximum capacity of 100 tons per hour. A dredge of this capacity would
cost about £5OOO. Your working expenses would be about £5O per week.
“ Yours faithfully,
“JOHN W. H. PIPER, M. Aus. 1., M.E., M. Am. 1., M.E.”
This valuable property will be worked for gold and tin by the modern and successful method of Bucket Dredging,
by which method gold to the value of one grain per ton—as stated by Mr. Jaquet, A.R.S.N., F.G.S., in his book issued
by the Mines Department of New South Wales- will cover all expenses, including management.
It is proposed to build a dredge on the bucket system, with a maximum capacity of treating one hundred (100)
tons per hour.
Allowing for the treatment of only half of the capacity of the dredge, say about 50 tons per hour, considering the
favorable nature of the ground to be worked this is a very moderate estimate. The dredge will t reat-7000 tons per week,
and on the lowest estimate a weekly gold yield of £350 may be looked for, and tin -.taking it at £BO per ton on the spot
—to the value of £62 per week, equal to a gross return of £412 per week, which, after deducting the working ex-
penses of £5O per week, leaves a net profit of £362 per week, or nearly £19,000 per annum.
With the above estimate of the profits, Mr. Piper concurs.
Special attention is drawn to the fact that this is all virgin ground which has never been bottomed owing to the
water, consequently the wash has never been depleted of its auriferous contents, but this will be entirely overcome
by the use of a dredge as proposed.
It will be noted that the ground to be worked is nearly all alluvial wash, there being very little overburden, only
a few inches in most places, and consequently the dredge will have little “ dead work ” to do.
It will also be noticed that the bottom being soft decomposed granite, the whole of the bottom wash can be
easily lifted by the dredge.
This property can be confidently recommended as a legitimate mining investment.
Financial.
To ensure ample working capital and speedily put the Spring Creek Gold Dredging Company upon a dividend
footing, the Vendors have decided to issue to the public 20,000 shares of 10s. each, payable 2s. Od. per share on appli-
cation, and 2s. 6d. on allotment; 20,000 shares fully paid up will be allotted to the Vendors in full payment of the
property.
The whole of the proceeds of the sale of shares will be placed to the credit of the Company, less brokerage and
flotation expenses.
As these shares will be simultaneously offered for subscription in Melbourne, early application is necessary.
Allotment will be made according to priority of application, the Directors having power to declare the list closed
at discretion.
