TWENTY-THIRD REPORT.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 271 h October, 1899.
The Directors have pleasure in placing in the Shareholders’ hands the annexed Balance Sheet and Statement of
Accounts to 30th September, duly audited and certified
The net profits (including £3040 10s. 9d. brought from last half-year) amount to £7,664 16 4
Out of which it is proposed
To pay the usual dividend at the rate of 3 per cent, per annum ... 2,250 0 0
And to carry forward the balance £5,414 16
Shareholders will be pleased to notice that the steady improvement at all points manifested in recent balance
sheets is still maintained. The directors, while cordially acknowledging the assistance received in the past, would
remind shareholders that a continuance of that assistance is necessary to ensure the same satisfactory progress in i he
future, and they trust, therefore, that unremitting efforts will be made to influence to the bank desirable business,
either in the shape of deposits or sound advances.
By order of the Board, H. T. WILSON, Manager.

The Caulfield Guineas, run in rain and mud
(uncommonly black mud, toof was true to ils old tradi-

where the splosh splashed thickest what time Treinarden
and Promontory were racing together in front of the
field. At the turn for home Treinarden began to get
awa.v from his co-leader, and then Scorn came along.
Given good gal I oping conditions, the favorite might have
been fast, enough to heat Treinarden -as it was, he made
up a lot of sticky ground and finished an imposing
second, Promontory third, and none of the others any-
where in particular. The Guineas race leaves one with
the impres-ion that Scorn has a boss chance in the Derby,
and Treinarden may go near it. Lancaster is probably a
“flat-catcher,” ami the handsome Gungadiu, who ran
bai 11v on Saturday, looks somewhat soft. The writer
prefers Parthian to the S.A. colt.

If Lochaber hadn’t been scratched for the Caul-
field Cup writer would have expected hint to run well,
and he would have started at fairly reasonable odds,
whereas the more popular fancies are already at a false
price, considering the history of the race. ’ Nothing is
worth backing for a Caulfield Cup at less than 10 to 1.
Last, year The Bulletin fancied a 10 to 1 chance in Reka.
Well, Iteka and half-a-dozen other horses came to awful
gri- f at the first turn, and the catastrophe spoilt the pio-
spects of some that didn’t actually fall.
The Chief and Merriwee have also had the pen

Gnullo, Mora, Scorn, La Carabine, Dewey, Blun-
denr, and Egypt the writer risks the opinion
that Dirk llammerhaiid and Town Clock are not
quite good enough at the weights, though S.A.
seems to he swearing by massive, slmwy Dirk. The mare
Gnullo is slippery, but 1A miles is a longer journey than
she likes. Blunderer and Egypt are possible winners
“on paper.” Mora, Scorn, Dewey, and La Carabine have
undeniable chances, as chances go in a bumping, fouling,
blocking race like Caulfield Cup. AH the same, this
doubtful prophet places

First N.S.W. two-year-old race of the season
was run at Rosehill on Saturday, and fell to Monaghan’s
colt, Gonaro. which started at 5 to 2, and performed
most promisingly. He had a lot of ground to make up
at the finish, and settled down to his lask more like a
veteran than a youngster. Gonaro hails from Tocal
(N.S.W.), and is by Medallion from Happy Moments.
A Sydney sporting-sheet, published on Satur-
day morning, tipped the first four winners at Rosehill on
Saturday.
* * • •
Major Thompson—representing British War
Dept. —is visiting N.S.W. with a commission to purchase
2000 horses, wanted for the Transvaal; and local horse-
breeders are considering how far they can put up prices
without losing the I usiness. There isn't much in horse-
breeding unless you can get about- ±l5 per head ; the ex-
penses are numberless, like ihe worries. Horse-transport
is an art in itself, and the thousands that have gone
to India have shown a big percentage of loss. The
biggest shipment to England was Galvayne’s 102
four years ago. He had bad luck crossing the
Bight, hut the rest of the trip only cost him half-a-
dozen. When yon do get to England the mat ket is worth
the pains. Lord Hindlip’s four averaged 1250 apiece
he nitisfrhave cleared JE3OO or ±4OO for his two months’
l rouble.

S. Australian enthusiasts in pigeon-homing are
busy breaking Melhonrne-to-Adelaide records. So far no
bird has got home on the day of its release, though
lately a couple tossed at 6.60 a.m. on Monday turned up
at 10.20 a.tn. on Tuesday. By the way, carrier pigeons
released by the Govt, lifeboat service took to Adelaide
the first official news of a recent wreck outside Port
Lincoln.

One recently taken weighed 4|lh.”

The League of Queensland Wheelmen lost illso
over the recent race-meeting, and the League is just
about ruined thereby. The cyclists did not advertise
freelv, and the whole sporting press, has risen as one out-
raged rag to remark that the omission was the.cause of
the downfall. Quite true. We (the Press) are a great
institution, and must be boosted up.
» * » *
Several of the Sydney Sailing Clubs postponed
Saturday’s fixtures out of respect to the memory of Mr.
George Hellings, who died suddenly during the week.
George Hellings was as well known about Sydney as
George Reid, as, in addition to having been identified
with Port Jackson aquatics since he was old enough to
handle a bailer, he sang a splendid comic soifg, and was
as much sought, after in one respect as the other. George
(erstwhile president of the old Canvas Dingy Club) came
of an aquatic family, his father, Mr. “Dick” Hellings,
having been the crack yacht-sailer of the harbor'in the
palmy days of the Xarifa, Australian, Minstrel, and
Magic. His brother “Jack,” now in England, is the
crack 100-yards swimmer, and the baby of the family,
Syd., knew no peer in Port Jackson as a rowing coxswain
when a youngster.
* * * •
Boat-sailing seems to he the one sport in which
the aphorism “youth must be served ” does not apply,
“ Geordie ” Ellis, who was boat-sailing before many of t he
fal hers of the present generation of tiller-handlers were
born, is still top of the tree, and on Saturday, in a spank-
ing north-easter, sailed the Keriki to victory in the first
handicap of Sydney Sailing Club’s season. “ Nick ’’
Johnson put the Effie in the “gutter” when holding
second place on the run home.
*** . *
The fight between Tim Murphy (champion
middle-weight of Victoria) and Bill Jennings, at Golden
Gate Club, Sydney, on Saturday night, was the fiercest
seen there between top-notchers. Both fought like
tigers but withal fairly—throughout. Murphy—clean-
built, long-limbed, and nearly Oft. high —looked the ideal
boxer. Jennings-thick-set, heavy-browed, and mus-
cular—was the I’.R. fighter. A veritable glutton, all
through his career, for punishment, Bill demonstrated
that that capacity was with him to as great an extent as
ever, but he lacked condition - had too much under his
waist-hand. From the jump they were at close quarters
—in-fighting is Jennings' game, and his only - chance
against a long-readied man of Murphy’s stamp. Latter
never evaded the challenge, but stood" up and gave and
took in a manner that made the oldest ring-goers
(there were dozens of them outside the ropes)
hold their breath in amazement. Jennings smothered
mostly ; Murphy swung, hooked and uppercut at
long range, hut dozens of these went round former’s neck
as he ducked-in to avoid. Jennings was cunning; lie
“crowded” his man whenever opportunity allowed, and
forced the whole of his (Jennings’) greater weight upon
him. Third and fourth rounds were a revelation, bull-
pups couldn't have got to it more determinedly, a <1 the
referee had a hot time forcing them asunder. Shoulders,
arms and face of each were smeared with gore. Jennings
bled freely from an old wound"'opened in his forehead
through violent contact- with Tim’s head. Carmine:
flowed copiously - from Murphy’s nose, hung round his
neatly-trimmed moustache, ynd filled lus mouth. Both
men were dead weak in the fourth 3mins., and, for a
while, Jenmngs looked like coming out atop, but Tim
braced up and sent, in several frame-shaking slabs on the
“ mark,” and two or three smashing half-arm hooks on
the ribs th it rattled Bill from neck to heel. Jennings
gradually weakened, and, hands and condition going hack
on him, he had perforce lo erv enough in his corner after
the sixth round, a batlly-hroken-up man.
* * » »
“Old Timer”: Saw Vic. Tim Murphy shape
with 81l Jennings on Saturday night. Though a fighter
through and through, Murphy makes one serious mistake
—t' at guard-arm rarely leases the vicinity of the waist.
He relies wholly upon long reach and gi o I foot-work ; a
man with a left such as Dooley’s, or the one Peter Jackson
used to own, would have it in Tim’s face always. Some
talk of Mick Dunn and Murphy fighting for middleweight
supremacy of the continent'; if they do meet, Mick’s
sinister member-will play a prominent part, hut I am
afraid Tim is too lusty and aggres-ive. The crowd hoo-
hooed Murphy several times for siriking vvhen one of his
arms was held by Jennings, and again when Jennings
clinched and Murphy did not. All this was fair, ami
shows how little the general run of boxing-patrons
know about the rules. One who has both arms free, or
one arm imprisoned bv his opponent, may hit as much as
lie likes ; idea is to prevent a beaten man from clinching
or holding to avoid trouble.
** ■ «
The .Tack Conlon-Miok Dunn battle at Alham-
bra A 0. (Sydney) last Wednesday night for the middle-
weight premiership of N.S. Wales was a big disappoint-
ment. Conlon undoubtedly funked before putting his
hands up, and became worse afterwards as Dunn’s piston-
rod-like left kept jabbing him in the mouth. Both kept
off each other (Dunn extra-careful and Cordon only too
gl ul to be as far away as possible) so much that many
looking on yelled “ fake ” and hooted. There wasn’t the
semblance of crookedness about it ; odds of 7 to 4 were
actually laid on Conlon to a pretty fair tune by his chief
hacker. In round 3 both let the right fly at same time ;
Dunn’s made impact first, and Conlon swaved for several
seconds, hut Dunn, instead of going in to finish the job,
held off, remembering that others had been caught at Ihe
same game—notably Choynski when he fought Fitzsim-
mons in Boston (U.S. A.). Later, Conlon pulled himself
together,and swinging the right promiscuously—a regular
blind-eyer—reached the point. Dunn now rocked and
twice fell in on his adversary to avoid a finisher getting
home. Conlon, apparently thinking it all “kid,"’ kept away
till Dunn recuperated and owned the situation, for Jack's
resistance was very feeble afterwards—he went down half-
a-dozen times in Ihe sixth tsound and was ultimately
counted out without being knocked out. Remembering
Conlon’s great fight with Captain s Flat miner Dan O'Con-
nell and bow he (Conlon) got up after taking a fearful
blow on the back of the neck—which all the spectators
thought had paralysed him—and then won, writer cannot
fathom why he showed the white feather with Dunn,
except it be that latter's four-continent reputation was
too much. Conlon promised well—looked the figluer—
seemed to have the necessary instincts. But he’s prob-
ably out of the game now for good anil aye.

“ What the South of England failed to do at
Hastings,” writes Tiik Bulletin s London correspondent,
“ in the final match of the tour, three Austral ex-Gover-
nors and others effected at the Inns of Court Hotel last
night, and fairly routed the ‘Darlings.’ It was a great
night for Australia, and a topper on a marvellous record
tour. The occasion was an invitation banquet given by
some mysterious admirers, through Frank Blackley, the
hostel’s manager and an ex-ARGCS scribe, and ’twas a great
night. The rufous and rubicund Lord Jerseyspread bin seif
in and out of the chair and over the hall, ilis left leg was
supported by Lord Carrington ‘Smith ' and dear old Gov.
Loch. ’Enniker Eaton, Dr. Coburn, of S.A., and several
‘Sirs’ were in the background. On the ‘off’ Joe Darling
and his men. Jersey is a charming old grotesque, and
he made a capital ‘ wiv-me-’ammer-in-me-’aiid.’ Car-
rington’s speech was astonishingly rattling, and
he dragged in a faithful friend and admirer when
referring to some titles bestowed upon him by the London
press years agone such as ‘ignoramus,’ ‘Balaam,’
‘cheeky whig peer,’ &e.—he said, ‘the attack, in tact.,
was strong enough to be worthy of the Sydney Bulletin.’
How the Mt. Rennie iron has entered his soul, and how
cramped its lodgings must he! Darling made a neat
little speech, and, with the worries of captaincy rele-
gated to his cricket-bag, he is quite a new and proud
man. ‘ Boh’ Cunningham ably engineer'd a programme
of variety in which many oid pals, including Charley
Warner, Ada Reeves, Bert Gilbert, Lempriere Pringle,
and Slade Murray took a hand. The P. of W. and 170
dukes, earls, and other unemployed sent cheap telegrams
of precious good wishes, and the wine-bearers waltzed
quick and lively all the time.
“In numerous ways the tour” (continues our
correspondent) “ has been a necklace of records. In 35
matches Darling lost the toss 21 times. In 15 consecutive
matches he only won it once. No Austral team has ever
suffer'd more from dropsy where ‘catches’ were con-
cerned. At Canterbury 13 were dropped, and Kent
dropped on the victory. Darling, within 5!) of his 2600
runs, breaks all records, and well has he done the smash-
ing. His .167 at Hastings before the curtain fell was a
magnificent, display, after four months’ hard play. He
hit, 28 fotirers, and did not give a possible until 150 was
totalled. It was far and away his finest innings this trip.
Laver’s 60 covered him with glory and pul him sixth in
the averages—a modest, gentlemanly cricketer with ihe
heart of a boy a d the pluck of two men. The ‘deadly-
earnest." Noble has been disturbed by no concatenat ion
of disasters at any time throughout the tour. Silent
and solid, his hypnotised indifference to barrackers
or bowlers has made him a pillar of granite to
his side and an always -to- be - reckoned • with item.
No man ever played the game for his country
more thoroughly than M. A. Noble since King Billy
with a boomerang hit a waddy-head. bowled by King
Jimmy, into Kooyong Creek for 40. The loss of Clem.
Hill's services since June has been most serious, and with
Iredale's illness, Worrall s cronk leg, and Syd. Gregory
not the Syd. of old, has undoubtedly heavily weighted
the team. Grace won the toss in the la-t. match and put
‘ the others ’ in. asking Joe what he’d ’a done. ‘Gone
in,’ said Joe, and it turned out all right. Trumble is per-
haps the marvel of them all. Head of the bowling—l 42
wickets at 18‘43 each and 27'51 with the hat (opening
each innings) is not too had fora veteran bowler. Jones
sadly soliloquises that he will be no good three years
hence, but be never has bowled more fiercely, nor so well,
in bis life before. Trump r a gem, Worrali the chief run-
foreer, and cruelly-unfortunate Charley M'beod are
others of a team on whose like we may never look again.”

An English clip:—“Merman is an equine
oddity. A week before the iace for Liverpool Clip he was
described as a most debilitated specimen of his kind ;
after Goodwood few con’d be found.lo dispute his rank as
one of the finest stayers in England. The cause of his
defeat in the race for Liverpool Cup was, I feel certain,
not. embodied in the horse himself. Bluntly, this means
that he was not. allowed to show his true form, and those
who are inclined to quarrel with the opinion are at
liberty to do so. There are more things in racing than
are dreamt of in our philosophy. Bui it. was not to point,
a moral that I took Merman in hand. The horse is now
well, and he assists to illuminate ihe barrenness of our
own country in the mat ter ot stayers. The horses we
have at. the present, moment that are capable of getting-
two miles are as slow as a South-Eastern express. What
are the reasons? Is our climate against ns? If so, how
can we hope to breed and perpetuate stamina even from
Australian blood ? I am convinced that notin climate hut
in system is the solution of the riddle to be found. The
subject is a valuable one, and it will keep. I shall return
to it anon.”

With the holiday season coming on, and num-
bers of people cither going themselves or sending their
families lo the mountains, the enterprising burglars’ har-
vest lias begun. Provident people, however, are provid-
ing against his visits by storing their valuables -plate,
jevvt lh ry, papers, etc. —at the Sydney Safe Deposit, Ash-
street, (hack of Palings), and business there is proportion-
ately brisk. We would recommend Bulletin, readers to
call and inspect (lie massive vaults, and ascertain terms
for storage. Safes may now be rented upbii reduced
terms, and boxes are received for storage at very moder-
at rates.*

The recent one-mile Australasian championship
was won by VV. Martin (on Dunlop Lyres).
The cure of piles by the new “ Cannalin”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“ Files, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert Sharp.
Copies, with testimonials, sent free to any colony
from the depot, 257 George-street, Sydney.*
The Aust. contingent will be off to the
war directly, and every bold warrior of it is eager
to get to the front. Which is just the position the
London and American Supply Stores, Bourke-st.,
Melb., has already attained. They have pushed
to the front in their business by proving to the
public they can supply durable, stylish goods, in
all lines of gentlemen’s wear, at the lowest priee.s.
Write for catalogue to L. and A. Supply Stores,
Bourke-st., Melb.*
Drake, M.L.A., knocked the wind clean
out of Premier Dickson’s “ patriotic ” offer of
wairiors by pointing out that, the whole thing
was illegal. Queensland troops are sworn for
service in Australia only, and if volunteers do not
step forward Dickson will be in the ludicrous arid
humiliating position of having offered 250 alleged
men who can’t be found, and Chamberlain will be
in the position of having accepted 250 alleged men
v ho don't exist.
The Dickson Ministry trembled severely
on the Transvaal vote. Had Opposition Leader
Dawson’s amendment simply expressed disappro-
val of the Premier's unconstitutional action in
offering troops without the consent of Parliament,
many malcontents would have crossed the floor
and voti d the Government down. The amend-
ment erred through excess—in inviting Parlia-
ment to repudiate Dickson’s accepted offer.
, Gillott's supporters in the Melb. mayoral
fight were confident that McEaeharn would be
elected only on bis own casting-vote, but the
absence of fevers, a councillor claimed by the
Gillotites, led to the kilted Cockney mayor
entering upon his third year of office by 13 votes
to 12. Had he been called upon to elect himself
on the casting-vote, it was McEacharn’s intention
to do so, and then immediately resign. The
sys.em of electing a mayor by a ballot-vote of
aldermen and councillors is a big mistake, and
opens the door to all sorts of crookedness.
The new Bird-Lewis Ministry (Tas.) has to
battle from the start. Lands Minister Muloaliy
is being opposed for Hobart by Norman Cameron,
M.H.A. for Dejoraine, the man who tracked
down ex-Works Minister Miles. The Cameron
resigns his Deloraine seat to fi jhtMulcahy, partly
because he doesn't like the latter, and partly from
pique at not being offered a portfolio in the new
Ministry. As Hobart electors haven’t had time
to forget the plucky Cameron’s services, the battle
may go hard with Mulcahy.
The downfall of the Q. Ministry may be
expected at any moment. Dickson has no control
over his supporters and his dazzling egotism and
arrogance are spreading contempt among his
party. The haughty ex-auctioneer has lost his
head and sees visions of Federal distinction and
titles and other far-off things. A cave with
C. B. Forrest at its head, and encouraged by
Dawson and Drake, will probably do the trick
before many weeks are passed. Dickson has no
influence in office; out of office he is utterly
impotent.
Whenever a demand for increased expen-
diture has been made of late in Q. Public Works
Department, the cry of economy has been effectu-
ally used to block the outlay, lint admiration for
the Dickson Govt.'s prudent thrift in this respect
was largely wasted. It came out in Parliament
the other day that the Transvaal contingent, now
in course of preparation, will cost Queensland for
a six months’ campaign at last <£32,000. This in a
land, too, that has for years been furiously
retrenching its Civil Service and calling upon the
uni vei se to note bow nobly it practises frugality
and self-denial. And the reward of this loyalty
to the Empire? A title next birthday for Jimmy
Dickson, one of the noble pillars.'and buttresses
that prop the Imperial temple of J. Bull. This
birthday bauble business is going to be a costly
thing for the Australian tax-payer, an.! very soon
the only safe policy will be to remorselessly fling
out every politician who gets decorated or who
sho vs the least inclination to move in that
direction.


A COOKERY BOOK FREE.
We have just received a copy of a cookery book
entitled “ Everybody's Cookery ” published by the
liile Bean Manufacturin''’ Coy. This production
is gotten up in a really first class manner, and has
32 pages. The recipes have been prepared by one
of Australia’s first chefs, and the cost of each
dainty dish is the lowest possible, combining
economy with excellence, so that the book is
equally, useful to the poor as well as the rich.
Some thousands have been printed, and the Bile
Bean Manufacturing Company offer to send a, copy
to any address, free of charge, on the condition
that you mention the name of this paper when
writing, enclosing a penny stamp to pay the
postage. Address Bile Bean Manufacturing Coy.,
39 Pitt Street, Sydney. The busy housewife will
find this publication well worth procuring, and
when writing for same, don’t forget to mention
the name of this paper.*
The little township of Beaufort (Vic.) en-
joyed itself last week in celebrating the return of
Thomas Ord, from Ballarat, without a stain on
his character. Also, the local population de-
rived much satisfaction from hooting the police-
man who had been responsible for the other
Hlow’s journey, and taking one thing with
another, The Bulletin reckons that Beaufort
(Vic.) was expressing a lot of public sentiment all
round. Townsman Ord, it seems, was taken to
Ballarat to stand bis trial on a charge of in-
decently assaulting his domestic servant, Mary
Boyd. Mary told her little tale, and the prisoner
stated, on his own behalf, that the story was a
tissue of lies. In between the two principal
witnesses came a constable who gave evi-
dence to show how it happened that he came to
hear of the alleged outrage on Mary, concerning
which poor Mary had “made no complaint to
anyone.” In cross-examination, the constable
admitted that he and Ord had been on bad terms
for two years. Ord had once been guilty of
knocking him up in the small hours of the morn-
ing to bail out a prisoner, and he (X) had
threatened to take his disturber into custody for
breaking his night’s rest. “In consequence of
this accused (Ord) had reported him, and he was
warned by his superior officer to be more circum-
spect.” The Ballarat jury promptly voted Ord
not guilty. Perhaps the Vic. Crown Law Dept,
will now consider the case from a glaring-con-
spiracy standpoint.

490 Bourke-street, Surry Hills, Sydney, Sept. 5, 1899.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir,—Four years ago I was very bad ; in fact, I had to
walk with crutches. The doctors told me that I was suffering from
kidney and liver complaint, and weakness. He gave me some medi-
cine, but it did not do me any good. I saw in the paper about a
Mr. Stranger having been cured by “ Webber’s Vitadatio,” and I
decided to try it. I took four small bottles and one large one ; the
result was that in six weeks I was perfectly cured, and have thrown
away my crutches. After I commenced taking “Webber’s Vitadatio” I
gained Ist. weight in six weeks. From that time to this I have never
taken a dose of medicine, and I am perfectly cured of the old com-
plaint. When I took the medicine four years ago I was residing at
Macquarie-street, Hobart. I give this testimonial of my own free
will, and you are at liberty to publish it if you so desire.
Yours faithfully, W. R. HURBURGH.
Witness —W. B. Thomas, 387 George-street.
Has it never Occurred to you that ‘Vitadatio’
is a Genuine Remedy?
READ THIS TESTIMONIAL and THEN ACT PROMPTLY.
Dugan-street, Kalgoorlie, West Australia, Aug. 23, 1899.
Mr. S. A. PALMER, Agent-General for Webber’s Vitadatio.
Dear Sir, —With pleasure I write to inform you of the good I
have derived from “Webber’s Vitadatio.” For many years I suf-
fered from hydatids, and a little over three years ago I underwent
two successive operations at the Hamilton Hospital, Victoria, the
doctors taking away “five quarts of hydatids.” Shortly after I
became ill again with a pain in my side, and after coming to Wes-
tralia the doctors informed me that I had another hydatid cyst, and
wished to operate on me. I decided to return to Victoria to be
operated on. It was then I saw “Webber’s Vitadatio ’’ad vertised,
and thought I would give it a trial, which I did, with the result that
the lump has entirely gone from my side, and that “I feel a new
man,” being better in health and strength than I have been for many
years. I shall only be too pleased to answer any inquiries made
about my case.
Yours faithfully, WM. J. TEMPLETON.
Witness—M. F. Smith, Kalgoorlie, W.A.

cured and restored.
Statutory
Declaration.
I, THOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 327 Elizabeth-
street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows:
Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.
Whereas I, as a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rass-
mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,
which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COM-
PLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and although
this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
THOMAS MUDIMAN.

NOBODY WILL STEAL THEM.
There are two reasons why the Crown jewels are
never stolen from the Tower of London. They
are in a strong place, well guarded, and are dan-
gerous things to handle when dishonestly come by.
Most losses of valuables are due either to care-
lessness on the part of the owners or custodians,
or to bribery. Perhaps the burglar’s vocation is
under no circumstances an absolutely safe one,
but not infrequently he chances upon a “job”
which is both easy and lucrative. Ido not men-
tion this fact for the unworthy purpose of induc-
ing any of the youth of the land to enter the
profession but solely to throw illustrative light
upon quite another theme. Still, there is a rela-
tion between them, and we shall hit it after we
have talked a bit about the case of Mrs. Mary
Rowlands, who lives in Wales.
The lady says that no longer ago than February
(1898) she had a severe attack of influenza, fol-
lowed by bronchitis. At this point we are con-
cerned to know whether the influenza was in any
sense responsible for the bronchitis, as cause and
effect. If so, why? It is well understood, of
course, in England, where influenza is so com-
mon, that other ailments do follow it; yet pro-
bably, the majority of us have not settled in our
minds the reason for it.
At all events, Mrs. Rowlands had a low, bad
time. The trouble lingered along as, sometimes,
winter does, away down into the period proper for
May blossoms. She could not seem to get the
better of the throat ailment nor master the
lethargy and weakness left her as a legacy by the
influenza. Her condition may be likened unto
that of a ship which has indeed survived a gale
but finds herself stripped of the sails needed to
enable her to take advantage of gentler winds.
The lady’s hope of getting back her lost
strength by taking plenty of solid, nourishing
food was illusory. Every meal of that kind caused
fearful distress in the stomach and acute pain
at the chest. Nature distinctly repudiated the solid
food, and almost as strenuously objected to the
liquids, such as broths, teas, and milk. It looked
as if Mrs. Rowlands must starve at her own table.
Then came on asthmatic symptoms, so bad she
was often obliged to gasp for breath. Between
this combination of complaints she got but little
rest day or night. Soon after arose a threat of a
still more serious disease—a threat happily not
fulfilled. I refer to the cold, clammy sweats that
broke out upon her, pointing to a fatal decline.
The fear was natural, as grim Consumption with
his poisoned dart, often approaches by that same
road.
“I became so reduced and feeble,” the lady
writes, “ that my daughter had to nurse me con-
stantly. I could not get into or out of bed with-
out her help. In the house, or to others, I was
of no use at all. The doctor who attended me
left nothing untried that he knew of; but, in spite
of all he did, I grew worse and worse.
“Where I should have been to-day, whether in
the land of the living or not, who can tell, if a kind
Providence had not intervened to save me ? In
some way my husband heard of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup, and persuaded me to try it. I began
taking it and soon felt better. My appetite re-
turned, and all kinds of food agreed with me.
With plenty of nourishment I gained strength
rapidly. The asthma and the bronchial trouble
vanished and almost before I could realise it, I
found myself in the best of health, and have con-
tinued so to be ever since. For my remarkable
and unexpected recovery I am under obligation to
Mother Seigel’s Syrup ; and so I tell all whom I
know.”—(Signed) Mary Rowlands, Deri, via
Cardiff, August 31, 1898.
One thing Mrs. Rowlands says in her letter
which I have reserved for this place, namely, that
she was a dyspeptic. Her system was weakened
and her blood rendered impure by indigestion.
That is the main point. That is why she was open
to the attacks of influenza, bronchitis, and asthma.
Her body castle being weak, the precious jewel of
health was stolen.
Keep the digestion strong and the blood clean
and pure, by the frequent use of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup and there will be no soil for the germs of
disease to grow in. The system thus guarded,
protects the health as the walls and door of the
Tower hold safe the Crown jewels.

Anaemia, Atony, Dyspepsia.
A VERY DANGEROUS CONDITION
And AN ABSOLUTE RESUSCITATION.
The Case of
MISS IRENE PAYNE,
(•From the “ Nepean Times”)
(by our special.)
Our reporter called on Miss Irene Payne,
of Penrith, N.S. W. That young iady
opened the door herself, and on hearing of
the reason of his visit smilingly welcomed
him into the house, and needed no pressing
to relate the story of her sufferings and her
prompt recovery. She now appears to be in
the possession of redundant health, and of a
bright and vivacious temperament. On the
pressman saying so, she said, “ Yet once
My Constitution Seemed
Shattered.
A long continued illness had quite broken
me down.”
“ How did this occur, Miss Payne ?”
“ I began to suffer from what is scientifi-
cally known as anaemia. My blood got
extremely poor. My appetite and strength
waned away till I gradually lost all my
healthy colour and became of a pale, leaden
complexion, with large dark rings under my
eyes. My food no longer nourished my
system, and I became weak and languid.
Very soon my digestive organs began to give
me trouble, and soon after eating, pain and
a sense of fullness in the chest was a regular
condition with me. As the days went by
I was Less and Less
able to properly digest and assimilate my
food Often enough I suffered from wind,
which slowly formed within, till the pain
caused by its pressure was almost beyond
belief. I always felt too dull and languid
to perform the light household duties which
fell to my share. I always felt too weary
and tired to move. When I stood up, in
a very few minutes I should feel too weary
to remain on my feet, and was only too glad
to
/Throw Myself Down
on a couch, as exhausted as if I had been
doing half-a-day’s hard walking. At night
my rest was always broken and unrefresh-
ing.”
‘ ‘ Surely this was the worst stage ?”
“Oh dear, no! This was only the begin-
ning. Sick headaches now made their
appearance, and these were always followed
by a ' iolent bilious attack. At such times I
eouldn’t approach the table; the mere smell
of cooking would bring on retching. My
eyes ached with an intense pain, and my
eyelids swelled and were badly discoloured.
My head
Felt as Heavy as Lead.
I couldn’t bear to speak or be spoken to,
and I shou d have to lie down in seclusion
till the worst of the attack had somewhat
moderated. I never had much time after
one of these attacks before another came on,
and every succeeding one became more
serious. You don’t know how
I Used to Dread
these attacks.”
“ They must have been weakening ?”
“ They were, indeed. I grew thinner and
thinner every month, and my constitution
became more and more enervated.
Some Very Dangerous
Symptoms
set in. Sometimes I vomited blood nearly
all day. It’s hardly necessary for me to
tell you how weakening this was, or to
What a Perilous Condition
I was then reduced. You could see by my
drawn and pallid face that my vitality was
very low. My lips, which should have been
red, were as pale and leaden coloured as my
cheeks, and if you looked at my gums you
would have found them white also.”
“ Surely you tried some remedy ?”
“ Yes; I was always taking medicine
from the very first. _ I took bottle after
bottle of different things. Tiny were all
the same. They none of them did me any
good.” J
“Ha e no doctors attended you ?”
“ Yes, but unfortunately their medicine
also failed to cure, although they had every
chance, as I always followed out minutely
the dire tions of my medical attendants
I was in Despair
what to do. My stomach was in 3uch
state that it was with the utmost difficult
that I could retain the lightest and mo.-’
easily-digested foods.”
“ But I understand Clements Tonic came
to the rescue ?”
“ Yes ; I had frequently heard of it dur-
ing the later stages of my illness, and it was
recommended to me by a friend; but I was
very loth to take any more medicine, as my
stomach was in such a tender and weak
state that I was
Fearful of Irritating It.
Ultimately, after having read a book of
cases cured by Clements Tonic, I sent for a
bottle.”
“ Did that remedy suit you ?”
“ Most effectively. Unlike some of the
other medicines, which used to make me
feel sick, this Clements Tonic at once had an
invigorating effect. The first instance of
its beneficia 1 qualities was noticeable iu the
fact that it put a stop to
The Vomiting of Blood.
And what a blessing it was to be able to eat
something without being almost certain to
vomit. By using Clements Tonic regularly
and carefully I was able to again take
strengthening foods and was also able to digest
them with great advantage to my health
generally. The bilious attacks, in two or
three weeks were nearly done with, only a
slight turn coming on now and again, and a«
I continued to progress
Steadily towards Health
they troubled me no more. My headaches
were banished, and sweet sleep once more
refreshed me at night time. You could
almost see Clements Tonic filling my veins
with the much-needed red blood. My
cheeks began to fill out and displayed a tinge
of red. My lips and gums became of a
healthy colour, and, once having got rid of
my painful internal troubles, I gained
strength and spirits daily, and I soon
regained the
Full Health and Strength
which I now enjoy.”
“ I ask your permission to put this in the
Nepean Times ?”
“ Of course you can,” replied Miss Payne.

The Origin of Bile Beans.
Bilk Bkans for Biliousness have earned for
themselves by their unequalled excellence, a
reputation which places them to-day far in ad-
vance of any other similar preparation. This
specific is not a carelessly prepared nostrum, on
the contrary, it is the result of long and thought-
ful research on the part of a medical man, an
American, who after years of study discovered a
natural vegetable substance which after further
study lie succeeded in compounding with other
ingredients, so that its effect on the digestive
organism of the human system is identical with
that of the natural bile created by the liver.
Finding this then to be the most natural carthartic
obtainable, the public rapidly accepted it in pre-
ference to all others, and consequently the old
methods of starving and the use of pepsin and
bismuth sank into insignificance and oblivion.
Acting on the bowels in a gentle manner, Bile
Beans do not weaken or in any way distress the
patient. This is a point in which they differ
materially from nearly all laxative remedies sold
to-day, as the latter usually act in a violent
manner,_ weakening the patient, and in many
cases doing more harm than good. Bile Beans have
been aptly termed “ a medicine for the people at a
popular price/’ Many wonder how this remedy can
have the great virtue that we claim for it and
still be sdd at so reasonable a price ; yet the
solution is simple. Millions upon millions of
Bile Beans are consumed annually (a fact that
in itself is of positive proof that the world’s
inhabitants know how to appreciate a really
genuine remedy, and that they have found that
remedy in Bile Beans for Biliousness). Owing to
this immense annual sale we are enabled to pur-
chase the very best drugs in exceedingly large
quantities, and thus get them at a great deal
lower price than if we bought only moderate
quantities at a time. Then again, Bile Beans are
manufactured in possibly the largest and best
equipped laboratories in the world, situated in
Detroit, Michigan, TJ.S. A. In this establishment
only the very best of latest improved machinery is
used, and this fact also greatly minimises the cost
of production. Thus it will been seen that
through the great consumption the proprietors are
enabled to sell this valuable remedy at a price that
places it within reach of all.

LIFE WAS PESPAIREP OF!
Interesting* Story of Mr. Thomas Buckett.
DOCTORS BATTLED, PATENT MEDICINES TAILED.
BILE BEANS step in and again Conquer
A prominent Daily Paper investigated the case, but if you are a sufferer, call and see Mr. Buckett, or
write, and find out for yourself as to the truth of the statements given below.
Investigation is tv fiat we like.
Mr. Thomas Buckett, who resides off More Street, Adelaide (at rear of St. Mary Magdalene’s Church) when interviewed by a
reporter of the Advektiser, said : —“ Between sixteen and seventeen years ago I was taken ill with what a leading doctor in Melbourne
called poor man’s gout. I was then residing at the corner of Clark and Ross Streets, South Melbourne.
The doctors I consulted could not really tell what my complaint was. My knees, ankles, and
toe joints swelled up considerably—at times my knees were as large as three. I suffered great pain and
even the walking of persons across the room increased my agony. I was confined to my bed for six months
at a time, and I used to be afraid to go out fearing a sudden return of the malady. This state of affairs con-
tinued up to iast Christmas. I also suffered with palpitation of the heart and severe indigestion—after eat-
ing it seemed as if there were heavy weights on my chest. I was scarcely ever free from headache, and often
felt ready to do away with myself. I tried all the remedies that were mentioned to me, in addition to
having the services of a number of doctors, but I secured little relief. As the years went by my sufferings
increased. lam well-known in South Melbourne, and Mr. Henry Morgan, of Palmerston Crescent, South
Melbourne, who served me with milk, and Mr. R. Darlington of Street, South Melbourne, commis-
sion merchants and others can bear out my assertions. I came to Adelaide five years ago last February, but
was unable to even earn my own living. I spent all my money in so-called remedies for my complaints, but
at length I could no longer afford to experiment with the nostrums, but had to grin and bear my agonies
hoping—it seemed against hope—that some day I should be cured by something.”
“ And that ‘ something ’ was discovered at last ? ” interposed the reporter. i
“Yes, Bile Beans. Just before Christmas I read in the papers of the wonderful cure of Mrs.
McArney, of Bowden, through taking Bile Beans, and I thought ‘ Well, if they can cure an old lady of 80
summers they can surely cure me,’ so I bought a box at Mr. B. R. Bowyers, grocer, in Angas-street.
After taking the contents I felt much better, so I purchased more, following the directions as near as pos-
sible, and in about a month’s time I was completely cured.”
“ Are you quite sure it was the Bile Beans that made such a wonderful change in you ? ” queried the
reporter.
“ Of this I am positive,” said Buckett in reply, as I have not taken any other medicine at all for some
time before commencing taking the Beans, as I previously mentioned to you. Since taking them, instead of feeling an old man. I feel
quite young again, and life’s a pleasure instead of a dreary burden. The praise of Bile Beans shall always be on my lips, and lam anxious
to let the public know of my cure so that other sufferers will try the Beans.”
The reporter, on taking his leave of Mr. Buckett, could not but marvel to himself that this, man after suffering so many years and
trying so many things, should be cured by these small objects, and he came to the conclusion that if a miracle had not been wrought a
great deal of suffering had been saved, and if the world at large only knew of this great preparation we should have much healthier and
happier men and women.
BILE BEANS
are an Undoubted Specific for Biliousness, Sick and Nervous Headache, Indigestion, Constipation, Piles, Female Weakness, Pale-Faced
Girls, Irregularities, Bad Breath, Pimples, Blotches, Dizziness, all Liver and Kidney Troubles, Dyspepsia, Influenza, Colds, Heart
Palpitation, Pain in Back and Side, Fulness after Eating, Lack of Physical Tone, Heartburn, and that Tired Feeling. At the price Bile
Beans are sold at they come within the reach of all, and as 30 million doses were taken, in Australia last year it is easily seen that their
worth is appreciated.

The Two Mortgages on the
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Perhaps it was the desire for a change of any
kind from the dreary monotony of the cow-yard
and the dairy that induced Mary Bell to marry
Jim Sawner.
Mary’s parents were hard-working, hard-living,
God-fearing, eminently respectable people, in
whose eyes a dance-room was a den of iniquity.
And poor Mary thought she had reached the
depths of humiliation when one night her father
dragged her out of a dance-room, amidst the ill-
concealed sneers of the female portion of the
assemblage; for Mary was much too good-looking
to excite any pity from her own sex, and they
prophesied no good would come from a brazen
lump, only sixteen years old, who would steal
through her bedroom window to attend a dance.
But they were very lenient with Dick Somers,
who helped her through, and sympathised greatly
with him, for the very obvious reason that Mary
Bell was losing all maidenly reserve in setting
her cap at him. Dick Somers was cur enough to
give a smiling, silent assent to their strictures on
Mary. And when Mary’s dearest friends in-
formed Mary of this fact, her humiliation was a
shade deeper than before. But she hadn’t touched
bottom yet.
Four more years of farm-life under the old
conditions of labor, and ten times the old condi-
tions of watchfulness ; and Mary was ripe for any
change. A few amorous glances from Jim
Sawner brought the change. Jim had the mis-
fortune to be an only son. It was also his parents’
misfortune. He was pampered and petted as a
boy, but, to do him justice, he never wasted any
such follies on his parents. When he reached
manhood he speedily made a pretty good breach
in his father’s capital; and the old man, as a last
resort, bought him a farm, stocked it for him, and
declined to have any more dealings with him,
financially or otherwise.
As a result of this harsh treatment, Jim
promptly mortgaged the farm, bought a good
horse, and married Mary Bell, whose parents
disowned her and acquired a worse opinion of
dancing than ever. After a week of married
quietness Jim developed a raging thirst, and
finished up by nearly finishing off Mary with a
bottle.
Then began a struggle on Mary’s part to pro-
vide funds for Jim. Every device it was possible
for an honest woman to practise to raise sixpence
out of she resorted to. Few women were as
capable of managing a farm as Mary was, but,
with a heavy heart, week after week she would see
Jim drive one or more of the best cows away to
sell to get a few pounds for the support of the
brewing industry. Twelve months after his mar-
riage Jim gathered together his last realisable
assets, a few miserable “ warrigals,” and started
driving them to Woronga, where a horse-sale
was being held. Mary gave him a pound (a
present from his mother to buy some finery for
baby), so that the poor fellow would not be short
of cash on the road.
Jim was mounted on a good horse, whose ears
had a habit of always lying back, and whose eyes
never seemed to lose sight of Jim. Jim was a
good horseman, and, drunk or sober, it was no
easy matter to unseat him. Also, he was full of
weird devioes in the way of cruelty, to make his
hack show his paces.
After a few beers at sundry pubs along the
road, he reaohed Paddy Farer’s hotel. A few
children, mounted on thin willow sticks, named
after renowned racehorses, racing down the
road, passed the hotel. One little fellow, about
two years old, .mounted on Carbine, occupied such
a hopeless position in the rear that not even the
V.R.C. stipendiary steward would have taken
any exception to his pulling-up. He did pull-up,
and turned his attention to the real live horse tied
up to the fence.
After a few more beers, Jim, who was now
about drunk, said he must be getting along.
“What do you think of my horses?” he asked
Patrick, the landlord. The landlord did not
think much of them, and opined that they
were not likely to create any spirited competition.
“Well, come out and have a look at my hack. I
am going to sell him. Too good for me to keep.
Too treacherous. Kick the eye out of a mosquito.
Want you to say how much he’s worth. You’re a
bit of a judge.” They went outside and the sight
that met their eyes sobered Jim and paralysed the
landlord.
Seated on the ground, stroking the hind legs of
Jim’s horse and calling him all the endearing
names his infantile imagination could conjure,
was the two-year-old child of the landlord. More-
over, the horse seemed to like it, as with ears
cocked forward he looked every way except at the
infant. That was a good sign. When Jim’s
horse looked at anybody he meant mischief. The
landlady came to the door, wondering where the
children were.
“I seen them just now down the road,” said
Jim, in a thick, terrified voice, and the mother
returned to the kitchen.
Jim quietly walked up to the horse whose ears
gradually settled back to their usual position, and
led him away from the child, who remonstrated
with him for his unkindness and filled the air with
howls.
Paddy Farer offered to buy the horse. But the
sale was off. “No ; money won’t buy him now,”
said Jim. “I won’t sell him.”
He never did. Two hours later, muddled
with sundry more drinks, he reached the sale-
yards and was about to dismount. He stooped
over his horse’s neck to caress him with his hand.
The horse, no doubt suspecting some new form of
devilry, started to buck, and unseated Jim, whose
foot caught in the stirrup—and a few well-directed
kicks lifted one of the mortgages off the farm !
Paddy Farer never bought the horse, although
he was sold a few weeks later ; but he lifted the
other mortgage off the farm by way of expressing
his gratitude.
YOKEL.
Dr. Ramsay Smith, the new Adelaide city
Coroner and Health President (he was one of the
seniors imported for the hospital) has, for a man
still under 40, seen and done much. In his
University student days he had to keep himself
going by teaching ; and at different times he had
pupils for arts, science, medicine, and music.
In his 13 years at Edinburgh University he was
successively student, graduate, lecturer, examiner,
assistant professor and acting professor ; and on
top of this he took to writing articles and reviews
for the Scotsman.
On Tramps.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Thebe was a strange tramp here the other day,
and as we were down in the paddock he had some
difficulty in making his presence known. He
looked dreadfully “hurt,” wearing a more in-
jured air than was usual to the persuasion, and
that is saying a good deal.
“I thought you was all hidin’,” he said, and
then explained that the late Gatton murders had
done a fearful lot of harm to the reputation of
tramps in general—in fact, almost as much as
they had done to the reputation of the Queens-
land police. He was quite pathetic about it, and
every remark he made found its mark, for, truth
to say, on account of the scare we two women had
not been “ at home ” to sundowners unless they
caught us unawares. We lived in a corner house
in Bordertown, and had weakly given bread and
meat to a tramp or two. After that we had them
on an average of four or five a week, and had
good opportunity of studying their idiosyncrasies.
Tramps are renowned for their sarcasm. It is
one of their most effective weapons when dealing
with defenceless females. They stand on their
dignity a great deal, too. That dignity is not
always apparent, but it is innate. One man I
especially remember. He was small, clad in rags,
awfully dirty, and had only one eye, and a purple
nose ; yet his dignity and power of making others
feel of no account were remarkable. He came at
sunset, and holding out a greasy billy demanded
to have it boiled on our fire. The stove was
being cleaned, and we hadn’t a tire. Later on, a
tiny spirit-lamp would suffice for our wants,
besides we were going to make a stand against
the entertaining of these gentlemen of the road.
“No fire ! My Gord ! ” That was all he said,
but the profound scorn of it was indescribable. I
felt myself color to the roots of my hair. If he
had added, “ That of all the mean, poverty-
stricken households,” etc., etc., I couldn’t have
felt it more. “ But there’s plenty of water in the
tank,” I said, apologetically, and he condescended
to take some.
Next day he was back again, and “supposed
we had a scrap of sugar in the house to give
away.” He was given a tin-full, and then said
he didn’t know what people wanted to keep
“ dorgs ” like that for, and for the first time our
hound’s sulky aspect and plebeian origin was
brought home to us. I don’t know why he
objected to the dog so. After an unknown and
presumably harried past it had attached
itself to our family, and, for some reason
or other, connected probably with that past, was
very partial to tramps; indeed, they were the
only visitors it was inclined to let near the house
at all. Respectably-dressed persons were always
snarled at, and kept at bay until one of us rushed
to the rescue with a broomstick. However,
“ Rusty ” was included in the general scorn shown
for all our belongings by this particular tramp.
He camped on the reserve for a week, and de-
manded something every day, but he finally shook
the dust of our home from his feet for ever in a
white heat, because we gave him jam instead of
butter for his bread on the journey.
Then there was the revengeful tramp, in the
shape of a benevolent white-headed and sleek old
man—who called down curses on our heads, and
on the heads of our children’s children because we
didn’t want him to sleep in our tool-house, but
presented him with a shilling to get a bed down
town. He preferred to keep tne shilling for
drink, and hoped that at his age we shouldn’t
have a roof to cover us, or a crust to eat. His
cursing was horribly convincing. My sister is
superstitious, and looks forward to an old age of
want now as a certainty. Everyone knows the
tramp with a trade. I remember one who cleaned
and sharpened knives. He stayed all day, ate
three good meals, and made great play with a
whet-stone and some oil. He also drank a. good
deal of tea between meals, demanded 25., and
went his way. He was really sorry that our
knives were such “ rotten bad steel.” I suppose
he was right, because, though slightly brighter,
the knives operated upon were certainly no
sharper. Of all the tramps I have met I know
only one who was really pathetic, though manv
pose in that rdle.
In the days when we lived a station-life, and
often took the long journey across the intermin-
able Moree Plains, we met him toiling across a
vast swamped tract of country, through which our
four strong horses were laboriously dragging the
buggy at the rate of 8 miles an hour. The Moree
Plain is of blacksoil, and there had been a three-
weeks’ rain, which is descriptive enough to an
Australian with average imagination. This
tramp was jumping from grass-tussock to grass-
tussock, wading in between whiles just ahead of
us. He looked for all the world as if he had just
stepped out of Queen-street, Brisbane, where
hundreds of city clerks of his stamp can be
seen any day—hard-hitter hat with respectably
curved brim, cut-away coat, and light trousers,
little tan-leather hand-bag, and tan boots, the
latter, of course, darkened by mud and water.
At closer quarters one could see that the boots
were in holes, and the soles on the point of dis-
solution, the hat and coat a very green- black, and
the light-grey trousers stained with grease. He
had an imposing iron-grey beard, and when we
offered him a lift he raised his hat and accepted
most politely. For two hours we toiled through
the water, and elicited frbm the passenger in the
back-seat that he had been head-shopwalker in a
well-known drapery house in Brisbane. Unfor-
seen circumstances had necessitated his leaving
and taking this journey, but remittances awaited
him in Moree. When he got them he was going
back to Brisbane and his old billet, which awaited
his pleasure patiently. He took off his hat with a
graceful flourish, and thanked us quietly when we
left him the other side of the swamp to pursue his
way to Moree, while we diverged to a station for
the night. Somehow or other a real sundowner
in sacking trousers, carrying bluey and useful
billy, would not have looked so hopelessly
poverty-stricken as the “ shop gent,” with his tiny
lunch-bag and frayed, dingy white shirt-cuffs, on
the wallaby in the midst of the unfriendly, tree-
less plains.
ETHEL MILLS.
Melb. recently produced a man with a
sense beyond his time of the duties of citizenship.
He had resolved upon suicide, and with an eye to
saving an overburdened State unnecessary ex-
pense, walked to the morgue and disposed of him-
self so that his corpse was the first thing to strike
the eye of the official who opened the gates in the
morning.


John Dancey, of Lara, Vic., started out
towards Geelong the other morning with the in-
tention of getting married. Changed his mind,
returned in a meditative mood, and thoughtfully
hanged himself. John was 75 ; the fiancee was a
widow and much younger, Second thoughts are
best.
Del Sorte, the hotel “barber” who
has just got some years’ hard for robbery in a
Melb. coffee-palace, has a speciality for
opening locked doors with a master-key
and hiding under the bed until the lodger goes
to sleep, when the coast is clear for robbery.
One time he and his mate, Pierre Douar, were
following their vocation in a temperance hotel
when detective M'Manamney caught one of the
pair, but somehow missed Del Sorte, although he
was quite sure he was not far off. The residents
were cautioned to look under their beds that night,
which they all did except a mining manager, who
ridiculed the idea, as his room was locked and he
knew it was all right. He knew next morning
that the “specialist ” just happened to be thei’e,
and had cleared off with enough loot to keep him
in clover for three months. .
Captain Russell, discoursing to Maoriland
House of Reps, on Purity of Administration, is a
spectacle for the gods to shriek at. Ten members
of Council retire shortly by effluxion of time, and
the Seddon Government, as a Government with a
working majority, naturally proposes to fill up the
vacancies. And good old Russell, in the name of
Purity of Administration, demanded that the ap-
pointments should be hung up till after next elec-
tion, to see if the Government still had a majority ;
he held that it was a sin against the Constitoosh’n
for a Ministry even with a majority to make
Council appointments just before an election.
Russell was a member of the last Atkinson Minis-
try which, at the beginning of ’9l, made a large
batch of Council appointments after an election —
and that, too, an election at which it had been
utterly defeated, and when, it was quite sure to
be thrown out as soon as Parliament met. On his
own exposition of political purity, therefore,
Russell had a hand in one of the dirtiest acts in
Maoriland politics, and, remembering this, the
House rejected the impudent old bluffer’s censure
motion by 33 to 25.

The Dancer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The house ia darkened, and the air Is thiols
With heavy odours of the musio-hall
Here in the crowded Oirole, eloquent
Of perfumed flesh voluptuously warm
In open bodices ; with this, faint scents
Of women’s hair, and tawdrier fragranoes
Incisively persistent. Here and there
A naked shoulder gleams across the gloom
Whitely.
Up in the teeming Gallery
Bill and his garish girl, exuberant,
Spice talk with hot, ungentle repartee,
And souffle for positions next the rail.
Down in the Pit, Respectability
Talks politics, and makes the best of things—
The orchestra preluding. In the Stalls,
Smug city-men exchange their dingy thoughts
And snigger anecdotage, mostly lies,
About the awaited lady.
Then the stage
Starts into vision as a pool of light,
With Nita smiling roseate in the centre,
Rouged, pencilled, undemurely confident—
The moment’s tyrant, wholly conscious of
Supremacy, she scorns the gods’ applause.
And so she stands a moment’s space at poise,
Immobile, almost listless, though her eyes
Are full of life. It seems as though she were
Half soothed and half intoxicated by
The greenish sweetness of the violins . . .
And then she dances . . .
Languorously at first
As though the motion bored her. First she lifts
One little foot (the modern dancer’s foot,
Scarce beautiful, the only charm she shows
That is not perfect in its shapeliness)—
Lifts one small foot and flings it far above
Her shoulder, while those mortals in the stalls
Divine the shrouded splendour of her hips,
And lay up thoughts for future use. And next
She bends her supple body forward, like
A wind-blown yarran, while her fearless eyes
Flash challenges the gleeful gods translate,
And her bare breasts protuberant take their part
In the so-insolent triumph of her pose.
And now the rhythm assails her, and she flings
Into a maze of motion that suggests
Romano's Graces frenzied with champagne.
She moves in purple worlds of art and sense,
Making the best of both ; and all the while
Bedevils men with her tormenting eyes,
And wins from brazenly decollete dames
(White- breasts abulge in open bodices),
Tjp in the circle, comment critical.
Murmurs of “Wanton hussy ! ’’ and the like.
And in the teeming gallery up above
Bill and his garish girl approve the dance,
Leaning wide-eyed and warm across the rail.
And in the stalls the city-men applaud ;
And in the Pit. Respectability
Is somehow glad he left the wife at home,
But claps and shouts his bravas with the rest,
As Nita, panting, smiles and slips away.

FRANK MORTON.
A Corner Man
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hfl Is a seller of ico-oream—a peasant from some
sub-Appenine village, come to Australia to make
his fortune. That was five years ago, and still
you see him with his little hand-oart at the corner
of the hot, dusty Adelaide street, leaning in the
shadow of the thin calico awning, awaiting cus-
tomers. All through the glaring summer days he
stands blinking there, or sits upon the handles of
his cart, which he calls his “ dear carretella ” or
his ** accursed carretella ” —according to his mood.
There he keeps guard over his little pots of vanilla
jelly, and dispenses dainty tumblers of pink ice-
orenm with big, coarse, brown hands.
With his stubbly cheeks and bushy eyebrows
and forehead puckered under his fad'd hat, he
waits and waits and thinks ; and of un evening he
wheels his cart away and disappears in some dirty
alley of the West End. No talking to anyone
except the business “ Seex-pinny,” “ Tree-pinny,”
“Tank yo,” “No, sare”—for he “no spikk
Inglees.”
Thus, in his enforced solitude, he dreams like a
lizard all day long in the sun and dust. Some-
times he looks like a brigand out of one of Ribera’s
pictures ; sometimes like a tavern sketch of Leo-
nardo da Vinci; and anon like Faun or Satyr
from a Pompeian vase.
There was a Mediterranean air about him that
attracted me, and one day I made a closer
acquaintance with this tawny fellow. With my
head leaning on my hands beneath the shadow of
his awning, I listened to his ready chatter. He
spoke the rich graphic dialect of the Basilicata
with rustic grace and pathos, and with that deep
throaty intonation which brings back to the tra-
veller the remembrance of many an Italian village
and landscape. I half understood the suffering of
this rough spirit, pining for his native land, and
without energy or hope enough to return to it.
What grimaces and shrugs and twitchings of
the brows, as he spoke, rudely symbolising Des-
tiny—Fate—-Resignation—Helplessness in the
hands of the gods. And what a voice! —with
inflections and tones as subtle and expressive as
the pleading of a child.
He should return never more to his kin and
native land—never-never. He was a prisoner
bound with unseen manacles to this ice-cream
cart, and mutely and bitterly hating it, and the
glasses he polished, and the people whose pennies
kept him. It was one of those petty, bitter
tragedies of vulgar life which the practical man
passes by with a smile.
This man’s talk was of common things enough.
Madonna ! how bare an existence did this ice-
eream'selling yield. Yet there was nothing else
to do. tSanto Christo ! For a while had he not
played the flute in a street trio, and had he not
been forced to give that up and pawn his instru-
ment. At present he was earning more than he
could hope to do in Italy, yet he was but ill-con-
tent. Food and manners were so different here.
Here with sixpence you buy, say, a paltry drink
of tea and a plate of meat with potatoes, and
perhaps a oouple of tomatoes if God wills it,
Dio! what a meal for a Christian! But with
that same sixpence in a trattoria of Naples what
a banquet you could have, fit for the Pope him-
self ! What a pile of steaming maoeharom, “ soft
and long as seaweed or the tresses of a woman’s
hair, signor; with red juioe of pomid’oro poured
on it, and a good deoanter of red Posillipo wine,
and a inandolin-player to sing and make you
laugh while you eat 1 Ah I che-e-e piacer 1 signor,
che piacer / ” drawling out the long Italian “ e’s ”
as if he were sipping wine.
“The English—they do not love singing or
mirth, and in the farms and vineyards all is dif-
ferent from Italy. And the English workmen
laugh and torment you because you cannot speak
their language.” How could he help it that he
could not learn English ? Our Lord Christ willed
it not that any tongue but Italian should suit his
lips, and therewith —basta /
“ Madonna ! what a misfortune to leave one’s
native land ! You have heard them, how they
sing there in the Terra di Lavoro, in the Marches,
and in the Val d’Arno. Two or three bare-legged
peasants hoeing between the vines from dawn to
dark sing to each other all day long, or the poor
women that cut the clover and lucerne—you have
heard them, signor? And in the month of June,
when all the village girls are down in the Cam-
pagna gathering the green, cool peas, Santa
Maria ! how they sing, and sit at mid-day be-
neath the almond-trees and sing again. Sempre
Vallegria , in spite of poverty. And in the even-
ing mayhap the village priest goes down among
them, and they sing the holy song together in the
fields and the sound comes up to the villages in
the hills as sweetly as the sound of mass ; and all
the young men who are, as young men will be, in
love, try to pick out their sweethearts’ voices.
“ My wife and my little daughter Carminella
used to do that. But she is dead and Carminella
has a husband, and I, ah ! I shall never return
any more to see them. No, no, I shall never earn
enough to be able to return, and here I am, in
exile— proprio esiliato —for my life. Ah ! the
misfortune to have to die in a strange land !
“ All round my village were vineyards and olive
gardens and fig-trees, but many taxes were put
upon us by the government, which has no pity for
the poor folk who cannot live. And there were
chestnuts in the woods for winter that Christ has
put there for poor people, and many nightingales
in spring and summer. You have heard it your-
self in the boscage in Italy, signor, have you not ?
Ah, ’tis a tiny creature with a heart full of passion
of music—and a most tender flesh to eat when
it is roasted ! Oh, yes, they are trapped and
eaten. You know they come back to Italy to
m ike their nests in the spring time, and then is the
season that they sing all night long, and all the
orchards are full of perfume, and the vines grow
so fast that it is a marvel. Once a nightingale
had taken his place on a trellis, and sang all night
to his mate close by, I suppose, and forgot to
move, for very love of singing. So that in the
morning when the light came and he wanted to
fly away, he found that one of the tendrils of the
vine had.entwined itself firmly round his foot and
bound him prisoner. And there I found him
when I passed into the vineyard—the poor little
creature ! —and I took him home for my wife to
roast. That will show you how fast the vines
grow in Italy, signor. Ah, what a life there in
the Cainpagna among the vines and figtrees ! But
eoco un fanciullo —' boy,’ you call. Want iee-a
erem-a ? One pinny—tree pinny? Yis. Eccolol ”
Always, since, we are nodding acquaintances
and have many a chat about the weather
and the ice-cream selling, and all the little things
that go to make the weft of human life.
But I believe that in his heart he still has a
vague hope of seeing Italy again, and Carminella.
